# The Naming

# Chapter 1

The rain found the seams in Suna's shawl before they were halfway up the switchback. Kael walked ahead, his sandals slapping the stone steps with a deliberateness she recognized as bravery — eight years old, performing steadiness for his sister's benefit. Behind her, Deshi climbed with one hand on Peyam's elbow, breathing audibly between the gusts, and Suna could hear the older woman's complaints gathering like weather of their own: the mud, the steepness, the weight of the bags they'd pulled from the truck before abandoning it where the water covered the wheels.

Asha rode on Suna's hip. Four years old and too heavy for this, but the child had stopped walking twenty minutes ago, and there was no argument Suna could win against a four-year-old's exhaustion that wouldn't cost more time than carrying her. Asha's fingers found the wet collar of Suna's blouse and rubbed the fabric between thumb and forefinger, the continuous investigative motion that was her default relationship with the physical world. She touched everything — buttons, bark, the grain of wooden spoons, the hems of strangers' clothing when Suna wasn't quick enough to redirect her hands. Now she was touching the rain, palm open to it, collecting what she could in the cup of her hand and licking her fingers.

"Rimi," Suna said. "Stay with Kael."

"I'm with Kael."

"Where I can see you."

Rimi adjusted her position one step closer to her brother without further comment. She was twelve, angular, already showing her father's long face, and Suna would have been grateful for the obedience except that obedience from Rimi always meant the girl was too frightened to argue.

The shrine appeared through the rain as they came around the last bend — stone walls the color of wet iron, a covered walkway extending from the entrance, and behind it the larger shapes of the compound on the hilltop. There were people under the overhang already, too many to be the shrine's own. Suna counted: a knot of bodies near the entrance, bags and bundles piled against the wall, a child's voice bright and complaining above the rain. Other families had arrived first.

Peyam said something to Deshi that Suna didn't catch. Deshi's response carried: "Of course there are others. Where else would they go?"

Where else. Below them, where the seven roads should have converged at the valley floor, there was only water. Brown, silt-heavy, moving with a weight that made Suna think not of rivers but of something being poured — the valley filling like a basin. She could see the roof of a truck at the waterline, just the ridged metal and a side mirror catching what remained of the afternoon light, and past it the dark shape of a road sign half-submerged. The water had no edges she could find. It extended into the treeline on both sides and vanished around the far bend where the northern road should have been, and the sound of it climbed the hill in a continuous, pressured rush that sat beneath the rain like a second weather, lower and steadier.

"Mama," Asha said, and pressed her wet palm flat against Suna's cheek. The gesture was neither kiss nor comfort — it was Asha's way of making contact, of confirming that the surface she was touching was real and warm and responsive.

"I know," Suna said. "We're almost there."

A man stood at the entrance to the covered walkway. He was lean, mid-sixties, with the unhurried posture of someone who had been standing in that position for some time and would continue standing in it for as long as the situation required. He watched them approach without coming forward to help, without calling out. When they reached the overhang and the rain stopped hitting them, the relief was almost physical — the world narrowing from open hillside to the close stone smell of the shrine and the press of other bodies underneath the roof.

"How many in your family?" the man said.

Suna set Asha down. Her back sang with the release. "Six. Three children."

"Names."

She gave them. Mera. Suna. Rimi. Kael. Asha. Deshi, who was her mother-in-law. Peyam, who was Deshi's brother. The man listened without writing anything, his eyes moving from face to face as she spoke each name, confirming what he saw.

"Your destination, before the road closed."

"Gelden," Suna said. "My husband went ahead. We're meeting him." The lie came out the way it always did, with the flatness of something rehearsed past the point of effort. She had said it so many times that it occupied the space where the truth should have been, smooth and without edges.

The man nodded. He did not ask about her husband, did not note the absence. "I am Olet. I am the head custodian of this shrine. You will be given a place to sleep and food. There are rules. You will hear them when everyone has arrived."

"How many are here?" Suna asked.

"You make seven families."

Seven. Suna looked past Olet into the covered walkway and through to the main hall beyond. The hall was stone — floor, walls, and a vaulted ceiling blackened by decades of lamp smoke. It was large enough to hold everyone, but the largeness was the compressed kind, a space that could fit the bodies but not the distances they wanted between them. On the far side, a wide arch opened onto the valley, and through it the flood was visible in full: the brown water filling the valley floor, smooth and unhurried, the submerged roads marked only by the crowns of trees that lined them. The sound of the water carried through the arch with a low, steady insistence that would, Suna realized, be constant. It would not stop when the rain stopped. It would be there at night when the voices quieted. It was the sound of the thing that was keeping them here.

Oil lamps burned along the walls, already lit against the failing afternoon. A woman with angular features and roughened hands was placing them in iron brackets, moving through the hall with the efficient, unself-conscious rhythm of someone performing a task she had performed a thousand times. She glanced at Suna as she passed — a brief, assessing look, not unfriendly, not welcoming — and continued to the next bracket.

People sat on the stone floor or stood along the walls, their belongings in wet heaps around them. Suna began to count, not the total — Olet had said seven families — but the composition. How many children, how many elderly, how many men. It was what she always did in a new room, the inventory that told her where she stood.

The largest group occupied the far end of the hall. At least ten people, maybe twelve, their bags and bundles claiming territory in the way of a family accustomed to taking up space. A man built like a wall — broad across the shoulders, thick-necked, his gray-streaked hair wet against his scalp — stood at their center speaking to a woman who was folding a length of embroidered cloth with the practiced motions of someone rescuing something valuable from the rain. Wedding cloth. Suna recognized the pattern, the heavy border stitching. This family had been going somewhere specific and festive, and the flood had intercepted them. Beside the big man, an elderly woman sat on a bundle with her hand against her left ear, rubbing it slowly in a circular motion — the absentminded, continuous gesture of a lifelong habit. A young man in his late twenties stood near the elderly woman, holding a bag in each hand, his face less set than his father's, more open to the room.

Near the entrance, where the stone floor was wettest, a young man sat with his back against the wall and a child asleep across his lap. He was thin, younger than Suna, with the dark half-moons of exhaustion beneath his eyes and a posture that made his body a kind of shelter around the sleeping girl. He had set his single bag between his feet and his hand rested on the child's back, rising and falling with her breathing. He was looking at the floor between his knees, his jaw held at an angle that suggested he was working to keep his mouth shut, and Suna felt the quick, involuntary arithmetic of another parent's assessment: one adult, one child, no partner visible, the girl maybe six. She looked away before he caught her looking.

"Suna." Deshi had found a dry section of floor near the wall and was lowering herself with one hand braced against the stone. Her wet sari hem dragged behind her, and she looked at it with the same expression she used for a pan left too long on the fire — a judgment so habitual it required no words. Peyam sat beside her, slowly, his knees giving their report. He was seventy-two and walked with the careful economy of a man who knew his body's budget and allocated accordingly. He reached into his shirt pocket and drew out a string of wooden prayer beads, running his thumb across the first bead as though checking it was still there after the climb.

"Rimi, Kael, come sit," Suna said. "Asha, don't — Asha."

Asha had already crossed three meters of wet stone floor and was reaching for the embroidered wedding cloth that the woman in the large family was folding. Her small hand extended toward the heavy border stitching with the unerring instinct of a child drawn to the most expensive fabric in any room.

"Asha." Suna crossed the distance and caught her daughter's wrist. "That's not ours."

The woman looked up with an expression that was more weary than annoyed. She had the face of someone managing twelve people through a disaster and who had already stopped sorting which annoyances came from inside her family and which from outside. "She's fine," the woman said, but moved the cloth to a higher bundle.

"Sorry," Suna said, and carried Asha back to their section of wall. Asha went without fuss. Her fingers had already found the next thing — the rough texture of the stone wall behind their bags, which she stroked with the flat of her hand, her face concentrated, absorptive, as though the wall were telling her something through her palm.

A tall man in glasses arrived carrying a baby against his chest in a cloth sling, his free hand on the shoulder of a teenage girl whose braids dripped water onto the stone floor. The girl was doing something with her face that she probably thought was indifference — a slack-jawed, half-lidded look — but the way she pressed against her father's side said otherwise. Behind them came a woman, shorter, rounder, holding the hand of a ten-year-old boy who walked close enough to be her shadow. The man said something to Olet that Suna didn't hear, but his voice had the modulated authority of someone accustomed to being listened to, the careful emphasis of a teacher or a speaker.

Two sisters — they had to be sisters, the same broad foreheads, the same way of carrying their weight — came up the walkway with three children between them. The younger sister's face gave away nothing, a blankness so complete that Suna recognized it as something chosen. The older one positioned herself slightly forward, a hand on each of her two children's shoulders, steering them toward the hall with the body language of a woman accustomed to occupying the space between her family and whatever came next.

The last family arrived slowly, because the oldest member could not be hurried. She was small, white-haired, perhaps seventy, and she climbed the final steps with a hand on her son's arm and a deliberateness that was not frailty but precision — each foot placed where she wanted it before she committed her weight. A teenage boy hovered behind her, tall for his age, dark-haired, watching the old woman's progress with a readiness that said he would catch her if she stumbled, though she showed no sign of stumbling. Behind them a woman in her forties and a girl of about eight, the girl looking back down the switchback at the water below with the frank amazement of a child who had not yet learned to be afraid of large things.

By the time the last family was under the overhang, the main hall held forty-three people and the particular density of sound that many bodies produce in a stone room — children's voices, adult murmuring, the rustle and thump of bags being moved, the thin persistent cry of the baby in the sling threading through the rest. And beneath it all, channeled up through the open arch, the flood. Its steady, unhurried rush.

***

Olet waited until it was clear no one else was coming up the road. Then he stood at the center of the main hall and spoke.

He did not raise his voice. He did not wait for quiet. He spoke into the noise of forty-three damp, uncomfortable, frightened people and the noise lowered itself around him, not silenced but diminished, the way weather sometimes abates without anyone deciding it should.

"This is a transit shrine," he said. "It sits at the meeting of seven roads and has served travelers for longer than records count. You are welcome here. You will be fed from our stores. You will be given places to sleep. In return, you will follow the shrine's schedule. Meals are communal. Labor is shared. There is a daily gathering, which the custodians lead. You will be told more tomorrow."

"How long?" the man in glasses — Bram, Suna would learn — asked. "How long until the road's passable?"

Olet turned to him without hurrying. "The road opens when the water drops. The water drops when the rain stops. The rain will stop when it stops."

A sound from the large family's end of the hall — not quite a laugh, more the exhale of a man who had expected precisely this kind of answer and was too tired to be angry about it. Venn Kondo stood with his arms folded across his broad chest, his wet wedding shirt clinging to his shoulders, and Suna could see the effort he was making not to speak first. He was accustomed to speaking first. His mouth worked briefly, as though testing and then discarding a sentence.

"Now," Olet said. "Sleeping quarters."

He had a system. Suna could see it in the way he moved through the hall — not consulting anyone, not asking for preferences, simply pointing to sections of the floor and the adjacent stone rooms and assigning families to them with the institutional certainty of a man distributing rations. This is your space. That is theirs.

"The Kondo family. The large room on the south side."

Venn moved immediately, but paused at the doorway and turned back. "My family is twelve," he said. "My aunt is seventy-three. There are children who need—"

"It is the largest room," Olet said.

"I can see what it is. I'm asking whether there's—"

"It is the largest room available, and it has been assigned to the largest family." Olet's voice did not change in pitch or pace. He was not arguing with Venn Kondo. He was not acknowledging that an argument was taking place. He was stating a fact about a room, and the fact was finished.

Venn held the doorway for a beat. Suna watched his shoulders shift — the physical recalculation of a man who was used to negotiating from strength and had just discovered that the other party was not negotiating at all. Then Tila, his wife, said something low from behind him, her hand on his back, and Venn turned into the room. The young man — their son — held the door for the elderly aunt, who passed through and touched his forearm as she went, a gesture of thanks or habit or both.

"The Okoye family. The northeast corner of the main hall."

Bram's hands moved — a small, unconscious rotation at the wrists that Suna would come to recognize as his thinking gesture, the motion his body made when an argument was assembling itself. His wife adjusted the baby in her arms and looked at the corner Olet had indicated. It was open to the hall on two sides, semi-private at best. Bram said nothing. He took his son's shoulder and steered the family toward the corner, the teenage girl trailing after them with her jaw set in an expression she must have thought was boredom.

"The Mera family. The room beside the kitchen."

Deshi spoke immediately. "Beside the kitchen? We'll smell cooking all night. Is there nothing—"

"It is what is available," Olet said. Not with emphasis. Not with apology. He said it the way he might have said the stone was stone, or the rain was rain.

Deshi's mouth thinned. She looked at Suna as though Suna were responsible for both the assignment and the custodian's tone. Suna picked up their bags.

"The Parra family. The alcove by the east wall."

The thin young man — Jin — stood without speaking and lifted his daughter onto his hip in one motion that said habit. The girl was awake now, her face pressed into his neck, her arms locked around him with the fierce concentration of a child who had decided that this particular adult was the only fixed point in the room. Jin collected his single bag and walked to the alcove Olet had indicated, a recessed space in the stone wall barely deep enough for two mats. He set the girl down, arranged his bag behind her as a backrest, and sat beside her. He did not look at anyone. He did not complain about the size or the placement. He took what was given with the absolute absence of objection that is its own kind of wall.

"The Tesh family. The west annex."

The old woman — Gitta — rose with the same measured precision she'd used on the stairs. Her son offered his arm. She took it without acknowledgment, as though the arm were a railing, a feature of the architecture rather than a person's gesture. The daughter-in-law gathered the younger girl by the hand. The teenage boy — Fen — picked up the heaviest bag and followed his grandmother, matching his pace to hers without being asked.

"The Dao family. The room past the cistern."

The two sisters. The older one nodded and began to move, her children close. The younger one stood for a moment longer than the rest, her gaze settling on the open arch and the valley beyond. The brown water was darker now as the light failed, and the sound of it had taken on the quality it would have all night — a low, continuous pressure that was not loud but was never absent. Then she turned and followed her sister without having spoken a word.

"The Leo family. The alcove near the prayer courtyard."

The anxious man — Cade — exchanged a glance with his partner, a quick look that carried a question and its answer in the space of a second. His partner touched the boy's shoulder. His mother, a woman in her sixties with a canvas bag and the expression of someone who would form her own opinion in her own time, followed. They moved together with the careful coordination of a family that had practiced being unremarkable.

"These are your spaces," Olet said. "You may arrange them as you wish. The kitchen area is communal. Water is from the cistern. The prayer courtyard is open to all. The custodians' quarters are not. If you need something, ask."

"Is there a radio?" Bram said from his corner. "We haven't had news in two days."

"There is a radio. It is in the custodians' quarters. We will share what news comes."

"Can we hear it ourselves?"

Olet paused — not the pause of a man considering a request, but the pause of a man allowing a request to finish before declining it. "The custodians will share what news comes," he repeated, in the same tone and at the same pace, as though he were reading the same line from the same text.

Bram's hands rotated again at the wrists. His daughter was watching this with the particular attention of someone cataloguing an adult's failures, and she caught Suna looking at her and dropped her gaze so fast it was itself an admission: the girl saw things and did not want to be seen seeing them.

Suna carried their bags into the room beside the kitchen. It was small. The stone floor was dry but cold, and the single window — barely more than a slot in the wall — opened onto the kitchen overhang, from which she could already smell ash and old cooking fires.

"Six of us," Deshi said from the doorway. "In this."

"We'll manage," Suna said. She was laying out mats — thin, woven, serviceable — from a stack the custodians had placed in the hall. Three for the children, one for Deshi, one for Peyam, one for herself. The room was perhaps four meters by three. She could touch both walls if she stood in the center and stretched her arms.

"The large family has a room three times this size."

"They're twelve people."

"We're six. This isn't proportional."

Suna didn't answer. She was counting: mats, blankets, their food supply from the truck — two bags of rice, dried lentils, salt, the tin of cooking oil that had leaked in the bag and now smelled rancid. Not much. If the custodians fed them, it wouldn't matter. If they didn't, it would matter quickly.

"Peyam needs space for his knees," Deshi said. "He can't sleep with his legs bent. You know that."

"I know." Suna positioned Peyam's mat against the far wall, where he could extend his legs without anyone stepping over them. She would take the mat nearest the door. The children between her and Deshi. This was the arrangement. Suna looked at it and saw the next days — the six of them in this room, the stone walls close, the kitchen smell, the sound of the flood outside the window, and no end she could name.

***

By nightfall, the rain had not stopped. It arrived in long, rolling waves — ten minutes of hammering followed by a lull that was never quite silence, then the hammering again. The shrine groaned with it, or the wind groaned through the shrine's stone openings, a low sound that wound through the rooms and corridors and made the oil lamps pull toward the walls.

Suna fed her children from their own supplies — rice cooked in the communal kitchen, lentils, a scrap of pickle that Deshi had packed and now parceled out in slivers, setting each one on the rice with the deliberate precision of someone distributing medicine. The kitchen was the most exposed part of the compound, an outdoor stone counter and fire pit covered by a deep overhang that kept the rain off but not the wind. Suna cooked beside the older Dao sister — Hala, she learned from the woman's daughter, who wandered over to ask Kael if he wanted to see a beetle. Hala worked with the steady competence of someone who could cook for ten in any conditions and had done it often enough that rain and strange kitchens were background noise. They did not speak much, but they shared the fire and the counter space with the wordless negotiation of women accustomed to working in kitchens not their own: a glance to claim a burner, a nod to share the salt, the brief choreography of two bodies avoiding each other's elbows. Hala's hands were large and certain, and she sliced an onion with the heel of her palm guiding the knife — a technique Suna recognized from her own mother, from another region entirely, and the recognition landed in her body before her mind caught up with it.

In the prayer courtyard, which was open to the sky and therefore open to the rain, Peyam stood barefoot on the wet stone and prayed. Suna could see him from the kitchen overhang — a thin, elderly figure with his hands together and his eyes closed and the rain running down his face without interrupting him. He had not asked whether prayer was permitted. He had not asked where to pray. He had found the courtyard, removed his sandals, and begun. His prayer beads hung from his right hand, clicking softly against each other, a small, regular sound that the rain could not quite erase. When he finished, he stood for a moment with his face turned up to the sky, and then he put his sandals on and walked back inside with the careful steps of a man who had just concluded a private piece of business and was ready for the next thing.

After dinner — if you could call it dinner, families eating in clusters in a stone room by lamplight while the rain beat on the world — Suna washed the children's faces and hands with water from the cistern. Kael submitted to it. Rimi did it herself, turning her back to the room for privacy, which she'd started doing six months ago. Asha turned the washing into exploration, rubbing her wet fingers against Suna's wrist, pressing her palm against the cool stone rim of the cistern basin, tasting the water — "Don't drink that, Asha" — and then, as Suna was wringing out the cloth, reaching up and touching the face of a boy who was waiting behind them.

The boy was Kondo. Nine or ten, round-cheeked, with a smudge of something on his collar that he'd been trying to rub out with his thumb. He looked at Asha's hand on his cheek with surprise but not displeasure, the way you'd react to a cat that had walked up and placed its paw on your knee.

"Sorry," Suna said, and caught Asha's wrist. "She does that."

"It's fine," the boy said. He touched his own cheek where Asha's hand had been, briefly, as though checking what she might have found there, and went back to his family's section of the compound.

Asha watched him go with her hand still open, her fingers slightly curled, as though she were holding the impression of his skin in her palm.

Their room was lamplight and stone and the close fact of six people in a space built for fewer. Peyam had not moved since lying down after his prayer. His beads clicked once, softly, and then he was still, asleep with the promptness of a man who had been falling asleep in strange places for seven decades and no longer required comfort to do it. Deshi was on her mat, her back to the room, though Suna could tell by the quality of her breathing that she was not asleep. She was composing tomorrow's complaints, or she was thinking about her son, or she was doing the same arithmetic Suna was doing — supplies, days, the distance between here and anywhere else — and coming to the same numbers.

Suna settled the children. Kael on his back, knees up, the way he'd slept since he was three. Rimi on her side, facing the wall, her breathing deliberately regulated — the twelve-year-old's performance of sleep for the benefit of the room. Asha last. She lay on her mat between Suna and Deshi, and her hands continued to move even as her eyes closed — fingers tracing the edge of the mat, the seam of the blanket, the loose weave of the cotton. She found the hem of Suna's sleeve and rubbed it between thumb and forefinger, that same slow investigation. Her breathing lengthened. Her fingers slowed. But they did not stop. Even at the lip of sleep, Asha was reading the world through her skin, taking the measure of each surface, each texture, each boundary between one material and the next. It was how she learned things. It was how she knew what she knew, through the pads of her fingers, through the palms of her hands, through the patient, unstoppable curiosity of a child whose body was a kind of open question.

Suna lay on her back and looked at the stone ceiling and did not sleep.

She could hear the compound settling around her. A man's voice, low and insistent, from the direction of the Kondo room — Venn, establishing something, commanding something, the bass note of a patriarch who could not stop managing even at the end of a day that had defeated him. The baby crying, thin and persistent, the kind of cry that was not hunger or pain but the pure bewilderment of an infant in a place that smelled wrong. Melie Parra's father murmuring something to his daughter in the alcove, too low to hear, the sound of his voice like a hand held out in the dark. The older Tesh woman coughing once, dry and careful. Someone walking on the stone floor, barefoot, the soft pad of footsteps going somewhere private.

And beneath all of it, the flood. The valley's steady rush, climbing the hill, filling the silence the way water fills a basin — from the bottom, from the edges, with a patience that had nothing to do with intent and everything to do with weight.

"Mama." Kael, already half asleep, his voice dropped to the pitch he used only at the edge of consciousness — when his bravery wore off and he was just a boy in a strange room and the rain was louder than anything he'd slept through before. "When are we going to Papa?"

"When the road opens."

"When does the road open?"

"When the water goes down."

"When does—"

"Sleep, Kael."

He slept. Children could do that — accept an insufficient answer and let it carry them under. Rimi, beside him, was still awake. Suna could tell by the held quality of her stillness, the deliberate steadiness of her breathing. Twelve years old and already performing rest for the benefit of the people around her. She had her father's long face and, Suna was beginning to fear, her father's talent for silence that meant something other than calm.

Suna pressed her hand flat against the stone floor beside her mat. It was cold. The cold entered her palm and climbed her wrist, and she held it there, the solidity of it, the factual indifference of the stone to everything she was carrying.

In the morning, or in two days, or whenever the rain stopped and the water dropped and the roads emerged from the flood like a landscape returning after anesthesia, she would take her children to Gelden. She would arrive at her husband's family's house and explain. That was the plan. That had been the plan since he left.

Since he left. Not since he went ahead. Since he left.

The distinction lived in Suna's body the way a swallowed coin might — hard, foreign, impossible to digest, impossible to pass. He had not gone ahead to prepare the house, to find work, to make things ready for them. He had packed a bag while the children were at school and Deshi was at the market and Peyam was praying, and he had taken his good shoes and the money from the tin above the stove and left a note that said *I'm sorry, I can't do this,* and Suna had found the note and read it and folded it into a square the size of a stamp and put it in her pocket and gone to pick up the children from school. She had made dinner. She had told Deshi that evening that her son had gone ahead to Gelden to prepare. Deshi had looked at her with an expression Suna could not decode — surprise, or suspicion, or the particular stillness of a woman who already knew and was deciding how to hold the knowledge — and said, "Without telling me?"

"It was sudden," Suna had said. "He wanted to get ahead of the rains."

Three months. Three months of sudden. Three months of Kael asking when they were going to Papa and Suna saying soon, and Rimi watching her with those assessing eyes that were her father's eyes in her father's face, and Deshi's questions arriving like weather — indirect, persistent, unanswerable. Three months of performing a marriage that was not a marriage, of pointing toward a destination where no one was waiting, of drawing the shape of a family around a space where a man had been and was not.

And now this. The flood, the shrine, forty-two strangers and a stone room and a custodian who assigned sleeping quarters with the finality of a man who had seen a hundred families arrive at his door and knew that none of them would be the same when they left. She was stuck on a hilltop with her three children and her mother-in-law and a lie that grew heavier with each repetition, and at the other end of the road — whenever the road surfaced — there was no husband waiting. There was a house belonging to people she had met twice, who would look at her standing in their doorway with three children and their bags and know by the shape of her arrival that their son had done what he'd done.

The panic came the way it always did — not as a wave but as a list. What she owed. What she couldn't pay. The rice in the bag, enough for perhaps four days if she stretched it. Kael's sandals wearing through at the soles. Rimi's silence, which was getting longer. Asha's hands, always moving, always reaching, the four-year-old's body open to everything in a world that was not safe to be open to. Peyam's knees. Deshi's questions. The lie about the husband. The money running out. The road underwater. The note in her pocket — she still had it, folded into its stamp-sized square, in the inner pocket of the bag where she kept the children's things, and sometimes she touched it the way Peyam touched his first prayer bead, compulsively, to confirm it was still there, that the thing she refused to look at was still a fact she would eventually have to answer.

From somewhere in the compound, a woman was humming. The melody was unfamiliar, low, circling back on itself the way lullabies do — built not for arrival but for return, the same phrase offered again and again until the child surrendered to it. It was not for Suna. It was for some other woman's child in some other family's room. But Suna lay in the dark and listened to it, and felt, beneath the panic and the lists and the weight of the lie she'd repeated so many times it had almost replaced the thing it covered, the involuntary warmth of being held inside a building full of people who were also afraid, also carrying things, also trying to get their children to sleep in a place that was not home and might not let them leave.

Asha's hand found hers. Small, warm, slightly damp. The child's fingers closed around two of Suna's fingers and held them — not gripping, just holding, with that gentle, total attention she gave to every surface, as if Suna's hand were another texture to be read, another piece of the world to be absorbed and known.

The rain hammered the shrine. The flood talked in the valley below. Suna closed her eyes and did not sleep, and outside the night grew heavy with water and the roads stayed gone.


# Chapter 2

The morning light through the main hall's open side had the color of rinsed brass, weak and already blurred by the humidity that preceded rain. Fasa moved through the space with a cloth, wiping the stone ledges where the oil lamps would sit, though they had been wiped yesterday and the day before that and every day for twenty years. The preparation was not cleaning. It was attention — the same sustained looking that preceded prayer, that preceded diagnosis, that preceded any act of genuine encounter. She had learned, in twenty years, that the preparation mattered more than the ceremony itself. The ceremony was speech. The preparation was seeing.

She set the cloth on the ledge and crouched to check the oil level in the first lamp. The wick was trimmed, the reservoir half full. She moved to the second. The routine of her hands freed her mind to do what it had been doing since yesterday's arrivals: reading.

Seven families. Forty-three people, if she counted correctly from Olet's registration ledger. They had come up the switchback path through yesterday's rain, arriving in clusters and singles over the course of the afternoon — some carrying luggage on their heads, one family hauling a cart they'd abandoned at the second turn, a young man climbing the last stretch with his daughter on his back and a bag in his free hand. She had watched from this hall, standing where she stood now, while Olet and the three assistant custodians received them below. She had not gone down. She never went down. The receiving was Olet's charge. The naming was hers.

What she had, before the naming began, was a set of observations. They were not conclusions. They were shapes that might sharpen into meaning or dissolve into the ordinary strangeness of strangers thrust together.

The Kondo family: twelve people, the largest group, arriving in a column with the patriarch at its front and the youngest children sheltered in the middle, moving with the disciplined coordination of a family that traveled together often. The patriarch — Venn — had spoken to Olet at registration with the diction of a man who considered his name sufficient introduction. His wife, Tila, had counted sleeping mats and then asked about the kitchen's water supply, twice, the repetition of a woman who needed to know her household could function before she could rest. The elderly aunt, Mava, had a slow left-hand gesture that Fasa had watched three times before recognizing it as habitual rather than palsy — a circling of her palm against her left ear, steady and self-soothing and as natural to her as breathing. She had settled into the corner nearest the open wall and sat looking at the rain with the patience of someone who had looked at a great many things in seventy years and had made her peace with waiting.

The Mera family: a woman of thirty-eight, her mother-in-law, an elderly uncle, three children. No husband. The woman — Suna — had a practiced competence about her, the efficiency of someone managing on insufficient resources. She had organized her children's sleeping area with the economy of practice, the kind of arrangement that spoke not of a household that ran smoothly but of one that ran on her hands alone. The mother-in-law, Deshi, had watched her with an expression Fasa could not yet read: judgment or concern or that corrosive blend of both that family produces when the person you depend on is also the person you blame.

A man alone with a daughter. Jin, the registration said. The child was six. They had taken the smallest sleeping space without being asked, as if practiced at taking up as little room as possible. The man carried the child's bag and his own in the same hand, a habit that suggested the other hand needed to be free for the child at all times. His speech, in the few words Fasa had heard him exchange with Olet, had a slight catch — a jaw-lock before certain consonants that she had first thought was the clenching of cold or exhaustion and then recognized as a permanent feature of his voice.

The Teshes: a man in his forties named Miro, thick-handed, his weight carried low, the build of someone who had worked outdoors most of his life. His wife Enn, dark-haired, a nervous habit of pulling her braid over her left shoulder and then pushing it back. Their daughter Kiri, eight, already asleep before the evening meal was served. A teenage boy, Fen, sixteen, tall, dark-haired, who had helped unload the Teshes' luggage without being asked and then sat on the edge of the group and read a book that was too waterlogged to read. And the grandmother — Gitta. Small and thin and careful in her movements. She had answered Olet's registration questions with a composure Fasa recognized. It was the composure of someone who had been asked these questions before and had her answers in order — not rehearsed, but prepared. The difference was subtle and might be nothing. Some people were simply organized.

The Okoyes: a family that moved with the urgency of people fleeing something rather than traveling toward something. The father, Bram, was tall and thin, with glasses and the posture of a teacher — slightly forward, engaged, hands shaping the air as he spoke. His wife, Nessa, carried the baby against her chest and kept her other hand on the ten-year-old boy's shoulder even when the boy was sitting still and needed no steadying. The eldest, a girl of fifteen named Leni, had said nothing to any adult since arrival. She had bitten her nails through the evening meal and watched every family in the hall with the unfiltered attention of someone who had not yet learned to disguise her looking.

The Daos: two sisters and their children. The older sister, Hala, had spoken for both of them — sturdy, protective, a woman who positioned her body between the room and the people she was responsible for. The younger, Bett, had not spoken at all since arriving. Not to Olet, not to the other families, not, as far as Fasa could tell, even to her own daughter — a girl of seven named Nori who held Hala's hand rather than Bett's and seemed to understand the arrangement as permanent rather than circumstantial. Bett was the only adult in the compound who had not exchanged a single social word since arrival. Her silence was not shyness. Fasa knew shyness, and it looked down, looked away, looked for cover. Bett's silence looked straight ahead.

The Leos: two men, a child, and an older woman. The younger of the two men, Cade, had started three sentences during registration and abandoned each one before finding the phrasing he needed. His partner, Wynn, had steadied him with a hand on his arm — a touch that was brief and habitual and spoke of years of knowing when the hand was needed. The child was seven, a boy named Pik, and Cade had not used the word adopted with Olet. He had said our son. The older woman was Cade's mother, Vera, and she had watched her son struggle with the registration form with the expression of a woman who had decided, years ago, that she would fight anyone who made her son explain himself and was assessing whether this was going to be one of those times.

Fasa straightened and set the last lamp on its ledge. She stepped back to survey the hall. The stone floor had been swept. The low benches were arranged in a loose semicircle facing the spot where she would stand. Behind her, the valley view: brown floodwater covering the roads below, and beyond the water the green smear of submerged fields and the suggestion of trees. The sound of the flood was the shrine's constant breath — a low roar that the ear adjusted to within an hour but that the body never quite forgot. She could feel it in the soles of her feet, transmitted through the stone.

She had been wrong before. She had read families as closed who turned out to be merely shy, read silences as concealment when they were only grief, seen patterns that resolved into coincidence or wishful thinking. The naming revealed, but it revealed on its own schedule, and twenty years had taught her to be patient with its pace and her own errors. What the years had given her was not certainty but the discipline to wait for it, and the willingness to look at strangers and believe — genuinely, with the full weight of her practice and her faith — that what they declared was never the whole of what they were.

She heard Olet's step behind her, unhurried, identifiable by the particular weight he placed on his right foot.

"Are they ready?" she asked.

"They are here," Olet said. It was his way — he answered what was true rather than what was asked. "I will ring the bell when you wish."

"Give me a moment."

He waited. He was good at waiting. In twenty years she had never seen him impatient, and the consistency of his calm had shifted, over those years, from reassuring to unsettling to simply the weather of his character. He stood in the doorway with his lean frame and his face that revealed nothing, and she had long since stopped trying to read him the way she read the families. Olet was not a text. He was a wall — not opaque but unneeding of interpretation.

"What do you think of them?" she asked.

"I think they are seven families stranded by a flood." He paused, and the pause was not hesitation but the natural rhythm of a man who treated silence as punctuation. "What they are beyond that is the naming's question, not mine."

She nodded. The bell, when it came, would mean obligation. She was about to ask forty-three strangers to stand in this stone hall and say who they were. Most would find it a small discomfort — a toll paid for shelter, unreasonable but endurable. Some would find it invasive. One or two might resist, though resistance in a building where the custodians controlled the food stores was a luxury only the very principled or the very well-provisioned could sustain.

She did not think of the naming as coercive. She thought of it as a question the road itself asked, a question the shrine merely gave form and voice and the discipline of witness. Twenty years of watching strangers arrive and declare and depart had taught her that the declaration was never the whole truth — but the shape of the untruth, the way a family chose to arrange itself for display, told her more than honesty would have. The gaps were the naming's real material. The silences, the pauses, the words chosen and the words avoided. What a family did not say was the door the naming would eventually open.

"Ring the bell," she said.

---

Olet rang it three times. The iron note carried across the compound, through the kitchen overhang where someone was boiling water for tea, past the cistern, into the sleeping quarters where mats were still unrolled from the night. The sound cut the morning into before and after. It was the sound of being asked.

They came in clusters, the way families always did — the large groups first, the small ones drifting in behind, the solitary figures last. The Kondos arrived and took up the most space, which was not rudeness but the physics of twelve bodies accustomed to arranging themselves as a unit. Venn led. His wife Tila counted heads as they entered — Fasa saw her lips move, tallying — and steered the younger children toward the bench nearest the open wall, where the light was best and the air moved. Mava settled into her seat with the measured descent of a woman whose joints made every change in altitude a negotiation, and her left hand went immediately to her ear, circling. Arash, the eldest son, came through the doorway last among the Kondos. He was built like his father but leaner, less settled in his body, and he positioned himself at the edge of the family cluster — not quite apart, not quite among them, a young man maintaining a distance so slight that only someone watching for it would notice.

The Meras followed: Suna first with Asha on her hip, then the older children, Rimi walking with her eyes on the floor and Kael scanning the room as if looking for something he expected to find. Behind them came Deshi, whose posture entered the room before she did — spine straight, chin elevated, the carriage of a woman who considered herself the standard against which arrangements were measured. Peyam came last, a thin old man in a faded prayer cap embroidered with a pattern so worn that only the thread's shadow remained. He found a spot near the wall and folded himself into it with the efficient grace of someone who had been sitting on stone floors for seven decades and knew exactly how to distribute his weight.

The Okoyes came together: Bram slightly in front, Nessa behind with baby Ada held close to her chest, Tomas walking between them, and Leni trailing. The girl sat on the bench nearest the door, as close to an exit as the hall's geometry allowed, and crossed her arms in a posture that Fasa had seen in a hundred teenagers — the posture of someone who wanted everyone to know she was here under protest.

Jin Parra came in carrying Melie, set her down near the Mera children, and took a seat at the end of the bench without looking at anyone. He sat with his hands on his knees and his shoulders slightly hunched, the posture of someone who had already calculated the minimum amount of space he was entitled to and was occupying exactly that much.

The Teshes entered quietly — Miro first, then Enn with Kiri between them. Enn's hand was on Kiri's shoulder, steadying or steering or simply maintaining contact, and she kept adjusting her braid with her free hand, pulling it forward and then pushing it back. Fen followed, and Fasa noticed that the boy had found a different book — a thin paperback he slid into his back pocket as he entered, the kind of instinctive concealment of a private pleasure that teenagers performed without thinking. At the back, Gitta moved with the slow precision of someone for whom each step was a considered act. She chose a seat on the far bench, arranged herself, and folded her hands in her lap. Her thumbs rested against each other. She did not look around the room. She did not need to.

The Daos arrived as two smaller bodies within the family body: Hala with Raya and Sem, then Bett with Nori. Hala guided them to a bench near the Okoyes with the efficiency of a woman accustomed to positioning people. Raya sat with her mother's face but a sharper chin, her legs drawn up, her sandal strap twisted and apparently bothering her — she kept reaching down to adjust it without committing to actually fixing it. Sem sat beside his sister and immediately began picking at a callus on his right palm, the focused absorption of a boy who had found something more interesting than the room. Bett sat at the end of the bench. Nori sat beside her with three inches of space between the girl's knee and Bett's thigh — close but not touching, a gap that seemed too precise to be accidental.

The Leos came last. Cade walked in with Pik's hand in his, and Wynn walked beside them carrying a canvas bag that sagged with a parent's preparedness — the things Pik might need if the ceremony took longer than a seven-year-old's patience could hold. Vera, Cade's mother, entered behind them, compact and gray-haired, and sat on the bench beside Wynn with her arms crossed over her chest and her gaze already moving across the room, cataloguing everyone with the vigilance of a woman who had learned that the world did not always accommodate her son and had decided to be ready for it.

Forty-three people in a stone hall. Five custodians — Olet at the doorway, the three assistants along the far wall, Fasa at the center. The sound of breathing, of children shifting, of rain beginning outside, of the flood's constant exhalation from the valley below. The oil lamps were not yet lit — the morning was bright enough that their light would be wasted, and Fasa had learned to read faces better in daylight than in the amber generosity of flame.

She stood where the floor was worn smooth by feet. Twenty years of namings had polished the stone to a dull sheen, and she felt the coolness of it through her sandals. She let the silence work for a moment. Silence in a room this full was not an absence — it was the sound of the flood and the rain and the baby breathing and someone's foot scraping against stone and a child whispering and the whisper being hushed.

"My name is Fasa," she said. "I am the custodian of the naming at this shrine. Olet, whom you met when you arrived, oversees the shrine's daily life. The naming is my charge."

She let the word charge sit. It was a word she chose deliberately — not duty, not role, not responsibility, but charge. Something given and something carried.

"The naming is a condition of shelter here. That is not my choice, and it is not recent — it is the shrine's law, older than I am and older than Olet and older than the road that brought you. Every group that stays more than one night is asked to declare itself. Your family name. Your number. Each member's name and their relation to the speaker. Where you have come from and where you are going. This is what the shrine asks in exchange for its roof."

She watched their faces. Venn Kondo had the expression of a man who found the process unnecessary but not worth contesting — he would comply because compliance cost him nothing he valued. Bram Okoye was frowning, a frown she recognized — a mind already assembling an objection it had not yet decided to voice. Suna Mera had shifted Asha to her other hip. Jin Parra had not moved. Gitta Tesh was watching Fasa with an attention so mild it was nearly invisible.

"Each family speaks for itself. One person may speak for the whole family, or each member may speak in turn. I will ask questions when I have them. The naming is not an interrogation." She paused. She believed this, mostly. "It is a declaration. You tell me who you are. That is all."

The rain hit the stone overhang outside with a sound like dry rice poured from a jar.

"Who will speak first?"

Venn Kondo stood. He had been waiting for the question — his body had been preparing for the act of standing since she began speaking, weight shifting forward, hands on his thighs, the gathering of a man who considered being first not an act of volunteering but a confirmation of rank. He was broad-shouldered, thick in the neck, graying at the temples, and he stood with the particular authority of someone who expected the room to organize itself around him.

"We are the Kondo family," he said. "Twelve. I am Venn Kondo." He turned and gestured with an open hand, precise, introducing his people the way a man introduces his holdings. "My wife, Tila." Tila inclined her head — a small, practical acknowledgment. "My son, Arash." Arash, from the edge of the group, lifted one hand briefly and let it fall. "My sister Habel and her daughter Ori." Habel drew Ori against her side, and the nine-year-old leaned into her mother's ribs without looking up. "My aunt Mava." The old woman in the corner made no gesture. Her left hand continued its slow orbit around her ear. "My brother Pol's sons, their wives, and their children." He gestured toward the remaining members — younger men and women, children between four and eleven who looked up when indicated and then lost interest. "We are traveling to Arash's wedding. The bride's family is in Tessar. When the road opens, we will continue."

"Twelve," Fasa said. "All Kondos? By blood or by marriage?"

"By blood or by marriage. Yes."

"Thank you." She noted the declaration: the most complete she would hear that morning. Every member accounted for, every relation specified, the purpose of travel stated, the family name offered as both fact and credential. Venn sat down with the controlled descent of a man who had paid a toll and expected his receipt. "Who is next?"

Suna Mera stood. She set Asha on the bench beside Deshi, and the four-year-old immediately reached for her grandmother's sleeve. Deshi permitted this without looking down. Suna straightened and clasped her hands in front of her — strong hands, work-roughened, held together as if to keep them from doing something more useful than speaking.

"Suna Mera. There are six of us." She drew a breath. "My mother-in-law, Deshi." Deshi's chin rose a fraction — an acknowledgment that was also an evaluation. "Her brother, Peyam." The old man in the prayer cap touched his forehead in a gesture that compressed greeting, acknowledgment, and a kind of habitual blessing into a single motion of the hand. "My daughter Rimi. She's twelve." Rimi continued staring at the floor. "My son Kael, eight." The boy was watching his mother with a concentration that Fasa recognized — the alertness of a child listening for a wrong note, who had learned that sometimes the adults' words did not match the adults' faces. "And Asha. She's four."

"Six," Fasa said. "Traveling where?"

"South. To my sister's town."

"And your husband?"

The pause was barely a breath. Fasa had been watching for it, and she almost missed it — a fractional tightening of the clasped hands, a stillness in the shoulders that was gone before it could be named.

"He went ahead. He's waiting for us there."

Fasa held the moment. She did not challenge. She noted. A woman traveling south to her sister's town with her husband's mother and her husband's uncle but without her husband, who had gone ahead. The arrangement was not impossible — it might mean nothing more than different schedules, different obligations, the ordinary logistics of moving a family across a country in crisis. But the pause was there, and the pause had a weight to it, and the weight might be embarrassment or might be something else.

"Thank you," she said. She turned to the room.

Bram Okoye stood before she could invite the next family. He stood the way he must have stood in front of thirty students — spine straight, weight forward, chin level, a man who had spent years making himself legible from the back row.

"Bram Okoye. My wife, Nessa." Nessa's arms tightened fractionally around Ada. "Our children. Leni, fifteen. Tomas, ten. Ada — she's one. We are five."

"Where are you coming from?"

"The capital." His jaw worked for a moment before the rest came. "There are checkpoints on the eastern roads. Identity verification, household declarations. We decided to take the mountain route instead."

"Which is now underwater," Fasa said.

"Which is now underwater." He met her eyes. The repetition was not agreement — it was the acknowledgment of a shared fact between two people who might draw very different conclusions from it.

"You're a teacher?"

"I was." He did not elaborate. Nessa looked at the floor. The correction — was, not am — carried the weight of something lost recently enough to still draw blood when touched.

Fasa understood what Bram had not said and did not need to: the checkpoints and the identity verification and the household declarations that had driven his family to the mountain road were part of the same political machinery that had diverted the road crews and left the valley flooded. A government that wanted to know who everyone was, that demanded families declare themselves in categories the state designed. The naming asked a version of the same question. She was aware of this parallel. She had been aware of it for months, and the awareness made her conduct the naming with more care, not less — but she could not demand that the families feel the difference between the shrine's question and the state's. She could only ask, and listen, and note.

"Thank you," she said.

Jin Parra did not stand. He spoke from where he sat, his hands on his knees, his voice flat and pitched to carry the minimum distance.

"Jin Parra. My daughter, Melie. She's six."

Fasa waited. Jin did not continue. The hall waited with her — the quality of the silence changed, became expectant, became the silence of thirty-seven adults and their accumulated curiosity pressing gently on one quiet man.

"Two of you," she said.

"Two."

"Where are you going?"

"North. To her mother's family."

"Her mother isn't with you?"

The jaw-lock. She could see it clearly this time: a brief seizure of the muscles that controlled his jaw, a held breath that resolved into speech one beat later than natural. The hesitation was part of his voice, she thought, the way some people's voices carried a rasp or a lisp — a feature, not a flaw. But the hesitation lengthened around this question, and the lengthening was not the feature. It was the question.

"She died."

"I'm sorry," Fasa said. "When?"

"Three months ago."

"And you're bringing Melie to her mother's people."

"Yes."

He offered nothing more. Fasa could feel the room's attention pressing on him — the sympathy, the curiosity, the faint discomfort of watching a man declare the shape of his life in three sentences and then close the door. Melie, who had moved from the bench to sit on the floor with Asha Mera, was not listening. She was braiding a piece of string she had found in a crack between the flagstones, holding it up to the light from the open wall and examining it with the total seriousness of a child who had found the one task in the room worth doing.

The declaration was the minimum possible. Name, number, destination, the single fact of the wife's death offered as a wall rather than a window. No narrative, no detail, no emotional register beyond flat statement. Fasa had heard grief declarations before — some were floods, some were angry, some collapsed into weeping that the room absorbed in collective discomfort. Jin's was a closed hand. She could not see what it held, and she did not know whether what she could not see was more grief or something else entirely.

"Thank you," she said.

Miro Tesh stood. He rose with the unhurried competence of a man who had spent his life standing up and sitting down in rooms where people waited — a field, a market, a job site. His hands hung at his sides, thick-fingered, the nails trimmed short and clean, the hands of someone who worked outdoors and was careful about it.

"The Tesh family. I'm Miro. My wife, Enn." Enn pulled her braid over her shoulder and held it there, as if the braid were a handle she needed to anchor herself. "Our daughter Kiri — she's eight. My son Fen." He paused, the ordinary pause of a man moving through a list and arriving at a relationship that required a different grammatical construction. "Sixteen. And my mother, Gitta."

"Five," Fasa said. "Where are you traveling?"

"My wife's cousin passed. We're going to the funeral. Were going." He corrected himself with a grimace that was both grief-adjacent and practical — the grimace of a man acknowledging that the dead cousin was still dead and the road was still flooded and neither fact was going to improve by being discussed in this hall. "In the valley."

"I'm sorry for your loss." The formality was directed at Enn, who dipped her head. "And Fen — he is your son?"

"He's my mother's grandson. She raised him."

Fasa turned to Gitta. The old woman sat as she had sat since entering — hands folded, thumbs still, back straight, her face arranged in an expression of such mild patience that it was nearly impossible to read as anything other than courtesy. She was small and white-haired and still with the economy of someone who conserved energy not from frailty but from long practice in knowing exactly how much each gesture cost.

"He is your grandson?" Fasa asked.

"He is my grandson." Gitta's voice was low and clear and carried no inflection that could be interpreted as anything but a simple declaration of a simple fact. She did not elaborate. She did not pause. She did not offer the small softening gestures — a glance at Fen, a nod, a smile — that grandmothers typically offered when speaking of their grandchildren in public. The sentence sat in the air with the solidity of something that had been true for so long it no longer needed proving.

It was the smoothest declaration Fasa had heard that morning. Every other family had produced a moment of friction — Venn's formality, Suna's fractional pause, Bram's corrected tense, Jin's closed hand. Gitta Tesh had no friction. Her declaration was a surface without visible seam, a door so flush with its frame that the eye passed over it. He is my grandson. Four words, evenly weighted, perfectly still.

Fasa noted this and moved on. She would return to it later, in the privacy of her own thinking. For now, she filed it alongside the other shapes: another observation, another thing that might be nothing, another surface whose smoothness might indicate composure or might indicate practice.

"Thank you," she said.

Hala Dao stood. She rose with her shoulders squared and her body angled slightly in front of the bench where Bett and the children sat, the unconscious geometry of a woman who had been positioning herself between her people and the room for so many years that the stance had become her resting posture.

"The Daos," she said. "I'm Hala. My children — Raya, thirteen, and Sem, eleven." Sem stopped picking at his callus and looked up at his mother with an expression that was half attention and half the particular interest of a boy who enjoyed hearing his own name said aloud by someone he admired. "My sister Bett." Hala gestured. Bett, at the end of the bench, did not acknowledge the gesture — did not nod, did not shift, did not perform any of the small social signals by which a person confirms that she has been named and accepts the naming. She sat with her arms at her sides and her face set. "And Nori. Bett's daughter. Seven."

Nori, in the gap between Bett's thigh and the end of the bench, looked at the floor.

"Five," Fasa said. "Where are you going?"

"The coast. Visiting family." Hala's voice had the flattened tone of someone who considered this none of Fasa's business but had decided that the price of objecting was higher than the price of answering.

"And your husbands?"

"I'm not married." The sentence came without apology, without explanation, without the defensive sharpness that some women brought to the statement. It was fact, the same way twelve was fact for the Kondos.

Fasa looked at Bett. "And you?"

Bett met her eyes. Her face in the morning light was a surface without movement — guarded, set, the controlled musculature of someone who had decided precisely how much of herself was available and was not going to revisit the decision. She did not speak. The silence lasted two breaths, three. The rain filled it. The flood beneath the rain filled it.

"Bett isn't married either," Hala said. The words were protective — not answering for Bett but covering for her, the way an older sister covers when the younger one won't play the game.

Fasa held Bett's gaze for a moment longer. Bett did not look away. She did not offer the small social concession of a nod or a shrug or even the thinning of her lips that would have signaled irritation. She simply sat inside her refusal and let Fasa's attention pass over her the way stone lets rain pass over it — without absorbing, without responding, without being changed.

"Thank you," Fasa said.

Cade Leo stood last. He stood slowly, and Wynn's hand went to his wrist and then dropped — a gesture of support withdrawn because the support might be visible and the visibility might become a thing that needed explaining in front of forty strangers. Pik, between them, looked up at Cade and then went back to rolling a chip of stone between his fingers, the smooth repetitive motion of a child whose hands always needed occupation.

"We're the Leos," Cade said. His voice was steady, but the steadiness was assembled — Fasa could hear the effort holding it level, the way you could hear the effort in a bridge that was carrying more weight than it had been built for. "I'm Cade. This is my partner, Wynn." The word partner landed in the room and lay there. Fasa noted who looked up and who did not. Venn Kondo's expression did not change. Deshi Mera's did, briefly — a flicker of assessment that might have been disapproval or might have been nothing more than the registering of new information. "Our son, Pik. He's seven. And my mother, Vera."

Vera sat with her arms crossed over her chest and her jaw set in an expression that did not dare anyone to speak — it simply communicated, with the economy of a woman who had lived sixty-one years and spent a meaningful number of them defending her son's choices, that she was present and she was watching and she had opinions she was not yet sharing.

"Four," Fasa said. "Where are you traveling?"

"We're relocating." Cade glanced at Bram and back — recognizing the parallel, acknowledging it, filing it. "The new registration laws require household declarations. Categories." He stopped. The word categories sat in the air like something he had picked up and found heavier than expected. "We're going somewhere the forms are simpler."

Wynn spoke. He had a quiet voice, the kind that made people lean forward to hear and in leaning forward gave him their attention without his having to demand it. "Pik starts school in the autumn. We want to be settled before then." The practical detail — school, autumn — was a floor laid over the more complicated architecture of what Cade had been trying to say. Wynn had built the floor with the ease of someone who had done it before.

"And Pik is your son?" Fasa asked. "By birth, or —"

"He's our son." Cade's voice found its footing on this sentence. It was the same grammatical structure as Gitta's — he is my grandson, he is our son — but where Gitta's had been seamless, smooth as poured water, Cade's vibrated with the effort of compressing a complicated truth into three words. The effort was visible. It was also, Fasa thought, the effort of a man who meant exactly what he said and needed the room to hear it.

Vera uncrossed her arms and placed one hand on Pik's head. The boy did not look up, but his hand stilled on the stone chip. The gesture was territorial and tender in equal measure — a grandmother's hand claiming a grandchild for anyone who might be confused about the matter.

Fasa looked at the Leos and understood. They were a family whose shape did not match the categories the naming assumed. The naming asked for blood and marriage, for lineage and relation, for the architecture of kinship as the tradition understood it. The Leos had built their family outside that architecture, and the tools of declaration available to them forced their truth into forms it did not fit. Cade's hesitation was not concealment. It was the difficulty of answering a question whose grammar did not contain his answer.

"Thank you," she said. "All of you. The first naming is recorded."

She stood at the center and looked at the hall. Forty-three faces in morning light, the rain strengthening outside, the flood's voice rising from below. The children had already begun the slow gravitational drift that children always began — Melie Parra was showing Asha Mera her string braid, leaning close, the two girls' heads almost touching. Sem Dao was watching Fen Tesh with the frank assessment of a younger boy who had identified an older boy worth studying. Pik Leo had found a second stone chip and was clicking the two together in a quiet, private rhythm. Kiri Tesh had fallen asleep against Enn's arm.

The adults sat in their declarations. The words they had spoken hung in the hall like the smoke from the morning cooking fire — thinning, dispersing, but present, and Fasa could taste them. She had what she always had after a first naming: a map drawn in absence, its borders defined not by what the families had said but by what they had not said, and by the particular way each family had arranged its silence.

Jin Parra had given the minimum. His declaration had the shape of a man holding a door shut with both hands, and behind the door was something he could not afford to let into a room with his daughter in it. The dead wife might be true. Death at three months would still be raw, and rawness could explain the flatness, the refusal of detail, the jaw-lock lengthening around the questions he did not want. But flatness could also be the surface of a lie too important to perform — the face a man wore when the performance itself would crack.

Bett Dao had not spoken. In twenty years, Fasa had watched many people decline to speak at the naming. The shy covered their silence with gestures. The angry covered theirs with posture. The guilty could not keep their hands still. Bett's silence was none of these. It was a decision made long ago and maintained without effort, a border so established it had become terrain.

The Leos had spoken carefully — not falsely but insufficiently, forced by the naming's grammar into a version of their family that left out whatever made it real. Their discomfort was not the discomfort of people hiding something. It was the discomfort of people who had been asked to stand in a doorway built for a different body.

And Gitta Tesh had spoken perfectly. Her four words — he is my grandson — had arrived with the evenness of a heartbeat, no pause before or after, no emotion to read, no seam to find. The declaration was a surface so smooth that the eye moved past it to the rougher, more interesting textures on either side. Which, Fasa thought, might be exactly the point.

She did not know this yet. She suspected it the way the naming had taught her to suspect — not as accusation, not as judgment, but as attention held steady and pointed in one direction. The naming would return. It always did. The first declaration was a foundation, and what each family had built on that foundation this morning would either hold or begin, in the days and namings to come, to show its cracks.

The bell rang once — Olet at the doorway, signaling the end of the naming and the beginning of the morning meal. The families rose. The children went first, pulled by hunger and by the relief of movement after sitting still. The adults followed, sorting themselves back into their family units with the practiced choreography of people who had been keeping track, all through the ceremony, of exactly where their people were.

Jin Parra was the last to leave. He collected Melie from the cluster of children with a single word and a hand held out, and the girl took it and walked beside him toward the sleeping quarters without protest, swinging his hand once, the string braid dangling from her other fist. He did not look back.

Fasa stayed. The hall was empty now except for the lamps and the benches and the sound of the rain and, beneath the rain, the flood. She stood where she had stood and looked through the open wall at the brown water covering the roads — patient, opaque, indifferent to the declarations that had been made in its hearing. Seven families had told her who they were. Seven families had offered their names and their numbers and their reasons for being on the road, and the offering had cost most of them nothing more than a few minutes of mild discomfort.

The next naming would cost more. It always did. The first was foundation. The second was the first weight placed upon it. Fasa did not know yet where the cracks would appear, but she knew they would appear, because they always did, because no family's story was as solid as the stone it was spoken on, because the naming asked and waited and asked again, and eventually the asking found the place where the story could not hold.

She folded her cloth. She blew out the one lamp she had lit. She walked toward the kitchen, where the families were assembling for the morning meal, and the sound of the flood followed her, steady and patient, the voice of water that had nowhere else to be.


# Chapter 3

The bell woke Leni for the second morning in a row, and she hated it already.

It wasn't a gentle bell. Not a good-morning-honored-guests bell. It was a flat iron clang that one of the custodians' assistants struck from the prayer courtyard at what had to be an hour no reasonable person would voluntarily inhabit. The assistant was a young woman with close-cropped hair and forearms like she split wood for a living, which, Leni had learned, she did. She rang the bell with the same expression every time — not unkindness, not warmth, just the face of a person performing a task that existed before she was born and would continue after she was dead.

Leni pulled her knees to her chest on the sleeping mat and pressed her face into the thin pillow, which smelled like stone dust and other people's hair, and listened to the compound wake up.

Forty-three people waking up sounds like this: baby Ada crying first, because Ada cried at everything, because Ada was one year old and that was her entire skill set. Then Nessa's quiet murmuring as she lifted Ada against her chest. Then the Kondo family — all twelve of them — rustling and murmuring in their corner like a single organism with too many limbs. One of the younger Kondo boys always coughed first thing, a dry bark that echoed off the stone walls and made someone else's child whine about being cold. Tomas turned over on his mat and bumped his knee against Leni's ankle, and she shoved his leg away without opening her eyes. The flood rose steadily beneath everything, that low permanent rush from the valley floor, like the hill itself was clearing its throat.

Bram was already sitting up. Leni could tell from the direction of his breathing — he breathed differently when he was upright, a teacher's posture reasserting itself even in the dark. He reached over Tomas and touched Leni's shoulder.

"Up," he said.

"Why."

"Because the bell rang."

"The bell's not for us. It's for people who want to pray."

"The bell is for everyone. Get up."

Leni opened her eyes. The sleeping quarters were dim — oil lamplight from the corridor, gray monsoon dawn barely reaching the high stone windows. Bodies in rows on mats, the intimate strangeness of sleeping near strangers. Across the partition, she could hear Deshi Mera telling Suna something in a voice that suggested Suna was doing it wrong, whatever it was. On the other side, one of the Kondo cousins was trying to fold his blanket while his mother corrected his corners.

She sat up, pulled her braids off her face, and bit the edge of her thumbnail before she caught herself doing it.

Morning prayers were in the courtyard. Olet led them, and they were not technically mandatory, but Olet noted attendance the way a substitute teacher notes who comes back late from the bathroom. The prayers themselves were in a language Leni didn't recognize — something older than the common tongue, with a cadence that rose and fell like the rain itself. Fasa stood beside Olet with her hands folded and her face arranged in an expression Leni couldn't quite decode. Not piety. Something closer to workmanship — a carpenter checking the joints.

Peyam Mera attended every prayer. He stood at the back, small and precise in his movements, his lips moving along with words he seemed to already know, though Leni couldn't figure out how. Maybe all old prayers sound the same if you've been praying long enough. He wore a thread around his left wrist that he turned with his thumb when he was concentrating — a constant slow rotation, as if the thread were a dial measuring something only he could read. His beard was white and carefully trimmed, and he carried himself with the quiet seriousness of a man who had been talking to god longer than most people in the room had been alive.

Jin Parra attended too. Not praying — just standing at the edge with his head slightly bowed, his hands at his sides, looking like a man who had nowhere else to be at that hour and preferred the courtyard to his mat. His daughter Melie slept through prayers, and Leni thought that was probably the point. The hour Jin spent in the courtyard was the hour Melie didn't need him.

Venn Kondo did not attend. Tila went. Arash went once, on Day 3, then didn't come again. The Leos didn't attend. Bram went to the first one and told Nessa afterward that the prayers were "interesting from an anthropological perspective," which was Bram-speak for he wasn't going back.

After prayers, water. Each family sent someone to the cistern with their containers, and the cistern was fed by a spring, so there was enough, but the path to it was narrow stone steps slicked by rain, and the Kondo family sent three people every morning because twelve mouths need more water than four, which meant everyone else waited. Venn Kondo didn't carry water himself. He delegated. Tila organized which of the younger Kondo children would go, speaking in a low voice that carried the earned authority of a woman who runs a household of twelve and has been doing it so long the children obey before they're fully awake. Her hair was always in a tight bun and her sandals were the most practical shoes at the shrine — flat, rubber-soled, the kind you buy for function and never replace because they never wear out. She noticed things. When the stone steps were especially slippery, she sent the older children first and had them pass the filled jars up hand to hand. Nobody asked her to organize this. She just did it, the same way she just did everything that kept the Kondos fed and clothed and accounted for.

By the middle of Day 3, Leni had identified the compound's real hierarchy, and it had nothing to do with who shouted the loudest.

Then breakfast. And this was where it got interesting.

The custodians had divided the cooking rotation into pairs — two families per day, working in the outdoor kitchen under the stone overhang. Day 3 was the Meras and the Okoyes. Suna Mera was at the kitchen fire before Leni had finished lacing her shoes, already grinding spices with a stone pestle she'd found in a nook beside the hearth stones. Nessa arrived with Ada on her hip and tried to help, but Nessa's cooking was the kind that got the job done without anyone being moved to comment on it, and Suna was already ahead of her — rice soaking, flatbread dough being kneaded on a flat stone, a pot of dal that she'd started before the bell.

Deshi supervised.

That was the word Leni's father would use. Supervised. What Deshi actually did was stand three feet behind Suna with her arms crossed and deliver judgments that sounded like questions.

"Is that how much salt you're using?"

Suna didn't look up. "Yes."

"For this many people."

"Yes, Deshi."
