"Your mother, god rest her, always said salt is cheaper than complaint. But then she was cooking for four."

Deshi Mera was sixty-seven years old and made of angles — sharp jaw, sharp elbows, sharp opinions that she dispensed with the calm certainty of someone who had been right about everything since before Leni was born. Her sari was immaculate even on the third day of sleeping on stone floors, and Leni had no idea how she managed that. She wore her judgment in her posture, spine straight, chin up, a woman who assessed a room by its domestic failures and found the evidence damning.

Suna added more salt. She did it without comment, without expression, and Leni recognized the technique — it was the same thing Leni did when her mother asked her to clean her room. You do the thing. You don't give them the argument. The argument is what they want.

"Your mother's helping," Suna said to Leni as Nessa tried to figure out how the stacking pots worked. It wasn't a request. It was a we're-all-in-this-together statement that also meant please keep my mother-in-law from talking to me for three minutes.

Leni peeled onions. Her eyes watered and she sat on an upturned crate near the edge of the kitchen overhang, where the rain came down in a curtain two feet from her knees. The stone beneath her was warm from yesterday's afternoon sun, not yet cooled by the morning. She peeled and watched.

Jin Parra was hauling water. He'd volunteered for the labor rotation before anyone asked — he was like that, the kind of quiet that looked like politeness but was actually a wall built to keep everyone at arm's length. He carried the heavy stone jars from the cistern to the kitchen without talking, his shoulders hunched forward in a way that looked permanent, like he'd been carrying something heavier than water for a long time. His daughter Melie trailed him at a distance, not helping but not wanting to lose sight of him, and when he put the jar down she came and leaned against his leg for a moment before drifting back toward the courtyard.

The Tesh family was assigned to latrine cleaning. Miro Tesh did it without complaint — a broad-shouldered, patient man who moved through unpleasant tasks with the resignation of someone who has accepted that life is mostly maintenance. His wife Enn stood beside him with her sleeves rolled up and a look on her face that said she'd done worse. Their son Fen — the older teenager, taller than Leni, with dark hair that fell into his eyes — carried the water buckets with a sixteen-year-old's grudging compliance who has been told this builds character. He didn't look like his parents. Leni noticed this without thinking about it, the way you notice that a puzzle piece is from a different box — you register the mismatch and move on.

Fen passed the kitchen and caught Leni looking, and she looked away so fast she nicked her thumb with the peeling knife.

It bled. She stuck her thumb in her mouth and tasted iron and onion.

"Careful," Nessa said, appearing beside her. "Let me see."

"It's fine, Mom."

"Let me see it."

Leni held up her thumb. It was a small cut, already slowing. Nessa looked at it with the focused worry she brought to any injury on any of her children, an attention that suggested a paper cut might be the beginning of gangrene. Nessa was forty-one and carried baby Ada against her chest like a shield, and her face showed fear more readily than she wanted it to. She had freckles across her nose that darkened in humid weather, and she kept touching her hair as if checking it was still there.

"Keep it clean," Nessa said. "We don't know what's on these surfaces."

"It's an onion, Mom. Not a biohazard."

"Leni."

"Keeping it clean. Got it."

Nessa drifted back to the stacking pots, and Leni watched her go. Her mother was scared. Not of onion knives. Of something she wasn't saying out loud, something that lived in the glances she exchanged with Bram when they thought the children weren't looking.

Breakfast was rice and dal and flatbread, served on shared plates the custodians provided, and forty-three people ate in the main hall in groups that were already sorting themselves. The Kondos took up one entire corner, their twelve bodies forming a wall of family. Venn ate at the center of them, not first but definitely served first — Tila passed him his plate and he accepted it as natural order. The Meras sat near the kitchen end, Deshi commenting on how the rice had turned out with the precise air of someone offering constructive feedback rather than criticism. The Daos kept to themselves — Hala, Bett's older sister, was a sturdy woman with a face that broadcast emotion whether she wanted it to or not, and she sat between Bett and the rest of the room like a physical barrier. Bett Dao ate without looking up, every movement precise and economic, a woman who'd learned to take up no more space than necessary.

The Leos were the quietest family. Cade and Wynn sat with Pik between them, a seven-year-old with bright curious eyes who kicked his feet against the stone floor while he ate. Cade's mother Vera — sixty-one, heavyset, with reading glasses she wore on a chain around her neck even though Leni hadn't seen her read anything — sat slightly apart from the two men, maintaining a positioning that was either habit or strategy. Leni hadn't understood the Leo family at first. Then she had, and felt stupid for not getting it sooner, and then felt annoyed at the naming for making their arrangement into something that required public explanation. Cade had stumbled at the naming ceremony, trying to describe his family in a framework designed for a different shape of family. Nothing about two men and a child and a grandmother needed explaining, and everything about it had been made to need explaining.

After breakfast the custodians assigned labor. This was Olet's domain — he appeared at the end of the meal with a list that must have existed only in his head, because he never wrote anything down, and distributed the day's tasks with the same flat calm he brought to everything. He was in his sixties, lean, and he moved through the compound the way the shrine's stones sat — without hurry, without agitation, as if he had been here before any of them and would be here after all of them left.

"The Parras and the Teshes will take the terraced garden this morning," Olet said. "The weeds have grown through the herb rows. The Daos will clean the main hall. The Okoyes and the Leos will gather firewood from the north slope — the fallen branches are dry enough."

"The Kondos?" Venn asked from his corner.

"The Kondos took the latrine yesterday, and the Meras cooked. They may rest this morning."

Venn nodded. He accepted rest as his family's due, the same way he accepted authority — as something that naturally flowed toward the Kondos because they were the Kondos. But Arash, his son, stood up anyway. Twenty-seven, broad-shouldered like his father but leaner, with a face that showed things Venn's face had learned to hide. "I'll help with the garden," Arash said.

Venn looked at him.

"I'd rather move than sit," Arash said. He said it to the room, not to his father, and Leni noticed the careful avoidance — the way you address a group when you don't want to address the person who'll object.

Bram leaned over to Leni. "Ready for firewood?"

"Sure. Is there anything more thrilling than firewood?"

"On a hilltop shrine in a monsoon? Probably not."

Her father smiled, and Leni felt the small warmth of it. Bram Okoye was a tall man made of angles and arguments, and he wore glasses that he pushed up his nose when he was building a point. His hands moved when he talked, which was always. But when he smiled, it reached his eyes before it reached his mouth, and it was the part of him that didn't feel like a teacher.

They gathered firewood on the north slope — Leni, Bram, Cade Leo, and Wynn Leo. The slope was steep and wet, and the fallen branches were heavy with rain. Cade moved carefully, testing each foothold before committing his weight, and Wynn walked ahead with the confidence of someone who didn't worry about falling. They worked in near-silence. Leni noticed that Cade and Wynn communicated in small touches — a hand on the shoulder to indicate a branch worth collecting, a tap on the forearm when the slope turned slippery. They had their own language, built from years of reading each other's bodies, and it was the opposite of how the naming wanted families to speak: no declarations, no formal categories, just a hand finding the right shoulder at the right time.

From the slope, Leni could see down to the terraced garden where Jin and Miro Tesh worked side by side, pulling weeds in the rain. Arash had joined them. Jin worked steadily, bending and pulling without pause, steady, punishing, labor chosen because the alternative was sitting with his own thoughts. Miro matched his pace. Arash was faster but less systematic — pulling weeds in clumps, leaving roots behind. Enn Tesh knelt at the far end of the terrace, replanting herb seedlings that the rain had displaced, her fingers pressing the soil around each stem with the care of someone who understood that food grown here meant food that didn't come from the custodians' stores.

By noon, the shrine smelled of wet wood and cooking smoke and forty-three bodies that hadn't had a proper bath in three days. The rain had paused, which didn't mean it was over — the clouds sat low and fat over the valley like a held breath. From the main hall's open side, the flood was visible below: brown water covering the valley floor, the switchback road drowned past the third bend. The roads that had brought them here were gone. All seven of them, vanished under silted water.

Leni looked at that water and thought: we're not going anywhere.

And it occurred to her — looking at who had cooked, who had hauled water, who had cleaned, who had pulled weeds — that the compound was already being run by the people who were useful, not the people who were loud. Suna's cooking gave her a say in how meals worked that Deshi's criticism couldn't touch. Jin's labor earned him a nod from Olet that Venn's resting did not. Tila organized the Kondos with more practical authority than Venn's pronouncements. And Enn Tesh, replanting herbs nobody asked her to replant, had just done more for the collective food supply than anyone who'd spent the morning asserting their rights.

Venn still spoke for the Kondos. Bram still framed arguments that nobody could answer. But the shrine didn't run on speeches. It ran on hands.

---

The children figured it out before the adults did.

Leni watched it happen from the courtyard steps, where she sat with her journal balanced on her knees, because the courtyard was the only place where she could be alone without being alone — people passed through, but nobody stopped to supervise her.

It started with Asha. Suna's youngest was four, and four-year-olds don't have politics. Asha had spent the morning following Suna around the kitchen, getting underfoot, being redirected, and finally being deposited in the courtyard with a firmness that suggested Suna's patience had a floor. Asha sat on the warm stone for exactly thirty seconds before she walked over to where Pik Leo was playing with a pile of smooth river stones he'd found near the cistern.

Pik looked up. Asha sat down next to him. They started stacking stones without discussing it.

Then Melie Parra drifted over. Melie was six, dark-haired, watchful — she had her father's way of observing before she engaged, but she was six, so the observation took about three seconds before she decided these children were acceptable and sat down to help build the stone tower.

Nori Dao came next — Bett's daughter, seven, with the same economy of movement Leni had noticed in Bett, everything purposeful, nothing wasted. She didn't ask to join. She brought a stone and placed it on top of the tower and that was that.

Kael Mera, eight, arrived running from the direction of the sleeping quarters, chased by Kiri Tesh, also eight, both of them making a noise that suggested they had decided a stone courtyard was a racetrack. They nearly knocked over the tower. Asha said something sharp — Leni couldn't hear the words, but the tone was pure Deshi, three generations distilled into a four-year-old's voice — and Kael stopped, looked, and sat down.

By the middle of the afternoon, the courtyard held a cluster of children that made no sense by the shrine's family map. Asha Mera, Melie Parra, Pik Leo, Nori Dao, Kael Mera, Kiri Tesh, Ori Kondo, two of the younger Kondo cousins, and Tomas — Leni's own brother, who had abandoned her to join the group with a ten-year-old's confidence that playing was more important than family loyalty.

The stone tower had evolved into a game Leni didn't recognize — partly building, partly tag, partly an elaborate system of rules that the children were inventing in real time and revising by shouting. Pik was the most inventive — he kept proposing modifications that the others either adopted or ignored, and he seemed equally happy either way, his face lit up — a child who had discovered other children after being, Leni guessed, often the only child in whatever room he was in. Ori Kondo was the enforcer — she had inherited her uncle Venn's sense of order, if not his rigidity, and she settled disputes with a firmness that made the younger children comply without resentment. Tomas, who at home would never have voluntarily spent time with anyone under the age of nine, was teaching Kiri a clapping game that involved increasingly complicated hand patterns and both of them failing and laughing.

Rimi Mera, twelve, sat at the edge of the group, old enough to feel the pull of both sides. She braided Ori's hair while Ori argued with Tomas about whose turn it was, and her fingers moved with the automatic skill of a girl who'd been braiding hair since she was old enough to reach. Raya Dao, thirteen, leaned against the courtyard wall with her arms crossed, watching the younger children with an expression that said she was absolutely not playing, she was just standing here, it had nothing to do with the game. Sem Dao, eleven, had no such dignity — he was in the middle of it, building and arguing and laughing, a lanky boy with a laugh that started in his stomach and didn't stop until someone pushed him over, which Kael did twice.

Leni watched. She was too old for this. Too old by exactly the right amount to make it painful, because part of her wanted to sit down and stack stones with Asha, and the rest of her knew that wanting that was itself the proof she was past it.

She bit her thumbnail and wrote in her journal: *The kids don't care whose kids they are.*

Fen Tesh was on the far side of the courtyard, sitting on the low wall that separated the prayer space from the sleeping quarters. He had a stick and was scraping patterns into the moss between the stones — not watching the children, not ignoring them, occupying the same in-between space Leni was in. The other teenager. She thought about going over and saying something, but what would she say? Nice shrine. Great flood. Love what they've done with the mandatory prayers. She stayed where she was.

The parents cared. Suna came to the courtyard door and stood there for a long moment, her eyes tracking Asha, Kael, Rimi — counting her children, placing them in the geography of the group. Her face was calm but her hands were doing that thing Leni had noticed, that restless organizational gesture, fingers working against each other as if she were sorting invisible tasks. Asha was sitting in Ori Kondo's lap while Ori braided a piece of string into something. Suna watched this and then turned back to the kitchen without saying anything, but Leni could see the effort of it — the deliberate decision not to call Asha back, not to draw a line, because drawing a line would require explaining where the line was and why.

Nessa appeared later with Ada fussing on her hip. She found Tomas in the scrum and relaxed visibly — Leni could see the tension leave her mother's shoulders when she confirmed all three of her older children were accounted for. Baby Ada reached toward the group with both fists and made a sound of longing that no one over the age of one could have produced without embarrassment. Nessa bounced her and retreated.

Jin Parra came to the courtyard and leaned against the wall, watching Melie play. He stood exactly far enough away that she wouldn't notice him watching, and his face held an expression Leni associated with people trying to memorize something. He rubbed the side of his jaw — that thing he did, the brief lock before his mouth opened, like speech was a door that stuck. After a few minutes, Melie saw him and waved, and he waved back, and she turned back to the game. He stayed.

Venn Kondo sent Tila to collect the Kondo children for afternoon rest, and Tila came to the courtyard with the authority of her voice alone. "Ori. The others. Come rest." The Kondo cousins peeled off from the group without argument, but Ori looked up from the string she was braiding and said, "Five more minutes."

Tila waited.

"Please."

Tila gave her five minutes. Leni timed it in her head. At four minutes and thirty seconds, Ori kissed the top of Asha's head — an odd gesture, casual and intimate, from one child to another child she'd known for three days — and followed Tila back inside.

The play group shrank but didn't dissolve. It kept reforming throughout the afternoon in different configurations, like water finding its level. The children moved between families without asking permission. They shared food, shared space, shared the game whose rules mutated hourly. By late afternoon, Melie was asleep with her head on Nori's stomach in the prayer courtyard, both of them curled on the warm stone like animals who'd chosen each other's warmth by instinct, and Kiri Tesh was trying to teach Tomas a song in a melody Leni didn't recognize — something pentatonic and haunting that sounded like it came from somewhere far away.

None of this was organized. None of it was sanctioned. The adults saw it happening and didn't stop it, because stopping it would require explaining why, and none of them had an explanation that didn't sound insane or cruel. *You can't play with her because she's a Kondo. You can't sit with him because he's a Parra.* Even Venn, who had the most rigid sense of family boundary, didn't say it outright. He sent Tila to enforce rest time and hoped proximity would create the distance he couldn't bring himself to name.

It didn't.

---

The radio was in the custodians' quarters, behind a heavy wooden door at the end of the covered walkway. The families weren't supposed to go there, and nobody had said they couldn't listen, and these were different things. Leni had figured this out on Day 2.

What she hadn't figured out — what came together on the evening of Day 3 — was that the news was bad.

The evening gathering was the custodians' final scheduled event. After the dinner meal, the families collected in the main hall for what Fasa called "reflection" and what Leni called sitting around while adults talked about nothing useful. Oil lamps threw their warm unsteady light across the stone walls. Moths the size of Leni's thumb circled the flames, casting shadows that pulsed and shrank. The evening air was heavy, saturated with the smell of rain and wet earth and the woodsmoke that permeated everything — hair, clothes, skin. By Day 3, everyone at the shrine smelled the same. Like the shrine.

But before the gathering settled, before the talk turned to the next day's assignments and Olet's calm directives, something unexpected happened.

Peyam Mera produced a deck of cards.

Leni didn't know where the cards came from. They were old, soft-cornered, played with for decades until the faces were blurred and the suits were guesswork. Peyam had been the quietest person at the shrine for two days — quieter even than Jin, and Jin had turned silence into a vocation. Peyam was small and deliberate in his movements, with his carefully trimmed white beard and his thread bracelet and his genuine absorption in Olet's prayers. He prayed the way other people breathe — without performance, without self-consciousness, as if the conversation with god were the most ordinary thing in his day.

But he was also, it turned out, a card player.

"You play?" he said to Mava Kondo. He said it across the room, his voice carrying in the way of old men who are used to speaking over distance and don't adjust for proximity, and Mava looked up from the corner where she'd been sitting with her knees under a blanket and her left hand rubbing her ear in that slow circle Leni had already come to associate with her.

"I play," Mava said. Seventy-three, white-haired, frail enough that the wind through the open side of the hall seemed to push her around. But her eyes, when she said it, were sharp as glass.

They sat cross-legged on Peyam's mat, the cards between them, and Peyam dealt. The game was one Leni didn't recognize — a regional variation, something with trumps and tricks and a complicated bidding system that involved tapping the mat with different knuckles.

The first hand went quickly. Mava won. Peyam studied his remaining cards and said nothing.

The second hand went slower. Peyam bid high. Mava matched him. When the tricks were played out, Peyam turned over his final card with a small flourish and smiled — the first smile Leni had seen from him that wasn't directed at god.

Then Mava turned over hers.

"That's not a legal play," Peyam said.

"It is if you're playing hill rules."

"There are no hill rules."

"There are hill rules. My mother taught me, and her mother taught her, and I've been playing hill rules since before your beard came in."

"You can't trump a trump with a lesser suit. It's not done."

"It's done in the hills. You're playing lowland rules, and lowland rules are for people who don't know how to play."

Peyam straightened. His beard bristled. It was, Leni realized, the most animated he'd been since arriving at the shrine — more alive in this argument about card rules than he'd been about the flood, the naming, or the sleeping arrangements. He pulled the disputed card from Mava's hand, examined it, held it to the lamplight as if checking for forgery, and set it down with a firmness that suggested he was also setting down a point of principle.

"This card," he said, "is a seven."

"I know what it is."

"A seven doesn't beat a twelve."

"It does if the seven's suited and the twelve is blind."

"There's no such thing as a blind twelve."

"Then what do you call a twelve played without a bid?"

"I call it a twelve."

"And that's why you lose."

A laugh escaped from somewhere — Sem Dao, who was watching from behind Hala's shoulder. Then Rimi Mera giggled, and Bram's low chuckle followed, and suddenly the room was paying attention, and the argument between two people with a combined age of a hundred and forty-five had become the first thing at the shrine that everyone was watching because they wanted to, not because Olet told them to.

Peyam reshuffled. "We play again," he said. "Properly."

"Properly means hill rules."

"Properly means rules. One set. Agreed upon before the first card is dealt."

Mava pulled the blanket tighter around her shoulders. "Fine. But I deal."

"Why?"

"Because you shuffle like a man who learned from a book."

The laughter was real this time — wide and sudden, the kind that breaks loose before you can decide whether it's appropriate. Venn snorted from his corner. Deshi covered her mouth but her shoulders shook. Hala's face split into a grin that transformed her entirely — for a moment she looked like someone who wasn't keeping watch over anything, who wasn't positioned between her sister and the rest of the world. Even Bett Dao's guarded face shifted, a small upward pull at the corner of her mouth, there and gone. Jin leaned against the wall and something in his shoulders loosened — not a laugh, not quite, but the absence of the tension he carried like a second skin.

They played four more hands. Mava dealt. The rules were negotiated card by card, with arguments that kept circling back to fundamental disagreements about what constituted a legal play, and whether the hill rules were real rules or an elaborate justification for cheating, and whether Peyam's lowland rules were any better given that he was losing by three hands and his beard was doing the bristling thing again.

Gitta Tesh watched from her place near the Tesh family's mat, small and still, her hands folded in her lap. When Mava won the seventh hand with a play Peyam couldn't even identify, Gitta's mouth curved into something private and almost fond — the expression of a woman who had once played cards with people she no longer saw.

Leni found herself smiling. She was still smiling when she noticed Fen Tesh watching the game from across the hall, his dark hair in his eyes, his mouth doing the same thing hers was doing — something that wanted to be laughter but had been out of practice. Their eyes met for a half-second. They both looked away.

For ten minutes nobody thought about the flood.

Then Olet spoke.

"The news today," he said, and the room quieted in the way rooms quiet when they have learned that this voice means the recreational portion of the evening is over. Olet stood by the main hall's open side, the valley behind him invisible in the dark but audible — that steady rush of water that never stopped. The oil lamp nearest him lit the lower half of his face and left his eyes in shadow.

"The radio received a broadcast from the eastern station this afternoon. Checkpoints have been established on the main highway east of the pass. The lowland curfew has been extended from dusk to dawn. There are reports of —" He paused. It was a brief pause, no longer than a breath, but Olet did not pause involuntarily, and Leni noticed. "There are reports of unrest in the capital district."

Bram leaned forward. "What kind of unrest?"

"The broadcast did not specify."

"Were there reports of violence?"

Olet regarded him. "The broadcast used the word 'disturbances.' We do not know what was meant."

"Disturbances can mean anything from a protest to —"

"I have told you what the broadcast said."

"Are they sending road crews? Is anyone clearing the route?"

"There was no mention of road crews."

The silence that followed had weight. Leni could feel it pressing against the walls, against the high stone ceiling, against her own ribs. She looked at her father. Bram's jaw was tight, and his hands, which were usually in motion, had gone still on his knees. Nessa had baby Ada pressed against her shoulder, and her other hand was on Tomas's back, touching him the way you touch something you're afraid of losing.

Venn spoke from his corner. "How long until the road is passable?"

"The water has not receded," Olet said.

"It's been three days."

"The water has not receded."

"Seasonal flooding on this road takes four to five days to drain. I've traveled this route before."

"Under normal conditions, yes. The conditions are not normal. The upstream infrastructure has been damaged. The drainage systems are not functioning."

Venn's voice hardened. "So what are you telling us?"

"I am telling you what the radio told us. The checkpoints, the curfew, the reports of disturbance. The water has not receded. That is what we know."

Leni looked around the room. The laughter from the card game had burned off like mist, as if it had never happened. Suna sat with Asha in her lap, her face doing that thing — that calm, competent, slightly too-still thing — that Leni had started to recognize as the face of someone managing a disaster she couldn't talk about. Hala had pulled Raya and Sem closer to her, a physical gathering that was also a reflex, the body's response to threat before the mind has named it. Cade Leo put his arm around Pik's shoulder. Wynn didn't move, but his hand found Cade's knee and stayed there.

Bram spoke again, and his voice was the careful voice he used when he was constructing an argument and needed every piece to land. "The political situation. The identity checks, the movement permits. That's what's keeping the road crews away. Not just the water."

"That is a reasonable interpretation," Olet said.

"So even if the water dropped tomorrow —"

"Even if the water dropped tomorrow, the road leads to a checkpoint. Whether you would be permitted to pass is not something we can know from here."

Bram sat back. He looked at Nessa. Their eyes met and held, and Leni saw something pass between her parents that she was not supposed to see — a conversation conducted entirely in the muscles of the face, the kind of communication that only happens between people who have been afraid together before and have learned to be afraid without speaking.

The gathering broke up slowly after that. Families retreated to their sleeping areas with the heavy quiet of people who have heard bad news and haven't figured out what to do about it. Peyam folded his cards and tucked them inside his vest with the tenderness of a man putting away something precious. Mava pulled her blanket around her shoulders and let Tila guide her back to the Kondo corner, her frail body listing slightly, the sharpness from the card game already draining from her face. The moth shadows danced on the walls as lamps were carried to sleeping quarters, each flame pulling its bubble of warm light along the stone corridors.

Leni lay on her mat in the dark. Tomas was already asleep beside her — he was ten, he could sleep through anything, his body still trusted the adults to handle whatever was happening. Ada was asleep on Nessa's chest, milk-drunk and limp. Bram and Nessa were lying down but not sleeping. Leni could hear them whispering.

She couldn't hear the words. She could hear the register — low, tight, whispering that wasn't a choice but a necessity, two people being afraid in the smallest possible voice. She caught fragments. "Checkpoints." "Papers." Her mother said something that ended on a rising sound — a question, or the word *how*, or a name Leni couldn't place. Bram whispered back. More fragments. "...morning..." and "...doesn't matter what we..." and then Nessa's hand found Bram's in the lamplight from the corridor — Leni saw it, her father's long fingers closing around her mother's smaller hand, and the grip was not comfort. It was ballast. Two people holding each other because the ground was moving and there was nothing else to hold.

Leni turned away from them. She reached into her bag and pulled out her journal and the pen she'd brought from home, from the school desk in her bedroom that she was starting to think about the way you think about a place you've already left. The pen was a cheap ballpoint, blue ink, almost out.

She wrote by the corridor's faint lamplight, her handwriting cramped and leaning hard to the right the way it always did when she was writing in the dark or in a hurry or both. She wrote about the day. The cooking rotation and Deshi's salt commentary. The children's game that made no sense and didn't need to. Ori kissing Asha's head. Fen scraping moss off the wall with a stick. Her mother's freckles darkening in the humidity. Peyam and Mava arguing about hill rules while the flood erased the road below them and neither of them cared, because for ten minutes the game was the only real thing.

She wrote: *Nobody's coming to get us. Dad knows. Mom knows. They're just not saying it out loud because once you say it out loud you have to do something about it and there's nothing to do.*

She wrote: *Tomorrow the bell's going to ring again and we're going to get up and carry water and peel onions and the kids are going to play like nothing's wrong because nothing is wrong for them. They don't know the road goes to a checkpoint. They don't know what checkpoints mean.*

She didn't write: *I'm scared.* She didn't need to. The handwriting said it — the hard lean of the letters, the pressure of the pen against the page, the way the words came fast and then stopped.

She closed the journal and put it under her pillow and lay there listening. The flood. The rain starting up again, soft at first and then heavier, drumming on the stone overhead. Ada's small sleeping breaths. A man coughing in the Kondo section. The insects, that constant monsoon hum from the wet vegetation around the shrine, a sound so persistent you stopped hearing it until the night got quiet enough to bring it back.

Forty-three people on a hilltop. Three days in. No road, no phone, no help on the way. The custodians' bell would ring again in the morning, and they would all get up, and they would cook and carry water and clean and eat, and the children would play across family lines as if the lines didn't exist, and the adults would watch them and wonder whether that was a problem or a consolation or both.

Leni bit her thumbnail in the dark. She could taste stone dust and onion and the faint metallic residue of the pen she'd been chewing without noticing.

Day 4 would start the same way. Then Day 5. Then however many days until the water went down or the checkpoints opened or someone, somewhere, figured out they were up here.

She didn't think anyone was figuring out they were up here.


# Chapter 4

The fifth morning at the shrine came in the same way as the four before it: Asha's mat damp with sweat, the cooking fire already lit somewhere beyond the partition, and the particular hush that meant the custodians' bell had not yet rung. Suna lay still for a moment, cataloguing. Asha breathing against her side, the small furnace heat of a child in deep sleep. Kael in the corner of the mat, curled tight, one ankle hooked over the other. Rimi asleep with her forearm across her eyes — twelve and already perfecting the art of blocking things out. Deshi's mat empty, which meant she was up, which meant criticism of the morning meal was already being prepared alongside the rice.

Peyam was on his mat by the far wall, still asleep or pretending, his thin chest rising under the worn cotton of his shirt. His devotional beads were wound around his left wrist even in sleep. He was seventy-two and had not missed morning prayers since arriving at the shrine — up before the bell every day, crossing the courtyard to his corner of the prayer yard while the stones were still damp with the night's rain. He didn't ask Suna's permission or tell her his schedule. He simply went. Of all the Mera family members she was managing alone, Peyam was the only one who required nothing from her.

She eased Asha off her shoulder and stood. The mat needed hanging again. She rolled it, tucked it under her arm, and crossed to the courtyard. The air outside the sleeping quarters was cooler but not cold — monsoon humidity, the stones still holding the night's damp. The sound of the flood was there as always, a low massed rush from the valley below that she had stopped noticing by the second day and now only heard again when she listened for it. Like living next to a river, except the river was the road, and the road was gone. She hung the mat over the low courtyard wall and pressed it flat. The stone beneath her hands was already warming.

Breakfast was communal now, the families rotating through kitchen duty on the custodians' schedule. This morning was the Kondos' turn, and when Suna reached the cooking area under the stone overhang, Tila Kondo was already portioning rice into bowls while two of the younger Kondo cousins carried them to the main hall. The smoke from the cooking fire drifted low under the overhang, the particular thick sweetness of monsoon-damp wood that got into clothes and hair and stayed. By the fifth day everyone at the shrine smelled of it — smoke and damp cotton and the mineral flatness of cistern water.

Deshi was there too, seated on the bench near the wall with the blue shawl she'd brought from home pulled around her shoulders, watching the Kondos work with the alert stillness of a woman conducting an inspection she hadn't been asked to perform. The shawl was frayed at both edges, pilling along the fold where she clutched it closed, and she had refused Suna's offer to wash it because it would lose what was left of its shape. It was the only thing Deshi had kept close through the whole journey — not sentiment, Suna thought, just the particular stubbornness of a woman who would not be separated from what was hers.

"The lentils are thin," Deshi said as Suna passed.

"They're fine."

"They're thin. More water than dal. You could read a letter through them."

"Then don't eat them."

Deshi made a sound that was not quite agreement and not quite objection — the sound of a woman who had won the exchange by provoking a response. Suna let it go and went to collect her children for breakfast.

The main hall was already filling. Forty-three people eating in shifts of twenty and twenty-three, the arrangement worked out by the custodians on the second day and adhered to since without further argument. The Mera family ate in the first shift with the Kondos and the Leos. Suna gathered Kael and Rimi — Asha was still half-asleep on Rimi's hip, her head tipped against her sister's shoulder, mouth slack — and found their spot on the long bench. The stone floor of the main hall was cool underfoot, not yet reached by the morning sun. The open side of the hall faced the valley, and the light that came through it was flat and gray, filtered through the cloud cover that hadn't fully broken in five days.

Suna had told the children their father had gone ahead. He was waiting for them at the end of the road, at their destination, and they would meet him when the water went down and the route opened again. Rimi had stopped asking about it on the third day. Kael had never asked. Asha was too young for the question. The lie sat in Suna like a stone she'd swallowed — not painful exactly, just present, the constant low-grade weight of something that would not dissolve and could not be passed. She ate her rice and did not look at her children while she ate it.

Across the hall, the Kondo family occupied their usual half of the second bench, their twelve members arranged with the unconscious precision of a family that had been traveling together and knew its own geometry. Venn sat at the end, upright, eating with the deliberate pace of a man who considered meals a public act. Beside him, at the far end of the bench where the draft from the valley came up cool and steady, Mava Kondo sat with a bowl of dal in her lap, her left hand rising periodically to rub her ear — palm flat against the cartilage, slow circles, a motion as habitual and unconscious as breathing. Suna had noticed the gesture on the first day and filed it the way she filed most things about other families: noted, categorized, set aside.

Asha ate slowly, picking rice grains out of her bowl with her fingers and dropping half of them on Rimi's thigh. She was four and had no interest in efficiency. Her attention moved across the hall, watching faces, watching hands, her gaze traveling from person to person with a four-year-old's undiscriminating appetite for the visible world. She touched everything — the bench, the bowl, Rimi's ear, the stone wall behind them — her hands in constant exploratory motion, absorbing textures like a second kind of sight.

She reached up and rubbed her left ear.

It was the gesture that caught Suna, not the fact of it. Children touched their ears constantly — Asha especially, who touched everything. But this was not touching. It was rubbing. Palm flat against the cartilage, slow circles, the fingers slightly spread. The same motion. The same hand. The same tempo. Suna looked across the hall at Mava, who was still rubbing her own ear on the far bench, and then back at Asha, who had stopped and was picking at her rice again, her attention already elsewhere.

It meant nothing. Children copied gestures the way they copied words — indiscriminately, temporarily. Asha had watched Mava do it across the hall and her body had reproduced it, the way she had reproduced Rimi's way of sitting cross-legged and Kael's habit of blowing on food that wasn't hot. That was what children did. Their bodies were porous, absorptive, borrowing from every adult and older child in range. It was ordinary. Suna ate her rice and told Kael to stop kicking the bench leg.

The play group formed after breakfast, as it had for the past two days — the shrine's younger children sorting themselves by affinity rather than family. Kael went immediately to the Kondo boys, who had invented a game involving pebbles and a crack in the courtyard stone that seemed to function as both goal and boundary. Rimi drifted toward the older girls — Raya Dao, a few of the Kondo cousins — not quite joining their circle, not quite apart, the careful geometry of a twelve-year-old managing proximity. Asha was with the younger ones: Nori Dao, Pik Leo, Ori Kondo. They had claimed the corner of the main hall near the open side where the light came in and the stone floor was warm with reflected sun, and they moved among each other with the uncomplicated physicality of children who had not yet learned to maintain the distances their parents kept.

Suna was sorting laundry — hers and Deshi's and the children's, the limited clothes they'd arrived with, washed and rewashed in the cistern until the fabric was thinning at the seams. She could see Asha from where she sat against the wall, the bright head moving among the other children, and she registered each position the way she registered everything about her children: automatically, continuously, the way you track weather or the level of water in a pot. Asha by the wall. Asha near the open side, holding Nori's hand. Asha standing next to Ori, reaching for something in Ori's pocket. Asha near Mava.

Mava was sitting in her usual spot — the section of bench near the open side where the draft came up from the valley. She had a small cloth bag in her lap, and she was sorting dried lentils with her fingers, picking out the small pale stones that hid among the grains and setting them aside in a neat row on the bench. She did this every morning after breakfast. It was her contribution to the kitchen — a task that required no standing, no lifting, no argument with Venn about whether she was overexerting herself — and she performed it with the patient sureness of someone who had been sorting lentils for longer than Suna had been alive, her fingers reading the difference between grain and stone by weight and texture alone. The stones she removed were small and smooth, washed to the same color as the lentils by decades of monsoon water, and she found them without looking.

Asha was standing near her, watching the old woman's fingers move through the grain. Mava gave her the mild automatic smile of a woman who had been around children her whole life and neither resented nor sought out their attention. Asha pointed at her and said something Suna couldn't hear across the hall.

Then she said it again, louder, turning toward Suna. "Nushi."

"What?" Suna looked up from the damp shirt in her hands.

"Nushi." Asha pointed at Mava again, then turned back to the old woman and the lentils. It was not a question. It was identification — the flat confidence of a child naming what she saw, the way she said bird or cup or Mama.

Suna did not know the word. It was not from any language she spoke, and it was not a word she had heard at the shrine — not from the custodians' ritual vocabulary, not from any of the other families' speech. She assumed it was something Asha had picked up from the Kondo children — a family nickname, a pet word, something in the private language that large families built among themselves. Ori or one of the cousins had called Mava something and Asha had absorbed it, the way she absorbed everything, her body and her mouth open to whatever the world put in front of her. Suna made a note to ask one of the Kondo children later and went back to the laundry.

The afternoon was the dead time at the shrine. Rain started again after the midday meal — heavy, steady, the kind that lasted hours and drove everyone inside, turning the courtyard into a shallow pool of runoff that crept slowly toward the terraced garden. The main hall filled with the particular restless stillness of forty-three people with nowhere to go. The space was large enough for all of them, but only just — bodies shifting on benches and mats, conversations murmured in corners, the ambient hum of enforced proximity. Baby Ada Okoye was fussing. Someone was snoring. Moths had found the first lit oil lamp and were circling it in the gray afternoon light, their bodies tapping against the glass with a sound like small fingernails.

Kael was playing quietly with the pebble game he'd learned from the Kondo boys. Rimi had borrowed a book from Leni Okoye and was reading against the far wall, knees drawn up. Deshi was asleep on her mat, her blue shawl tucked under her chin, mouth open, the particular uninhibited snoring of a woman who had stopped caring what she sounded like in her sleep decades ago.

Asha had worn herself out in the play group and climbed into Suna's lap after the meal, her eyelids dropping, her body performing that heavy, gradual collapse that small children did when sleep took them between one word and the next. She was asleep in minutes, her weight tipped against Suna's chest, one hand curled around a fold of Suna's shirt. Her breathing deepened and steadied.

Suna held her and watched the rain. The light in the main hall was gray and even. Across the hall, other families moved in their own slow orbits — Hala Dao wiping down the cooking pots with a rag, Jin Parra sitting near the wall with Melie asleep across his knees, one hand resting on her back in the absent protective gesture of a man who slept lightly. Cade Leo and his partner Wynn sat together with Pik between them, Wynn's head on Cade's shoulder, the three of them arranged in a quiet private triangle that excluded the room. Mava was on her bench, eyes closed, hands in her lap. Not rubbing. Still.

Asha shifted against Suna's chest. Her breathing stayed deep and even, the breath of real sleep, not the light doze that could be broken by a voice or a closing door. Her left hand, the one that had been gripping Suna's shirt, loosened and rose.

She brought it to her left ear and began to rub. Palm flat. Slow circles. Fingers slightly spread.

Suna watched the hand move. The motion was precise and unhurried, the same tempo and the same pressure she had seen that morning at breakfast, and the same gesture she had watched Mava Kondo make every day for five days. But Asha was asleep. Her eyes were closed, her body slack, her breathing unchanged. There was no one across the hall to watch, no gesture to mirror. Her hand moved against her ear with the certainty of a thing the body knew how to do on its own, without instruction or audience.

Suna sat very still.

This was her response to things she could not manage — not panic, not a gasp, but a cessation. She stopped the way she stopped when a column of figures came out wrong, not with alarm but with the specific paralysis of someone whose system for organizing the world had just produced an answer that didn't fit. She sat with her sleeping daughter against her chest and watched the small hand move and did not think anything coherent for what might have been ten seconds or might have been a minute. The rain hit the courtyard stones outside. The moths tapped the lamp glass. Asha's hand kept its slow circuit.

Then she thought: I need to talk to the old woman.

---

She found Mava after the rain eased, sitting alone on the courtyard steps. The stones were wet and dark, and the sky had gone to the thick white that came between rain spells — not clearing, just pausing, the clouds rearranging themselves before the next fall. The air was cooler and smelled of stone and wet earth and the particular green sharpness of monsoon vegetation growing faster than anything could eat it. The flood sound was louder after rain, swollen with fresh runoff, a continuous low roar from the valley that made ordinary voices feel temporary.

Mava was watching the view. From the courtyard steps you could see down the slope to where the switchback road disappeared into brown water — the point where the seven roads converged, all of them drowned. The water was the color of milked tea, opaque, slow-moving at this distance, its surface pocked with rain. A bird Suna couldn't name was working the updraft above the waterline, banking and tilting with the concentrated effort of something hunting.

Suna sat down next to her. She didn't know Mava. Five days of shared confinement had made them neighbors but not acquaintances — the proximity of shared meals and sleeping quarters and the main hall's enforced closeness, without the private conversation that would have turned proximity into familiarity. Mava was a Kondo, one of Venn's twelve, and the Kondo family kept its own close. Suna had heard Mava laugh at a card game two nights ago — a dry, low sound that carried across the hall with more force than the old woman's small frame suggested — and she had seen her sorting lentils and rubbing her ear and walking slowly to the latrine and back, and that was the full extent of what she knew about a woman who had lived seventy-three years and carried a grief Suna was about to find.

"Can I ask you something?"

Mava looked at her. Up close, her face was deeply lined, the skin around her eyes creased in a way that suggested decades of squinting against bright light — a life lived outdoors, or at altitude, or both. Her eyes themselves were sharp and dark and unhurried, the eyes of someone who had long ago stopped feeling obligated to respond quickly.

"You can ask."

"Your ear." Suna touched her own left ear. "The way you rub it. You do it all the time."

Mava's hand rose, almost involuntarily, to her ear. She caught herself and lowered it. A small embarrassed sound, almost a laugh. "Old habit. My sister used to say I'd wear a hole through it."

"Asha does it too."

The faint amusement faded. Mava's face stayed open but the quality of her attention shifted — she was listening now, not just hearing.

"My daughter. She's four. She started doing it this morning — the same motion, the same hand, the same circles. I thought she was copying you. Kids do that, they pick up gestures." Suna paused. She was aware that what she was about to say would sound unreasonable, and she could not make it sound less so, so she said it directly. "She did it in her sleep. Just now, in the main hall. She was completely asleep and her hand came up and did the same thing. Exactly."

Mava did not speak. She looked at Suna for several seconds with an expression Suna could not categorize — not disbelief, not alarm, something that sat closer to the face of a person hearing a sound they had been straining to hear for a very long time.

"There's something else," Suna said. "She used a word I don't know. She pointed at you and said it. Nushi."

Mava made a sound. It was not a word. It was a small involuntary thing, the sound of air leaving the chest when the chest is struck — not hard, not violently, but in the precise place where it cannot brace. She put her hand flat on the stone step and pressed down, as if she needed to confirm that the stone was solid, that she was sitting on something that would hold.

"Say it again."

"Nushi."

Mava closed her eyes. She sat like that for several seconds, her face turned slightly toward the valley, the wet air moving past them both. When she opened her eyes they had a bright, hard shine that was not tears — the gloss of pressure held at the surface.

"My sister called me that." Her voice was low and steady, with the particular care of someone choosing each word from a very small and very precise set. "Nushi. It's from our home dialect — the village we grew up in, past the Anke pass. Nobody speaks it anymore. I don't think there's anyone left alive who does, except me. And I don't speak it. I just remember it."

"Your sister."

"Iru." Mava said the name the way she might set down something fragile — with both hands, with her full attention. "My younger sister. She called me Nushi because she couldn't manage my full name when she was small, and then it stuck. Even when we were grown women. Even when I told her it made me sound like a cat." The faintest pull at the corner of her mouth, not quite a smile — the ghost of an old joke that had outlived the person who'd made it. "She died on this road. Forty years ago. She was coming down from the pass with a traveling group — porters, another family, I don't remember who they were — and the road gave way in the monsoon. Three people went down with it. Iru was one of them. She was twenty-nine."

She said it simply. The words had the worn smoothness of a thing told many times — not to others, Suna understood, but to herself. The private recitation of a loss that had not dimmed but had been shaped by forty years of repetition into something she could carry.

"I haven't heard anyone say that word since she died," Mava said. "It isn't a word you'd find in a book. It isn't a name. It was just what she called me, in a language that was already dying when we were girls."

Suna did not speak for a moment. She was aware of the stone step under her, the damp seeping through the fabric of her skirt where she sat, the flood sound filling the valley below with its steady undifferentiated roar. She was aware that she had come here hoping to be told something ordinary — that "nushi" was a Kondo family nickname, a grandmother's word, something the children shared among themselves — and that she was not going to be told anything ordinary.

"Your daughter's never heard me speak in the old dialect," Mava said. It was not a question. "I haven't spoken it here. I haven't spoken it anywhere in years."

"No," Suna said. "She hasn't."

They sat together on the wet steps. The bird was still working the updraft above the waterline. From inside the main hall came the muffled sound of children's voices — Kael's shout, the babbling run of the younger play group, a baby crying and being hushed — the ordinary noise of forty-three people crammed into a space that had been built for travelers passing through, not for travelers stuck.

Mava rubbed her ear. She caught herself doing it and this time did not stop. Her hand moved in its slow circles, palm flat, and she watched the valley with the particular fixed attention of someone who was seeing not the present landscape but something underneath it.

"The road," she said. "Iru died right below us. Somewhere down there, under the water. I didn't know that when we arrived — Venn planned the route, he doesn't know about Iru. Nobody in the family knows about her except me and Habel, and Habel was a baby when it happened." She paused. "I recognized the road on the second day. I've been watching the water cover the place where she fell, and I haven't said anything about it to anyone, because what would I say? It was forty years ago. The road gave way and she went down with it and they found her body two days later in the river below. She was twenty-nine years old and she called me Nushi and nobody has said that word to me since."

Suna thought: twenty-nine, the same age as Jin Parra. She did not know why she thought this. It was the kind of useless, exact connection her mind produced when it had no useful response to offer — filing details into categories because the alternative was admitting that the space in front of her held no category at all.

"I don't understand how Asha could know that word," Suna said.

"No." Mava's voice was quiet. "I don't either."

The honesty of it — the refusal to explain, to narrate, to frame the strangeness in terms that would make it manageable — was worse than any theory would have been. Suna wanted someone to tell her this was a coincidence. That children plucked sounds from the air, from dreams, from the particular permeability of early childhood when language was a game and not a system. She wanted someone to file this for her, the way she filed things for her own family — noted, categorized, set aside. Mava was not offering to file it. Mava was sitting with a dead sister's name in her mouth for the first time in forty years, and neither of them had anywhere to put what it meant.

"Can I —" Suna started, and stopped. She had been about to ask if she could bring Asha to Mava, to sit them together, and she realized she did not know what she expected to happen if she did. What she expected the old woman and the child to do in each other's presence that would make this resolve into sense.

"I'd like to see her do it," Mava said quietly. "The ear. I know it's my habit — I've done it my whole life. My mother did it too. And Iru."

"Iru did it?"

"All of us. On my mother's side. Left ear. I never noticed until Iru pointed it out — she thought it was funny. She'd do it and then point at me doing it and laugh." Mava's hand was on her ear again, and she let it stay this time. "It's just something our bodies did."

Our bodies. Not our family, not we. Our bodies — as if the gesture belonged to the flesh itself, passed along through some channel that ran beneath choice or knowledge, carried in muscle and tendon and the blind habit of a hand finding the same place on the same ear, over and over, through generations that didn't know they were repeating anything.

Suna sat with her a while longer. They did not talk much more. Mava asked after Asha — how she was sleeping, whether she was eating well, whether the damp in the sleeping quarters bothered her — the practical questions of a woman who had raised children and grandchildren and great-nieces and understood that the body was the first thing you checked, regardless of what else was happening. Suna answered. The rain started again, softly, more mist than fall, and they watched it settle on the courtyard stones and on the brown surface of the flood below.

When Suna went back inside, the main hall was dim and warm with breath and lamp-oil, the evening meal being prepared in the kitchen outside, its smoke drifting in through the open side. She found her children: Kael at his pebble game, Rimi reading, Asha on their shared mat, awake now, arranging a strip of cloth she'd found somewhere into shapes that followed a logic only she understood.

"Asha."

"Mama."

"Who's Nushi?"

Asha looked at her with the transparent certainty of a child who did not understand why an adult would ask something so obvious. She pointed through the doorway, toward the courtyard, in the direction where Mava had been sitting and might still be sitting.

"Nushi," she said.

Suna picked her up and held her, pressing her face into Asha's hair, which smelled of smoke and sweat and the particular warm-grain scent of a child who had been sleeping on a cotton mat all afternoon. Asha allowed it for a moment, then squirmed, and Suna let her go. She watched her daughter return to the mat and the strip of cloth, small fingers working it into a new arrangement.

That night Suna slept badly. Deshi snored on her right side, one hand tucked under her cheek. Peyam murmured through the tail end of his prayers, the beads clicking softly against each other in the dark. Rain fell and stopped and fell again, and the flood below the shrine filled the night with its low, constant sound — water over stone, water over earth, water over the place where the roads converged and drowned.

Asha slept deeply, curled against Suna's side, her face pressed into Suna's ribs. At some point in the middle of the night — the rain had paused and the only sound was the flood and the breathing of her family and the forty other lives separated from them by nothing but thin partitions of stone — Asha's left hand rose to her ear.

Palm flat. Slow circles. Fingers slightly spread.

Suna lay still and watched the hand move in the dark. The water below them sounded like something she had never noticed before — not like weather, not like a natural thing, but like the road itself dissolving, and Iru twenty-nine years old going down with it, and the word Nushi sitting in a four-year-old's mouth where it had no way of arriving. She closed her eyes and held the weight of her daughter against her ribs and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 5

Venn counted his family before the bell rang. It was a habit from long before the shrine — before every departure, every arrival, every meal at his sister's house where children scattered across three rooms and you couldn't leave until you'd counted them back. Twelve. He had twelve Kondos, and they would enter the naming hall as twelve, seated together, declared together, leaving together when the road cleared.

The bell sounded from the custodians' walkway. A single iron note that traveled through stone.

Venn rose from the bench along the courtyard wall where he'd been sitting with Arash, talking about the road. Not about the wedding — they had stopped talking about the wedding two days ago, by unspoken agreement, because each mention of it tightened something in Arash's jaw that Venn did not want to examine. Eleven days. The wedding was in eleven days, and the road below was still brown water reaching for the stone switchbacks, and every morning Venn looked down at the flood and calculated, and every morning the calculation came out the same: not yet.

"Everyone," he said. Not a shout. He never needed to shout at the Kondos. They gathered the way a flock gathers — not from obedience but from the bone-deep habit of moving when the head moved.

Tila came first, folding the shirt she'd been patching before she stood. She carried her sewing kit everywhere, a cloth wrap she'd packed thirty years ago for their own wedding trip, and the wrap was battered and restained and Tila could find the right needle in it by touch alone, in the dark, with one hand. She had a way of being finished with whatever she was doing in the instant she was called — never hurrying, never leaving thread or thimble behind, just done. Venn had loved this about her for twenty-nine years without telling her so, because the telling would imply he had noticed its absence as a possibility, and he had not.

Habel brought Ori by the hand. Habel was fifty-five and had the same broad shoulders as Venn, the family shoulders, but she carried hers differently — not thrust forward like a declaration but gathered around Ori, sheltering. Ori was nine and ordinarily walked unguided, but she'd been clingy since the day before, since she'd seen Asha Mera rubbing her left ear in the slow circular motion that belonged to Auntie Mava and no one else. Ori had stared. Habel had pulled her away. Now Ori kept her mother's hand and watched the Mera girl whenever they crossed paths, with a wariness that should not have belonged to a nine-year-old watching a four-year-old.

Mava walked slowly, Arash at her elbow. She had not spoken much since yesterday, since the Mera woman — Suna — had found her in the kitchen alcove and asked about the word. Venn had extracted the story from Mava that evening, and it had taken patience because Mava cried twice during the telling and both times stopped herself as if crying were a breach of protocol. The word. Her sister Nima's word. A pet name from the old dialect, from the valley speech of Ishe where Nima had married, and Nima had died on this road forty years ago — on the shrine road, her cart in the ravine — and the word had died with her. Or should have died with her. Mava used it now for Ori, the same endearment, the same soft shape, but it was not the same because it came from Mava's mouth and not Nima's, and Mava knew the difference even if no one else did.

And the four-year-old Mera girl had said it. In her sleep, Suna claimed. In her sleep, the word that meant something close to *the other half of my face*, a word from a dialect no one at the shrine spoke, no one in the Mera family had ever heard, no one in the Kondo family used except Mava in the privacy of her grief.

Venn had told Mava it was coincidence. Children babble. They produce sounds. Some sounds resemble words. He didn't believe this, precisely. But belief was not the point. The point was that the Kondos did not need a connection to the Mera family. The point was the wedding, the road, the need to leave this place before it changed them into something they had not chosen to be.

The rest of the Kondos gathered — Ger, Habel's husband, who stood where Habel stood and did not require instruction; Arash's cousins Rem and Osh, fifteen and thirteen, subdued by the shrine's routine into a compliance that would have been unthinkable at home; the younger children clustering near Tila, who managed them without appearing to manage them. Twelve. All accounted for. Venn counted them again from habit and then walked toward the main hall.

The stone floor was cold through his sandals. Rain light washed in from the hall's open side — the side that faced the valley and the flood below, a flat brown expanse that erased the road and the switchbacks and the convergence of seven routes that had once meant travelers could come and go as they chose. Oil lamps had been lit along the inner wall despite the morning hour; the cloud cover was heavy, and the hall needed the flicker to feel like a room rather than a cave.

The other families were already seated or settling. Venn assessed them the way he assessed any room — by composition, position, and the quality of attention they brought.

The Okoyes: the teacher Bram sat with his knees wide and his hands on his thighs, the posture of a man who would rather be standing and arguing. His wife Nessa had the baby on her hip and a crease between her eyes that never smoothed. The girl Leni was beside her mother, braids slightly unraveled, biting a thumbnail and looking at everything with an attention that did not match her age.

The Parras: just the two. Jin Parra sat against the far wall with his daughter in his lap, his posture folded inward, his face a door that did not open. He had a way of being present without being visible, which Venn recognized as the posture of a man who did not want to be asked questions.

The Teshes: the old woman Gitta, her hands folded in her lap, her thumbs moving in a slow pattern that might have been prayer. Her son Miro and his wife Enn flanked her like bookends, and their two younger children — Kiri, eight, who squirmed, and the boy whose name Venn hadn't retained — sat at their feet. The grandson, Fen, sixteen, sat slightly apart. He resembled no one in his family, which Venn had noticed and filed under things that were not his business.

The Daos: the sisters. Hala sat with her children Raya and Sem, her body angled slightly in front of Bett, as it always was — a physical shield that had become posture. Bett was beside her with Nori, and Bett's face was the face of a woman who had decided long ago what she was willing to show in public and would not be moved from that decision.

The Leos: the two men, Cade and Wynn, their adopted son Pik between them, and Cade's mother Vera on the end of the bench, her hands worrying the hem of her shawl. Venn had taken note of their arrangement during the first naming and decided it was not his concern. This was, in his reckoning, a form of generosity.

Forty-three family members. Five custodians. The shrine was too small for this many bodies, and the naming made it smaller, because it required everyone in one room, facing the same direction, answering the same questions.

Fasa stood at the front of the hall, by the open side, the valley behind her. Rain blurred the view at her back. She wore the same loose linen tunic she always wore, and her hands were clasped in front of her, and she waited with the patience of someone who believed time belonged to the ritual rather than the people in the room.

Olet sat to her left on a stone bench set into the wall. He had the look of a man who had sat on that bench through a thousand namings and would sit through a thousand more. His face communicated nothing Venn could read.

Venn positioned the Kondos on the right side of the hall, near the inner wall, in the arrangement that said *we are here and we are one thing*. Tila beside him. Arash at his other side. Mava seated on the bench because she could not stand for long. Habel and Ori behind Mava. Ger, Rem, Osh, the younger ones — arranged in the configuration Venn had built over decades of family weddings, funerals, market squares, and negotiations. The Kondo formation. It had never occurred to him that it could be broken by anything smaller than a death.

Fasa spoke.

"The naming continues," she said. "Each family declared itself four days ago. Since that naming, I have watched. You have lived here together. And some things have become visible that were not visible before."

Venn felt his shoulders set. He knew what was coming. He'd known since Mava told him about the Mera woman's visit, since Mava's face crumpled and reformed and crumpled again as she recounted the word in the child's mouth.

"I ask the Mera family and the Kondo family to come forward. To stand together."

The silence was immediate. Even the Okoye baby, who had been fussing, went still — or Nessa covered her mouth, or the room's pressure settled on all of them like the drop before a downpour.

Suna spoke first. "No."

The word was flat. She held Asha against her chest and looked at Fasa the way you looked at someone who had asked for something that was not theirs to ask for — not with outrage but with the plain refusal of a person who had already decided.

"We're not going to do that," she said. "What happened between Asha and Mava is between me and her. It's private. It isn't naming business."

Venn should have been relieved. The Mera woman was saying no. His position was being held for him, without his needing to hold it.

But Suna's refusal did something he hadn't expected. She was declining the Kondos — as though the Kondos were the ones who had reached across the hall, as though the connection was something Venn's family had sought and the Meras were now turning away. Her *no* put the Kondos in the position of being refused rather than refusing. And she hadn't even looked at him.

He looked at her across the hall. She was watching Fasa, not him. His family's involvement was secondary to her own decision.

"No one from my family has asked for this either," Venn said. He directed it at Fasa, not at Suna. He was not going to argue with the Mera woman across the hall like market vendors shouting over each other's stalls. "The Kondo family declared itself clearly at the first naming. We are twelve. We are traveling to a wedding. We have no connection to the Mera family beyond the accident of this flood, and we didn't come here to be told otherwise."

Fasa let his voice die in the stone hall before she answered. The pause was the same pause she always used — the custodian's silence, which made whatever came before it sound smaller.

"The naming does not ask what you have chosen," she said. "It asks what has emerged."

"Nothing has emerged."

Fasa looked at Mava. The look was gentle, and the gentleness was a weapon — it said *I see your grief and I intend to make the room look at it.*

"Mava," Fasa said. "Will you tell the naming what Suna Mera came to ask you yesterday?"

Mava's hand went to her left ear. The gesture — the slow circular rub of palm against cartilage, a soothing motion she had done for as long as Venn could remember — happened without her seeming to notice. She looked at Venn.

He shook his head. A small movement. A command.

"The naming asks," Fasa said. "Not your nephew."

Mava's hand stopped. She lowered it to her lap and held both hands together.

"The Mera girl," Mava said. Her voice was thin, worn to its edges, the voice of a woman who had not raised it in years. "Asha. Her mother brought her. The child used a word."

"What word?" Fasa asked.

"My sister's word. An endearment. From the old speech — the valley dialect of Ishe, where my sister married. Nima called me by this name when she visited. It means something like *the other half of my face.* I haven't heard it from anyone except Nima." She paused. "Nima has been dead for forty years."

The hall was still. Venn heard the rain on the stone outside, and someone's breathing — quick, catching — and the soft pop of an oil lamp's wick adjusting in its clay dish.

"Does anyone in the Mera family speak this dialect?" Fasa asked, turning to Suna.

"No," Suna said. Her arms were tight around Asha.

"Has anyone in the Mera family traveled to Ishe? Lived in that valley? Met anyone from that region?"

"No. I've never heard of the place before yesterday."

Fasa looked at the rest of the Mera group. "Deshi. Peyam. Have either of you been to Ishe, or known anyone who speaks the valley dialect?"

Old Peyam opened his eyes fully. He was holding his prayer beads in both spotted hands, and he shook his head — a slow, careful negation that carried the weight of seventy-two years of being certain about what he did and didn't know. Then he returned to his beads.

Deshi, seated behind Suna with her back as straight as the stone wall she leaned against, said nothing. She was watching. Not Fasa. Not Suna. She was watching Mava.

"Then the word has no path," Fasa said. "No connection between the Mera family and the dialect of Ishe. No way for a four-year-old child to have learned it. The naming asks both families to stand together and acknowledge what has appeared between them."

"I've already said no," Suna said.

"The naming hears your refusal," Fasa said. "The naming asks again."

Venn stood. He had intended to sit, to let the refusals from both sides form a wall the custodian could not breach. But Fasa's repetition — *the naming asks again* — was the voice of an institution that believed it could outlast any individual, and Venn had been fighting that voice all his life. Every official. Every document clerk. Every border guard who looked at twelve Kondos and wanted to know why so many, where from, where to.

"This is not a connection," he said. His voice filled the hall. He heard it strike the stone walls and come back. "A child said a word. Children say thousands of words. They babble. They pick up sounds from the air. My aunt lost her sister forty years ago — forty years — and now a stranger's child produces a sound that my aunt recognizes, and the naming tells us this means our families are what? Related? Bound?" He looked at the other families. The teacher was leaning forward. The quiet Parra man was staring at his own hands. The Leo couple sat very still, as if making themselves small would keep the naming's attention off them. "I don't accept this. The Kondo family doesn't accept this."

Fasa waited. She did not argue, did not respond, did not shift her weight. She waited, and the waiting had its own pressure, because silence after a man's declaration made the declaration smaller.

Then Deshi stood.

Venn had not been watching Deshi — had categorized her at the first naming and not revised: the sharp grandmother from the Mera side, the one who complained about the rice and the sleeping arrangements, who wore flat leather sandals cracked and reshaped by years of use, who assessed everything and everyone through standards she considered self-evident. She was the kind of woman Venn recognized from his own family's history — domestic authority wielded as judgment, tradition used as a blade. He'd assumed she was Suna's problem, not his.

But Deshi stood, and she did not look at Fasa or Suna or Venn. She looked at Mava.

She crossed the hall. Not quickly — her pace was the deliberate pace of a woman who had never hurried in her life because hurrying was an admission that someone else controlled the schedule. She walked between the watching families, her worn sandals silent on the stone, and stopped in front of the bench where Mava sat.

Mava looked up at her.

"Your sister," Deshi said. "What was her name?"

Mava blinked. "Nima."

"And the word. The one my granddaughter said. Was it Nima's word for you, or your word for Nima?"

"It was hers. For me."

Deshi nodded. She turned her head and looked at Asha across the hall — the girl on Suna's lap, thumb near her mouth, her left hand resting against her ear.

"She doesn't know what she said," Deshi said, mostly to herself. "She's four years old. She doesn't know any of this."

Then Deshi sat down next to Mava. Not across the hall, where the Meras were seated. She sat on the Kondo side, on the stone bench, close enough that her knee pressed against Mava's, and she took Mava's hands in both of hers.

"Tell me about Nima," Deshi said.

Venn heard Suna behind him: "Deshi —"

"Be quiet," Deshi said, without turning. It was not a request. It was the voice of a woman who had been telling younger people to be quiet for forty years and had never once doubted the timing.

Mava stared at Deshi's hands around hers. She was very still, and then she was not still. Her face broke, and the sound she made was small and terrible — not crying the way people cry in front of an audience, performed and recoverable, but the sound of something old giving way, a grief so long held that it had calcified, and now the calcification was cracking.

"She was younger than me," Mava said, through the sound. "By four years. She was the pretty one. She married a man from Ishe and loved the valley and was coming home to visit me and the cart went off the road. This road. The shrine road. They found her in the ravine. Forty years ago."

Deshi held her hands and said nothing. She did not look at anyone else in the room.

The hall watched. Venn watched. He was standing, and he did not know what to do with his body — whether to sit, step forward, speak, or simply wait. Tila's hand found his forearm, a pressure that said *not yet.*

***

The naming did not resume its formal structure. Fasa let it go. She stood at the front of the hall and watched Deshi sit with Mava, and she did not ask further questions, because something had happened outside the naming's script and Fasa — for all her rehearsed patience — seemed to understand that the script had been superseded.

Mava wept for a long time. Not the convulsive crying of a crisis but the steady, slow weeping of someone whose grief had found its channel after decades of containment. Deshi sat with her. Habel moved to Mava's other side and put a hand on her shoulder. Ori pressed against her mother and stared at Mava with an expression Venn recognized as fear — not fear of Mava, but the fear of a child seeing an adult she considered unbreakable begin to break. Arash stood behind the bench, his hands in the pockets of his rain-damp trousers, and his face — Venn saw it and looked away — had the expression of someone watching a door open that they'd been told would always remain shut.

The Kondo formation was gone. His careful arrangement — right side, inner wall, the configuration that said *we are one thing* — had come apart around the two old women on the bench, and Venn could not reassemble it without physically pulling Deshi away from his aunt, which he could not do because Deshi was not his to command, and more importantly because Mava was gripping Deshi's hands and would not let go.
