Venn sat. Not a choice — his body decided, the way it had when Arash was born and the midwife said *a boy* and his knees had given out and he'd sat on the floor of the birthing room without caring who saw.

Suna spoke from across the hall. Not loudly. Not to anyone specific. Just into the room, the way you speak when the thing you're about to say has been pressing against your teeth and you can't hold it any longer.

"She said it in her sleep," Suna said. "Asha. She said the word in her sleep. She'd never heard it awake. I watched her say it while she was dreaming."

The detail changed the room's temperature. Venn felt it. A word used while awake could be imitation, parroting, coincidence. A word produced in sleep — a word from a dead woman's dialect, a word that meant *the other half of my face* — was something else. Something that explanation could not reach.

Gitta Tesh sat in the far corner with her hands folded in her lap, her thumbs still circling each other in that slow prayer motion. Her grandson Fen was watching Mava and Deshi with an intensity that seemed older than sixteen, as though he were looking at something that had to do with him personally, though Venn could not imagine what.

Bram Okoye spoke. "Can we be clear about what's being claimed?" His voice had the teacher's quality of a man arranging information into an argument he intended to win. "Because either this is coincidence — a child produced a sound that resembles a word from a dialect — or this is something that doesn't have a rational explanation. And if it's the second, then the naming is asking us to accept something that none of us—"

"The naming isn't asking you to accept anything," Fasa said. "The naming asks you to name what is in front of you."

"What's in front of me is an old woman crying and a child rubbing her ear," Bram said. He stopped. His wife touched his arm. He adjusted, the way a man adjusts when he hears himself sounding colder than he meant to. "I'm not dismissing it. I'm asking what the naming wants us to do with it."

"Acknowledge it," Fasa said.

Venn stood again. Standing was better. Standing was the posture of a man who had a position and intended to hold it.

"Acknowledge what?" he said. "My aunt is grieving a sister she lost before most of us were born. The Mera girl is four years old. She doesn't know what she's saying. She doesn't know who Nima was. This is grief, not connection — my aunt's grief, the Kondo family's grief. It's ours, not the naming's." He looked at Deshi — at Deshi's hands wrapped around Mava's. "And not anyone else's."

Deshi looked at him. Her eyes were dry. She had held Mava's hands and let Mava cry and she had not shed a tear herself, and the dryness of her eyes was its own kind of statement — not coldness but the discipline of a woman who did not cry in public because she had decided long ago what she spent her tears on, and other people's opinions had never made the list.

"A dead woman's word is in my granddaughter's mouth," Deshi said to Venn. "What do you want me to do? Walk away from it?" She did not raise her voice. She did not need to. "My granddaughter says a word that has no path to her. No route. No explanation. She says it in her sleep. And the woman whose sister owned that word is sitting here weeping because she hasn't heard it from anyone living in forty years." She paused. "What is the proper response? What does your authority tell you the proper response is?"

Venn had no answer. He had answers for disputes about property, about marriage arrangements, about who sat where at the family table. He had answers for the dowry negotiations, for the letters he would write to Arash's bride's father when they finally came down from this hill. He had answers for everything that belonged to the architecture of the Kondo family — who owed what, who deferred to whom, how twelve people moved through the world as one body.

He did not have an answer for a dead woman's word in a living child's mouth. He did not have a category for it.

"The Kondo family will consider this privately," he said. It was the best he could produce — a deferral dressed as authority, the sentence of a man retreating but making the retreat look like command.

"There is nothing to consider privately," Fasa said. "The naming is where private things are spoken aloud. That is its purpose."

Tila spoke. Venn turned, because Tila almost never spoke in the naming — she managed, she assessed, she supported, but she did not address the room. "Let him sit down," Tila said to Fasa. Her voice was practical, not pleading. "He's heard it. We've all heard it. Mava is upset. Give us time."

Fasa paused. She looked at Olet, who had not moved from his bench, who had not spoken, whose face remained exactly what it had been when the naming began — nothing. Olet gave no signal. Fasa nodded.

"The naming will continue tomorrow," she said.

But the formal close did not close anything. The families did not rise and scatter the way they had after the first naming, when the ceremony had felt like a toll paid and forgotten. This time people stayed. They stayed because Mava was still weeping, and Deshi was still holding her hands, and the hall felt like a room where something had been cracked open and the pieces were still on the floor and no one wanted to be the first to step around them.

Old Peyam rose from the Mera side and crossed the hall. He moved slowly, beads still in his hand, and lowered himself onto the bench beside Mava — Habel shifting to make room. He did not speak or reach for her. He simply sat, an old man beside an old woman, and the sitting was enough. Two elders from two families, flanking a third, and the arrangement looked like something that had always existed and was only now becoming visible.

The Dao woman — Hala — was leaning toward Bett and whispering something Venn could not hear. Bett's face was still closed, still guarded, but her eyes were on Mava with an attention that Venn recognized as the attention of someone who understood grief from the inside and was watching it happen to someone else.

Jin Parra had not moved. He held Melie in his lap, his posture a mirror of Suna's with Asha across the hall, and he was staring at the stone floor with the focus of a man counting cracks. When the families began to leave — the Okoyes first, Bram murmuring to Nessa, Leni casting a long backward look at the two old women before following her parents; then the Leos, Cade's hand on Pik's shoulder; then the Teshes, Gitta's slow careful exit — Jin was the last of the uninvolved families to go. He stood with Melie on his hip and hesitated at the hall's entrance, looking back at the bench, at the old women and the old man and the fractured Kondo arrangement, and his face did something Venn could not read. Then he was gone.

Mava spoke again. Her voice came from deep in her chest, roughened by the weeping but clearer now, as if the crying had scoured something clean.

"She was coming to visit me," Mava said. "Nima. She was coming to see me, and the cart went off the road, and I never saw her again. And I've been rubbing this ear for forty years because she used to rub it for me when I was upset — when we were girls she would hold my head and rub my ear and call me by the name and I would stop crying." She looked at Deshi. "I haven't stopped. I just stopped doing it in front of people."

Deshi did not look at Venn. She did not look at Suna. She looked at the old woman whose hands she held, and she said, "Then the word isn't dead. It's in my granddaughter. Your sister gave it to her. I don't know how, and you don't know how, and nobody in this room knows how. But it's there."

This was the thing Venn could not fight. Not the custodians' ritual language, not Fasa's patience, not the other families watching. This — two old women on a stone bench, one weeping and one certain, the domestic authority of someone who had spent her life judging what was real applied to something impossible and arriving at a conclusion that his own authority could not reach. Deshi's certainty came from the same tradition he claimed. The grandmother's authority. The elder's right to judge. And it had landed on the opposite side.

Tila touched his arm. "Come outside," she said.

They stood under the stone overhang by the kitchen, out of the rain, close enough that their shoulders touched. The courtyard stones were dark with water. The cooking fire had been banked for the naming, and the smell of cold ash mixed with the green of wet vegetation and the mineral tang of the rain itself.

Tila did not ask him what he was thinking. She stood and waited, and when he did not speak, she waited longer.

"The wedding is in eleven days," Venn said.

"I know."

"The road isn't going to open."

"I know."

"And now this. This word. This whatever this is."

He was aware that *whatever this is* was a failure of language from a man who prided himself on never failing to name things. The Kondo family's identity was built on naming — on Venn saying who they were, where they came from, what they owed, and who owed them, and the names he gave held because he said them with the full weight of fifty-two years of being right about the shape of his family. And now a four-year-old had said a word, and an old woman had cried, and another old woman had crossed a hall and sat down, and the names Venn carried felt lighter than they had that morning.

Tila was quiet for a moment. Then: "Mava hasn't cried for Nima since the funeral. Not once. Forty years. You know that."

He did know that. Mava's grief for Nima was a fact of the family, carried but never performed — acknowledged in the occasional softening of Mava's voice when the old dialect came up, in the ear-rubbing gesture everyone noticed and no one mentioned. Mava did not cry. She had attended her sister's funeral dry-eyed and had not cried again, and the family had understood this as strength. The Kondo way.

"She's crying now," Venn said.

"Because someone asked her to," Tila said. "Not the custodian. That woman — Deshi. She asked, and Mava answered. That's what happened in there."

Venn looked at his wife. Her face was calm and practical, the face she wore when she was managing him — not through manipulation but through the steady work of a woman who understood the difference between what he wanted to hear and what he needed to.

"It doesn't change anything," he said.

Tila stood beside him and let the rain fill the silence. From inside the hall, he could still hear Mava — not crying now, but talking, her voice low and steady, telling Deshi something about Nima that Venn had never heard. The rain came down on the courtyard stones, and the flood roared below in the valley, and the road was drowned, and the naming would come again tomorrow, and the day after that, and every day until the water dropped and Venn could take his family down the hill.

He counted them. Twelve Kondos under this roof. All present. All accounted for.

The number had not changed. But the number, he understood now, was no longer the thing that held them together.


# Chapter 6

The children had taken over the lower terrace.

Jin watched from the kitchen overhang where he'd been scrubbing pots for the past hour — volunteer work, the kind nobody argued about, because it kept him useful and silent and away from the naming hall where the adults were still sorting through whatever had happened yesterday between Mava Kondo and Suna Mera's mother-in-law. He didn't want to know. He didn't want to have an opinion about it. He wanted to scrub the black crust off the rice pot and watch his daughter.

Melie was in the middle of the group, which was new. For the first four days she'd circled the edges, watching the Kondo children and the Mera boy with the cautious hunger Jin recognized because he'd felt it at her age — wanting to be included but not knowing how to step in. She'd stood near Rimi Mera once, close enough that the twelve-year-old had to notice her, and Rimi had handed her a smooth pebble without looking down, an offhand kindness that Melie had carried in her pocket for two days afterward, turning it over and over until the warmth of her hand was indistinguishable from the warmth of the stone.

Now she sat cross-legged next to Nori Dao, the two of them arranging sticks in a pattern that seemed to involve rules Jin couldn't follow. Nori was a year older and a head taller and kept rearranging Melie's sticks without asking, and Melie — his Melie, who had spent three months going quiet at the table, who had stopped singing in the bath, who had developed the habit of checking the front door whenever she heard footsteps — didn't object. She just waited for Nori to finish correcting her placement and then added her next stick with careful concentration. The patience was new. Or not new. Recovered. She'd been a patient child before Reva left.

Jin's hands stopped in the water. He made himself keep scrubbing. The pot was copper, dented at the rim, and the burnt rice came away in black flakes that dissolved between his fingers. He pressed his thumb against the metal hard enough to whiten the nail. The water was cold from the cistern, cold enough that his knuckles ached, and he pressed harder.

Reva had walked out on a Tuesday. He remembered the day because she'd left her lunch in the fridge — the container she packed every morning, the one with the cracked blue lid — and he'd come home from the site and seen it sitting there on the shelf and known before he checked the closet, before he found the note, before he counted what was missing. She'd left her lunch. She hadn't planned on going that morning. Or she had planned it and then still packed her lunch out of habit and then left it there and walked out anyway, and he didn't know which version was worse — the impulsive abandonment or the deliberate one that was so rehearsed she could go through the motions of staying even while she was leaving.

He'd told Melie that her mother had died. He'd told her that evening, quickly, before anyone else could tell her something different, because Reva's sister knew and her sister's husband knew and Jin could already see the machinery of explanation gearing up around him, the concerned faces and the careful words and the particular head-tilt of pity that precedes unsolicited advice. He'd needed to control the story. A dead mother was clean. A dead mother couldn't come back. A dead mother wasn't a woman who looked at her daughter and her husband and their kitchen with the cracked blue container still in the fridge and decided she wanted something else.

The shame of it sat in him like a stone he'd swallowed and couldn't pass. Not the shame of lying to Melie — he'd tell the truth eventually, he told himself, when she was older, when she could hold it — but the shame of being left. Men whose wives died got the casseroles, the soft voices, the neighbors who checked in and brought extra food on weekends. Men whose wives left got the quick sideways look, the change of subject, the specific silence of people calculating what he must have done wrong. He'd watched it happen in the first week after Reva walked out, before he'd settled on the lie — the way his brother-in-law's expression shifted from sympathy to assessment. Something must have been wrong in the house. The man must have done something, or failed to do something, or been the kind of man a woman picks up and walks away from like a coat she's tired of wearing.

And the worst part, the part that woke him at three in the morning on the mat in this stone compound with his daughter's ribs pressed against his: maybe they were right. Maybe he was exactly that kind of man. A woman had looked at her life with him and decided it wasn't enough to stay for, and that meant something about who he was, something no amount of telling himself that Reva's leaving was Reva's choice could dislodge from the place where it lived, somewhere behind his sternum, heavy and permanent.

He pulled the pot from the basin and set it upside down on the stone ledge to dry. His hands were red and his knuckles were swelling slightly from the cold. Below him, on the terrace, Melie laughed.

The sound went through him like a needle. She was laughing at something Nori had done — knocked over the entire stick arrangement, maybe, or said something in the blunt way that Nori had, a seven-year-old's declarative certainty about the world that seemed to delight Melie in a way that adult gentleness never did. Jin watched his daughter's face: open, unguarded, the gap where her front teeth used to be visible because she was laughing with her whole mouth. She hadn't laughed like that since before Reva left. Since before the note, the empty closet, the lie.

She was thriving here. In this stone compound surrounded by strangers who asked too many questions and custodians who rang a bell for every meal and prayer and an old woman who wanted to know the maiden name of his dead wife. She was thriving because there were other children and because no one here knew the specific shape of what was missing from her life, and the story Jin had told at the naming — my wife passed, we're traveling to her family — sat over the truth like a lid, and the lid let Melie move through this temporary world without the particular cruelty of being the girl whose mother chose to leave.

The girl whose mother chose to leave. He couldn't even think the words without his jaw locking. The old hesitation, the catch before the consonant — he pressed his teeth together and waited for it to pass. It always passed. It had been doing this since he was twelve, long before Reva, long before any of this, and it always passed.

He stacked the clean pots on the ledge, dried his hands on the cloth hanging from the overhang post, and stood watching his daughter play until the light shifted and the shadows of the compound walls reached the lower terrace and the children began to scatter toward their families.

---

Bett Dao was at the cistern when Jin brought Melie down to wash her hair before the evening meal.

She was filling a clay jug, one hand braced on the stone basin's lip, and she straightened when she heard them on the steps. Her sleeves were rolled to her elbows in precise folds, the kind of careful cuffing Jin associated with people who'd worked with their hands for years — tidiness as reflex, not vanity. She wore a thin brass ring on her right hand, tarnished nearly black, that she turned with her thumb while she waited for the jug to fill. She nodded at him — the same flat acknowledgment she gave everyone, not unfriendly but not an opening — and went back to her water.

"Papa, it's cold," Melie said, touching the surface with one finger and pulling it back.

"I know. It's always cold." He'd washed her hair here twice since they'd arrived, both times in the late afternoon when the stone basin caught the last warmth from the sun. The water came from a spring somewhere beneath the shrine, cold even in monsoon heat, and Melie complained about it every time with the specific outrage of a child who has been warned about something unpleasant and then discovers that the warning did not make it less unpleasant.

"Nori says her aunt washes her hair with soap flowers," Melie said, looking at Bett's back. "She says they make your hair smell like the garden."

"We don't have soap flowers."

"Maybe Bett has some."

"We're not going to bother her. Come here."

Jin cupped water in both hands and brought it to Melie's head. She ducked and squealed and he caught her by the shoulder, firm but careful, the grip of a parent whose child was always about to dart away from the one thing that needed doing. "Hold still. I'll be fast."

The water ran through her dark hair and down the back of her neck. She shivered and he felt the shiver travel up through his hand into his wrist. Her hair was getting long — past her shoulders now, the longest it had ever been. Reva had always kept it trimmed, sitting Melie on a kitchen stool with a towel around her shoulders and cutting with the good scissors, the ones with the orange handles that were still in the drawer at home. Jin didn't know how to cut hair evenly. Melie didn't seem to mind the length, or if she minded she hadn't said, and he hadn't asked because asking might surface the question of why her mother wasn't here to do it.

He worked the soap through her hair — a brown block the custodians provided, harsh and unscented, the kind that stripped everything — his thumbs moving along her scalp in the pattern he'd learned from watching Reva do this. His hands remembered the small circles. The gentleness required at the temples where the skin was thin. His hands had learned this from a woman who was alive and somewhere else, living a life that didn't include the small warm skull under his fingers, and his hands did it anyway because muscle memory doesn't care about betrayal.

"Papa."

"Hmm."

"Is Nori's mum here?"

His fingers went still in her soapy hair. "I'm not sure about their family," he said. He didn't know the Daos' arrangement — he knew there were two women, Bett and her older sister Hala, and their children, and that no husbands had been mentioned at the naming. He hadn't asked, because asking invited questions in return, and questions were the one thing he couldn't afford.

Bett was still at the basin, her jug full now, but she hadn't moved to leave. She was listening — not obviously, her body angled away, her free hand pulling at a thread on her sleeve cuff — but listening in the way Jin recognized because he did it himself. The practiced stillness of someone who has trained themselves to catch a conversation's shape without entering it.

"Bett's Nori's aunt," Bett said, without turning around. Her voice was even, unperformative. "Hala's her mum."

"Oh." Melie's face was upside down, looking up at Jin through the fall of her wet hair. "But Nori said Bett is more like her mum."

"People can be more than one thing to someone," Jin said, and the sentence sat in his mouth too large, too close to a truth he was keeping from her. He reached for the tin cup by the basin and rinsed her hair, pouring with one hand and shielding her eyes with the other, and Melie endured it with the stoic misery of a child who has learned that protest doesn't shorten the process.

Bett had turned and was watching them. Not watching — something less intrusive. She held the jug on her hip with the ease of someone who carried water most days, and her face, which Jin had only ever seen from across the hall as a guarded surface, was doing something he hadn't expected. She was looking at Melie's wet hair and Jin's careful hands with an expression that wasn't pity and wasn't judgment. It looked like memory. Like she was watching something she had done once, for someone, in another kitchen, and the watching cost her something she was choosing to pay.

Then she turned and climbed the steps with the jug on her hip, and the moment closed behind her.

Jin wrung Melie's hair between his palms and twisted it into the rough shape of a braid. He didn't know how to braid properly. Another thing Reva had done. Another skill he'd never learned because he'd assumed she would always be there to do it, and the assumption had felt so solid, so much like ground, that he'd never noticed he was standing on something that could move.

"Come on," he said. "Dinner soon."

Melie took his hand. Her fingers were cold and small and damp, and she swung his arm as they climbed the steps, and Jin held on.

---

The evening meal was set out on the stone ledge that served as the communal table — flatbreads stacked under a cloth, a lentil pot still bubbling over the last of the coals, rice with fried shallots, and three small bowls of condiments the Dao women had prepared from their own stores. Jin knew the Daos had been on kitchen rotation because Hala had been there all afternoon, her broad back bent over the cutting board, humming something under her breath while she worked — a woman who filled silence the way some people fill plates, automatically, without thinking about it. Bett had been grinding spices in the stone mortar when Jin had passed through with Melie on the way to the cistern, her wrist turning in steady circles, her face absent and focused at the same time, the look of someone doing a task so familiar it had become a form of thought.

The children ate together, as they had since the third or fourth day — a cluster of bodies on the lower step, plates balanced on knees, the older ones helping the younger ones without being told. Rimi Mera tore flatbread for Asha, who was sitting in Rimi's lap, four years old and unbothered by the fact that she was using a stranger's twelve-year-old as furniture. Kael held his own plate with the defensive grip of an eight-year-old who has been told he is old enough to manage. The Kondo children ate in their own tight group — Venn's influence, visible even at mealtimes in the way they kept their elbows close and their voices down — but Ori, the youngest at nine, kept glancing over at the other children with an expression that was harder to read than plain loneliness. She looked like someone watching a door she'd been told not to open.

Melie sat between Nori and Sem Dao. Sem was eleven, quiet, with the same compact build as his aunt Bett and the same economy of movement — he did things once and correctly, without wasted effort, as though he'd been taught that resources were finite and motion was a resource. He'd started saving a spot for Melie at meals without being asked, a small territorial gesture that Melie accepted without comment, the way children accept kindness they haven't been taught to question.

Jin ate standing, leaning against the kitchen overhang's wall, tearing bread and scooping lentils without tasting either. He'd taken some of the fried shallot rice and a smear of the brown-gold paste from one of the small bowls — rich, sweet, with a depth he vaguely identified as nut-based. He ate mechanically. The food was good. The Daos could cook. He chewed and swallowed and watched his daughter.

Melie was eating with both hands on her bread, tearing and dipping with the focused intensity of a child who takes food seriously. She dipped her torn bread into the brown-gold paste, took a large bite, chewed, dipped again. Nori was telling her something about a game they would play tomorrow, and Melie nodded while she chewed, her attention split between the conversation and the food with the particular greed of a six-year-old who hasn't fully learned to pretend she isn't hungry.

She paused. Her hand stopped halfway to her mouth. She looked at the bread in her fingers as if she'd never seen bread before.

"Melie?" Jin set his plate on the ledge.

She scratched her neck. Then her forearm. Both movements quick and distracted, the way you scratch at a mosquito bite without deciding to. She took another bite. She chewed. She scratched her neck again, harder.

"What's wrong?" He was already moving toward her, not running, but the five meters between the kitchen wall and the lower step felt like a distance he couldn't cross fast enough, each stride landing on stone that seemed to stretch.

"Itchy," Melie said. She scratched her forearm and Jin saw the skin — raised, blotchy, a red rash spreading from her wrist to the inside of her elbow in the time it took him to reach her.

He knelt beside her. "Show me."

She held up both arms. The rash was on both now, blooming outward from the insides of her elbows, welts rising under the skin like something pushing up from beneath. Her face was changing — the skin around her mouth puffing, her lower lip thickening, a flush climbing her cheeks that had nothing to do with the evening heat. She scratched at her throat with both hands.

"I feel funny," she said. Her voice was wrong. Thicker, squeezed, a six-year-old's voice forced through a narrowing passage.

Jin grabbed her plate. Bread, lentils, the brown-gold paste smeared across the edge. He looked at what she'd eaten and didn't know what was wrong and the not-knowing opened a hole in his chest that felt exactly like falling because he was her father and he was supposed to know. "What did you eat? Melie, what did you eat just now?"

"The bread. And the—" She coughed. A small cough, nothing, except beneath it was a wheeze Jin had never heard from her, a high thin scraping sound like air pulled through wet cloth. She coughed again. Her eyes were watering.

"Melie." He pulled her into his arms. She was light — she'd always been small for six, the kind of lightness that made strangers guess she was younger — and her body was hot against his chest, hotter than the evening warranted, and the rash was spreading down her neck now, red welts rising on skin he'd been washing with his own hands twenty minutes ago. "Breathe. Just breathe for me."

She tried. The wheeze was louder. Her fingers went to her throat, scratching, pulling at the skin as if she could widen the passage by hand.

"Something's wrong with her." He said it to the courtyard, to everyone, to anyone. Adults were turning. Suna had already stood from where she'd been eating with Deshi and the children. Hala was coming out of the kitchen, wiping her hands on her apron, her face shifting from confusion to something faster than confusion. "Something's wrong — she ate something, I don't know what—"

"Let me see." Bett was beside him. She'd been sitting four meters away with Nori, eating from her own plate, and now she was here and he hadn't seen her cross the space. Her hand went to Melie's face — not gently, efficiently, the practiced touch of someone who has assessed a child's airway before. She turned Melie's chin up, looked at her lips, pressed two fingers under the angle of Melie's jaw where the swelling was worst.

"Her throat's closing," Bett said. Her voice dropped, not to a whisper but to the low, clear register of someone who needs to be heard without adding noise to a situation that already had too much. "What did she eat?"

"Bread, lentils, the—"

Bett picked up Melie's plate. She touched the smear of paste with her finger and brought it to her nose. "Cashew," she said.

"She's eaten cashews before. She eats them all the time, she's never—"

"Not this." Bett set the plate down. She was moving fast now, her body stripped of everything that wasn't functional, her hands already reaching behind her as if she expected something to be placed in them. She looked at Melie's swelling face and her expression shifted — a hardening that wasn't coldness, that was something closer to the opposite of coldness, the focused intensity of someone recognizing a crisis they have met before. "Hala. The kit. Go."

Hala ran. Not walked, not hurried — ran, her feet slapping the stone, back into the kitchen area where the Daos kept their things, and the sound of containers being shoved aside carried across the courtyard.

"What's happening to her?" Jin's voice broke on the second word. Melie was wheezing against his chest, each breath a whistle through a gap that was narrowing by the second, and her fingers were clawing at her throat hard enough to leave red marks on skin that was already welted, and her eyes were swollen to slits and he could feel her ribs heaving against his forearms, each inhalation pulling her ribcage wide as her body fought for air that wasn't coming fast enough.

"Anaphylaxis." Bett said the word with the flatness of someone naming a thing she has lived alongside. "Tree-nut allergy. Cashew. It's our family's allergy. Sem has it. My son had it."

The words didn't arrange themselves into sense. His daughter was choking in his arms and this woman he'd spoken to three times in a week was saying something about families and allergies and a son, and Melie was making a sound now — a high, thin, aspirated whistle on each inhale, air forced through a space the width of a straw — and the sound was the most terrifying thing Jin had ever heard because it was the sound of something that could stop.

"Hold her still." Bett took Melie from his arms. She didn't ask. She lifted her with the competence of someone who has carried a struggling child through a medical crisis — one arm under her back, the other supporting her head to keep the airway as straight as possible. She set Melie on the stone step, propped against her own bent knee. "Melie, I know it's scary. Don't scratch. Look at me."

Melie looked at her. Her eyes were wet and swollen and terrified, her face a stranger's face, puffed and distorted, and her hands were still going for her throat. Bett caught both small wrists in one hand and held them against Melie's chest. "You're going to breathe. It's tight but you're going to breathe. Look at me."

Hala came back with a tin box — battered, scratched, a dent in the lid that had been hammered mostly flat. Bett opened it one-handed, her other hand still holding Melie's wrists. Inside: a small glass vial and a syringe wrapped in white cloth. The cloth was clean and carefully ironed, the creases sharp, and this was the detail that would stay with Jin afterward — the ironed cloth, the care taken with it, the particular devotion of someone who maintained a medical kit the way another person might maintain a shrine.

Bett drew the dose with the same economy she brought to everything else. She did not hesitate over the measurement. She did not double-check the vial. Her hands moved with the specific confidence of a person who has done this enough times that the dosage lives in her fingers, not in her head.

"Epinephrine," she said to Jin. She didn't look at him. Her eyes were on Melie's face, reading the swelling, measuring the progression with an attention that was clinical and also something more than clinical — something personal and hard-earned. "It'll open her airway. Her heart's going to race. It'll scare her but it's the drug working."

"Do it." He didn't know what epinephrine was. He didn't care what it was. Melie was wheezing and her lips were going grey and her fingernails were digging into Bett's palm where Bett held her wrists and Jin would have let this woman pour fire into his daughter's body if it meant the whistling would stop.

Bett pushed Melie's shirt up and exposed her outer thigh — the skin there still clear of rash, pale and goosebumped in the cooling air. She drove the needle in. Not tentatively, not with the caution of someone doing something for the first time. She pressed the plunger with the steady force of practice, and then she held the needle in place and counted — silently, her lips moving, her thumb keeping the muscle steady while the drug entered.

She withdrew the needle and set the syringe on the stone. "Now we wait."

The courtyard was quiet. Forty-odd people, some standing and some still sitting with plates in their laps, watching. Suna had her hand on Asha's head, holding her daughter against her hip. Hala stood behind Bett with her arms crossed over her chest, her face tight with something fiercer than worry — the look of a woman who has watched her sister do this before and never gotten used to it. The Kondo children had stopped eating. Old Peyam had put down his pipe. Nori Dao was standing exactly where Melie had been sitting a minute ago, holding the plate Melie had been eating from, her face white and absolutely still, a seven-year-old who understood what was happening because she had grown up understanding it.

Melie breathed. The whistle was still there. Bett's palm was flat between Melie's shoulder blades, feeling each breath — checking the vibration, the depth, the resistance, the way you'd feel a pipe for blockage, your hand reading what your ears couldn't measure. Twenty seconds. Thirty. Jin counted with her, or tried to, but the numbers kept slipping because his hands were shaking and his jaw was locked and the sound of that whistle was still there, still narrowing, still the width of a straw—

And then it changed. Loosened. Deepened. The sound of a passage widening by millimeters, the wheeze dropping half a tone, and Bett's hand went still on Melie's back.

"There," Bett said.

Melie took a breath that sounded like breathing. Then another. The swelling around her eyes was still bad — her face still looked distorted, unfamiliar — but the color was coming back into her lips and her breathing was slowing from panicked shallow gasps to something Jin's body recognized before his mind did: she was getting air. She was going to keep getting air.

"Papa," Melie said. Her voice was hoarse and her eyes were still nearly shut. "My heart's going really fast."

"I know. That's the medicine. It's supposed to do that." He was repeating what Bett had told him. He was reaching for Melie, pulling her from Bett's knee into his own arms, and she came — hot and shaking and alive, her heart hammering against his chest through both their shirts, her breathing ragged but open, open, the air going in and coming out. He pressed his face into her damp hair and smelled the harsh soap from the cistern and the faint residual sweetness of the cashew paste and his own sweat, and he held her, and his hands were shaking so badly that he could feel them vibrating against her back like something mechanical.

"She can't eat tree nuts," Bett said. She was still sitting on the step, her hands on her thighs, the syringe on the stone beside her. The ironed cloth had come open and the glass vial caught the last daylight, a small bright point. Her voice was steady but her jaw was set in a way that Jin recognized as effort — steadiness as a choice, not a condition. "Cashews, pistachios, walnuts. Almonds are sometimes safe but I wouldn't test it here. Not without a clinic."

"She's never—" Jin's jaw locked. The consonant caught and held, the old hesitation, and for a moment he couldn't make the word come out. He pressed his mouth against Melie's hair and waited for his throat to release. "She's never been allergic. To anything. She eats everything."

"She is now," Bett said.

The flatness of it. Not cruel. Not bewildered. Not wrapped in any of the language the compound had been generating for a week about manifestations and connections and what the naming revealed about families. Just the fact, set down on the stone beside the syringe and the vial and the ironed cloth: your daughter has this thing now. It is real. It will not go away because it shouldn't be there.

Hala knelt beside Bett and put a hand on her sister's shoulder. Bett didn't lean into the touch. She didn't acknowledge it except by not pulling away. She sat on the stone step with Hala's hand on her shoulder and looked at Melie breathing in Jin's arms, and Jin saw her gaze go to the spot on Melie's thigh where the needle had entered — a small red dot, already closing, unremarkable. Whatever Bett saw in that dot lived in a place Jin didn't have access to. He understood, without knowing the details, that she had done this before for someone who was not here anymore.

"I've got one more dose," Bett said. She nodded at the tin. "After that we'd need to get to a clinic, and the road's not open."

"What if it happens again?"

"Don't let her eat tree nuts."

"I don't know what has tree nuts in it." His voice was rising and he couldn't stop it — the flood, the loss of volume control, the specific humiliation of a man unable to protect his child from a bowl of paste at dinner. "I've never had to know. She ate cashews at home, I gave her cashews, I put them in her — she's eaten them her whole—"

"So come to me before she eats." Bett stood. She brushed the stone dust from her knees — one brisk pass of each hand, no fuss. The syringe went back into the tin, the vial beside it, the cloth folded over both with the same careful creases. She closed the lid and tucked the tin under her arm. "I've been managing this allergy for eleven years. Sem's had it since he was born. I can tell you what's safe and what's not."

Jin stared at her. He was holding Melie against his chest and his daughter's heart was still hammering and this woman was offering to manage what he couldn't manage, to know what he didn't know, and the offer was made without softness, without the gentle tone people used when they felt sorry for you. It was made the way you'd hand someone a tool: here, you need this, take it.

"Why does she have your family's allergy?" He didn't mean it as accusation. He meant it as the question it was — the thing he could not hold inside his body, because his daughter had just had her throat close from a condition she didn't have yesterday, a condition that belonged to strangers, and a woman he barely knew had saved her with a needle and a counted silence and hands that knew exactly where to push the plunger.

Bett looked at him. The guarded surface was back — the face she showed the compound, economical, closed. But the muscles around her eyes were tight in a way that had nothing to do with composure and everything to do with something she was choosing not to say.

"I don't know why," she said. "I just know what it looks like."

She climbed the steps. Hala followed, her hand briefly touching the back of Bett's arm as they went — a sisterly gesture, practiced and unremarkable, the habit of two women who had been looking after each other for a long time. Nori was still holding Melie's plate. Bett took it from her daughter's hands as she passed — gently, the way you take something with an edge from a child — and set it face-down on the ledge where no one else would eat from it.

The courtyard emptied in stages. The crisis was already becoming the thing people would talk about in low voices after the children slept — did you see it, how fast, the Dao woman's kit, how did she know. Suna caught Jin's eye as she passed with Asha on her hip and Rimi trailing behind. She didn't speak. She gave him the look of a parent who has just watched another parent's worst moment and is recalculating — her children, the food, what else might change without warning, whether the world she thought she understood had shifted again beneath all of them.

Jin sat on the step with Melie in his arms. Her heart was still going too fast. He could feel it through her ribs, a hummingbird tremor against his chest, and he kept hearing Bett's voice — it's the drug working, it'll scare her but it's working — and repeating it to himself as if the repetition could reach whatever part of him was still falling through the hole that had opened when he heard that first wheeze. Her breathing was steady now. Regular. The swelling was going down, slowly, the puffiness around her eyes receding enough that she looked like herself again — the gap teeth, the dark hair he'd just washed, the chin that was Reva's chin. He saw it every time. Every single time.

"Papa," she said. Her voice was scratchy and small. "My throat still feels weird."

"It'll get better. The medicine's still working."

"How did she know what to do?"

"Because someone in her family has the same thing you have."

"But I don't have it. I ate cashews at Auntie's house. I ate them loads of times."

"I know."

"So why did my throat do that?"

He didn't have an answer. He had a lie about her dead mother and the copper pot he'd scrubbed that afternoon and the memory of her laughing on the terrace twenty minutes before her throat closed, and he had the present fact of her body in his arms — hot, trembling, breathing — and a debt he owed to a woman who'd known what was happening because her family carried the same thing, her child, her nephew, her blood, and now his daughter's blood too, impossibly, undeniably, a connection written in the body that no story he could construct would explain away.

"I don't know," he said.

Melie pressed her face into his shirt and didn't ask again.

The custodians' bell rang for evening prayers. The compound moved around them. Somewhere above, Bett's voice carried briefly — saying something to Nori, ordinary words, a mother's or an aunt's evening instructions — and then the compound settled into its nightly routine of mats and lamps and the sound of rain beginning again on stone.

Later, when Jin carried Melie to their sleeping space and laid her down and sat beside her with his back against the wall, he found the tin box on the floor next to their mat. Bett had left it without telling him. No note, no instructions, no explanation. Just the dented tin with the ironed cloth inside and the single remaining dose of the drug that would keep his daughter breathing if this new, impossible thing in her body decided to close her throat again.

He sat with the tin in his hands and listened to Melie breathe and understood something with the particular clarity that comes only when terror has passed and left the ground rearranged. He could not keep her safe alone. He had never been able to keep her safe alone. The lie he'd built — the dead wife, the self-sufficient father, the man who needed nothing from anyone — was exactly as strong as Melie's certainty that she could eat cashews. Both had been true this morning. Neither was true tonight.

Around him the compound breathed. Rain on stone, mats shifting, a baby crying somewhere — Ada, the Okoye infant, her small voice carrying through the thin walls and then stopping as someone picked her up. The flood's steady rush from the valley below, unchanged, indifferent. Melie's breathing beside him, even and open.

His jaw locked on nothing — no word, no thought he was trying to form, just the old hesitation firing on its own, the body's habit of catching before speech. He pressed his thumbs against the dented lid of the tin and held it in his lap and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 7

The naming bell was not beautiful. Leni had settled this on the third day and nothing since had changed her mind. It was a flat, bossy sound — bronze on bronze with no sustain to it, no ring, just the blunt announcement that someone wanted your attention and didn't care whether you wanted to give it. It reminded her of the substitute teachers at her old school, the ones who rapped the desk with a ruler because they'd already lost the room and had nothing else.

Day nine. She knew because she'd been counting in her journal, a line drawn under each date at the bottom of the page, and because Tomas had asked that morning. He'd wanted the exact number. She'd said nine and he'd said *that's a long time* with the flat certainty of a ten-year-old stating a fact, and she'd said *yeah* and he'd gone back to throwing stones at the courtyard wall.

She unfolded herself from the steps and went inside.

The main hall had developed a seating geography nobody had assigned but everybody maintained. The Kondos filled the right side — twelve of them, impossible to miss, a family that seemed to travel by census count. Venn sat at the end nearest the open wall where you could see the valley and the brown water below, a position that let him look outward when the naming bored him and inward when it threatened him. Tila sat beside him, her hands in her lap, her posture a careful complement to his — not subservient but orbiting, the practiced geometry of three decades with a man who took up a great deal of space. The Meras anchored the left, near the kitchen passage. Deshi sat with her back very straight, as if poor posture were a moral failing, and old Peyam beside her with his prayer beads wound around one wrist, his eyes half-closed even before the naming started. Suna was behind them with Kael and Rimi and Asha, organizing children's positions with the small efficient gestures of someone who could manage a crisis and braid hair at the same time. The Daos sat in the middle: Hala always with both hands flat on the bench beside her, half-ready to stand, the posture of someone who had been getting between her younger sister and trouble for forty-four years and saw no reason to stop now. Bett next to her, arms crossed, face closed. The Leos took the back wall. Cade kept Pik between himself and Wynn, a quiet triangle of protection that Leni had noticed from the first day — the way Cade's hand went to Pik's shoulder when anyone official spoke, the way Wynn leaned slightly toward the boy as if proximity were a form of legal claim. Vera, Cade's mother, sat at the end of their bench, a gray-haired woman with steady hands who carried a sewing needle in her front pocket and used it the way other people used worry beads — Leni had watched her mend three separate garments since they'd arrived, always with tiny, precise stitches, always while listening to something she wasn't supposed to comment on.

Jin and Melie sat wherever there was room, which these days meant near the Daos. Since the allergic reaction, Bett checked every plate before Melie touched it. Jin accepted this with the stiff courtesy of a man who didn't know how to say *thank you for keeping my daughter alive* without the words costing more than he could afford.

The Okoyes sat wherever Bram decided to sit. Today, that was the front.

Leni clocked it immediately. Her father usually chose the middle — close enough to engage, far enough to observe, the teacher's instinct for sight lines. Front-row Bram was argument-Bram. She'd seen the posture at parent-teacher nights, at the town-council meeting he'd dragged her to when she was twelve, at the kitchen table when her parents argued about whether to leave the city. His shoulders came forward and his chin dropped and his hands went into motion, the left circling loosely while the right chopped air for emphasis. She'd once timed the hand-chops at a school-board meeting: three per sentence when he was warming up, five or six when he meant it.

Her mother sat beside him with Ada on her chest. Nessa's face wore the careful blankness that meant she'd tried to talk him out of whatever this was and failed. She was picking lint off Ada's blanket in small, methodical strokes — the cleaning that wasn't really cleaning, just something to do with her hands while she waited for the argument she couldn't prevent.

Fasa came in from the custodians' walkway, followed by Olet. She carried the naming ledger — cloth-bound, water-stained along the spine — held against her chest with both hands, the grip of someone who had carried this same object a thousand times and no longer noticed its weight. She took her place at the center of the room and waited.

The conversations thinned. They always did. Fasa had a quality of silence that worked better than volume — she didn't shush anyone, didn't raise a hand, just stood there with her attention aimed at the room like a lens, and people stopped talking because the quiet she carried was heavier than whatever they'd been saying.

"The naming gathers us," Fasa said. "We speak what is true. We hear what is spoken."

Leni could have said it in her sleep by now. The formula was always the same. What changed was what came after.

Her father stood up.

The room noticed. Standing during the opening was new. Fasa paused with the ledger half-open, her eyes going to Bram with the focused attention of someone who had, perhaps, been waiting for this.

"Before we continue," Bram said, "I want the room to consider what we're being asked to do."

His voice was steady — the teacher's voice, calibrated to carry without shouting, clear enough to reach the back wall where the Leos sat. Leni watched his left hand begin its circle.

"Nine days ago, the road flooded and we came here because there was nowhere else to go. The custodians took us in. They've fed us, given us shelter, and I'm grateful for that — genuinely grateful."

The *genuinely* was his setup word. She'd heard it a hundred times. It meant the reversal was two sentences away.

"But the naming isn't hospitality. It's a requirement. Every day we stand in this room and declare our families — our composition, our relationships, our private circumstances — in front of strangers. And each time the questions go deeper. Who your spouse is. Where they are. Whether your children are biologically yours. Whether the family you've described is the family you actually have."

He paused. Jin's face went still — a quick tension around his eyes, then nothing, the careful blank of a man who had spent nine days keeping his expression under control. Across the room Cade shifted on the bench and rested his arm along the wall behind Wynn, a motion so deliberately casual it announced itself.

"Outside this shrine," Bram continued, and his voice picked up the slightly faster cadence Leni recognized as conviction — not anger yet, but the gathering force of an argument he'd been building for days — "there is a government requiring identity papers. Ethnic registration. Movement permits tied to documented family structure. People are being stopped at checkpoints and made to prove who they are, who they belong to, whether their papers match their faces. That's why most of us are here. We were trying to get away from exactly that system."

His right hand chopped once. Twice.

"The naming is the same impulse in different clothing. It asks the same questions the state asks. It requires the same public declarations. And it carries the same implicit threat — cooperate or face consequences. The only difference is the state uses soldiers and checkpoints, and the shrine uses" — he paused, choosing — "tradition."

The room held its breath. Not because the argument was shocking — Leni suspected most of the adults had thought some version of it — but because someone had finally said it aloud, in front of the custodians, with his hands moving and his teacher-voice carrying and his glasses catching the grey light from the open wall.

Bram turned to Olet. The head custodian stood where he always stood — slightly apart from Fasa, near the stone pillar, with his hands at his sides and his face arranged in the same expression it always held, which was no expression at all. Olet's face was a policy. It did not change for arguments, for children crying, for the sound of the flood below them. It did not change now.

"I'm asking you to address this directly," Bram said. "Not the formula, not the tradition. The substance. Why should these families be required to declare their private business to you?"

Olet was silent for a moment. Not a dramatic silence — Leni had learned to distinguish Olet's silences from the pauses people used for effect. His were simply empty. The space where urgency should have been, and wasn't.

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said. "Families who come to the shrine participate in the naming. This has been so for longer than I have served here, and for longer than anyone living remembers."

"That's not an answer," Bram said. "That's a description. I'm asking you to justify — "

"The naming does not require justification. It requires participation."

Leni watched her father's hands stop moving. Just for a second. Then the left one started again, faster.

"You don't hear how that sounds? You genuinely don't hear yourself?"

"I hear you clearly, Bram Okoye. You have spoken. I have answered."

The thing was — and this was what Leni couldn't stop tracking — her father was right. She could follow his argument the way she could follow a proof in math: premise, evidence, conclusion, and the conclusion held. The naming *was* identity registration. The shrine *was* doing what the government did, just with a leather-bound book and a ritual opening instead of a checkpoint and a stamp.

But Olet wasn't arguing. He wasn't engaging with the logic at all. He was describing the way things were the way you'd describe weather — not defending it, not justifying it, just stating it. And description didn't need to defeat argument because it wasn't playing the same game. Her father was trying to pick a lock, and the lock wasn't locked — it was welded shut. There was nothing to pick.

"If a family refuses to participate," Bram said — and now his voice was quieter, which Leni recognized as danger, the dropping register that meant he was about to ask the question he already knew the answer to — "what happens?"

"They may stay," Olet said.

"And?"

"They may stay."

"That's it? No consequences?"

Olet's pause was longer this time. A fraction of a second, maybe two. Leni noticed because she'd been timing his pauses since the second day — not consciously, at first, but then as a kind of practice, a way to measure what the man didn't say by how long he didn't say it.

"The naming and the common stores are one provision," Olet said. "The shrine offers both together. Families who do not participate in the naming may continue to stay at the shrine. They cannot draw from the common food stores."

The room changed. Not the sound — the quality of the silence shifted, from attention to arithmetic. Leni watched it happen on face after face: people counting mouths, counting days, counting what they had left in their bags. Suna uncrossed her arms and put one hand on Rimi's shoulder, which was not comfort but inventory — the automatic gesture of a woman confirming her children were still in reach. Venn looked down the row of Kondos — the quick, assessing scan of a man counting the mouths he was responsible for. Tila's thumb went still on her wedding ring. Hala's hands pressed harder against the bench. Nessa pulled Ada closer and the baby made a small sound of protest at the tightening.

"You're saying you'll starve us into compliance," Bram said.

"I am saying what the shrine provides and the terms on which it provides it."

"That's coercion. You understand that? You're using food as — "

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said, and the repetition landed with a flatness that was worse than any rebuttal. He had said what he was going to say. He would go on saying it. The room could object as long as it wanted and his answer would not change, because it was not an answer — it was the shape of the wall.

Fasa had not moved. She stood with the ledger against her chest and her eyes on Bram, and for a moment Leni saw something cross her face that she couldn't quite name. Not agreement with Bram. Not disagreement with Olet. Something more private — a flinch, almost, as if the mechanism she'd served for twenty years had been opened in front of her and the workings were uglier than she'd remembered. It lasted half a second. Then Fasa's face settled back into the custodian expression — calm, attentive, present — and the moment was gone.

"If there are no further questions," Fasa said, "the naming will continue."

"There are further questions," Bram said.

"They will be heard. The naming hears what is spoken."

Bram looked at Fasa, then at Olet, then at the room. His left hand closed and opened. He pushed his glasses up with one knuckle and pressed his fingers against his eyelids — the gesture he made at home when the internet failed for the third time in a week, not dramatic, just the specific exhaustion of fighting something that wouldn't acknowledge the fight.

Nessa reached up and touched his hand. Just that. Not pulling him down, not squeezing — her fingers against the back of his wrist, and Leni could read the whole conversation in the touch: *sit down, this isn't working, the baby needs to eat, you've said what you came to say.*

He sat down.

&nbsp;

Hala was the first to speak after the custodians left. She waited until the covered walkway's door closed behind them, then said, "Well, shit," in a flat voice that did more work than Bram's entire argument.

Nobody suggested leaving the hall. Moving would have meant the conversation was over, and nobody was ready for that — even if nobody knew what to do with it while it was still happening.

"We don't comply," Bram said. He was leaning forward, elbows on his knees, glasses pushed up into his hair. Without the glasses his face looked younger and more uncertain, which was something Leni did not want to see right now. "All seven families refuse together. If we refuse collectively, they can't enforce it. They're five people. We're forty-three."

"We're forty-three people who can't leave," Suna said from the Mera side of the room. "That's not the same as forty-three people with options."

"If we refuse as a body — "

"We are not a body," Venn said. His voice carried across the hall with the weight of a man who did not need to raise it to fill a room. "We are seven families. My family is twelve. How long do you propose we hold out?"

He didn't wait for an answer.

"I will tell you how long. Our provisions lasted until the fifth day. Everything we've eaten since then has come from the common stores. Mava is seventy-three. Ori is nine. There are four other children under sixteen in my family alone. If you want to stand on principle, Okoye, stand on it. But not with my family's meals."

"And that's exactly what they're counting on," Bram said. His sentences were getting longer, the subordinate clauses multiplying — Leni could hear the teacher in him building the argument even as the argument was losing, the reflex of a man who believed that a well-constructed case should still matter. "The leverage works precisely because the larger families can't afford to resist. Which means the larger families are being punished for — "

"Don't lecture me about my family's size."

"I'm not — "

"My family is not your argument."

"Our provisions are gone too," Suna said, cutting between them with the neutral efficiency she brought to everything — settling children, distributing blankets, stopping fights before they fully committed. "We have the children's emergency food. Dried fruit, some biscuits. Two days' worth if I stretch it. Maybe three."

"The Parras have nothing," Hala said. She looked toward the back of the hall where Jin stood against the wall with Melie beside him. Jin gave a nod so slight it was more a contraction of muscle than a gesture. Melie was braiding and unbraiding a strand of her own hair with one hand, the other locked in Jin's, her face carrying the particular opacity of a child who knows the adults are talking about something bad and has decided not to ask.

"We've got some things," Hala continued. "Dried fish, some oil. Bett packed for a longer trip than the rest of us." She didn't look at Bett as she said it. Bett sat with her arms folded, face in its public setting: guarded, level, offering nothing she hadn't chosen to offer. "But it's for four. Not forty-three. And it won't last."

Vera Leo's needle paused in the cloth of Pik's shirt. She looked at Cade, and Cade looked at Wynn, and between the three of them passed the quick, well-practiced glance of people who had spent years navigating systems that weren't designed for them. They would comply. Leni could see it in the way Cade's hand settled on Pik's back — the steady pressure of a man who had already done the calculation and arrived at the only answer that kept his family fed. One more set of questions to answer. At least these didn't involve courts or paperwork.

"So there's nothing," Bram said. He looked around the room, and Leni could see him counting — not mouths but allies. She could have saved him the trouble. She'd been counting since he stood up.

Jin: uninvolved. His face was a closed shop. He wasn't going to fight anyone about anything. Melie needed food and he would do whatever the food required.

Suna: arms crossed again, her eyes moving between Bram and Venn and the empty spot where Olet had stood. She was running her own kind of math — three children, an uncle, a mother-in-law, a budget of trust she was spending carefully.

Venn: already decided. The Kondos would comply because the Kondos were twelve people heading to a wedding, and Venn Kondo was not going to let his family go hungry on a stranger's principle. He sat with his hands on his knees and his shoulders squared and his face wearing the expression of a man who had never been confused about the relationship between being right and eating.

Deshi had been watching from the Mera side of the room with her chin slightly raised, the posture of a woman who was going to say something and was choosing her moment.

"You thought," Deshi said, and her voice had the dry, scoring quality that made Leni glad she wasn't on the receiving end, "that the custodians who feed us, house us, and control every hour of every day were offering the naming as a gentle invitation? A polite request we could decline?"

The room absorbed it. Deshi's sharpness had a clarifying effect — not because she was right in a way Bram was wrong, but because she was right in a way that made everyone's surprise look naive. They had all been participating in the naming for nine days. They had all eaten from the common stores. The transaction had been in place the entire time. The only thing that had changed was that someone had spoken its name aloud.

"It's coercion," Bram said. He said it to the room, not to Deshi, and his voice had gone flat in a way that Leni recognized — the register he dropped into when he stopped trying to persuade and started trying to mark something permanent. "I want that stated clearly. What we're doing when we comply isn't consent. It's compliance under material duress, and I want that acknowledged."

"Whose record?" Venn asked.

Bram looked at him. For a moment neither man spoke, and Leni saw something pass between them that wasn't hostility — more like the grim mutual recognition of two people who understood power from opposite ends. Venn understood power because he held it, inside the borders of his family. Bram understood power because he'd spent his life trying to argue against it, which required understanding the exact shape of the thing you couldn't move.

"Mine," Bram said.

Nessa's hand was on his arm. She didn't say anything. She didn't need to. The conversation was the touch: *you've made your point, you've put it on record, now let it go because Ada is going to need to eat in two hours and there is nothing in our bags.*

"We need to talk about tomorrow," Suna said. She said it the way she said things that needed saying — without emphasis, without ceremony, as if the practicalities were a relief after the principles. "The naming will happen. We should figure out what we're willing to say and what we're not."

The conversation shifted then. Became practical, bounded — who would speak, how much to offer, whether to coordinate their answers or let each family handle it alone. It was easier than principles because it had edges. You could hold it.

Leni stopped listening. Not because she didn't care, but because she already knew how it would end. The families would comply. The resentment would sit in the room like smoke from the kitchen fire — present, ambient, stinging the eyes — but they would comply, because the alternative was watching their children go hungry over an argument that the other side refused to have.

&nbsp;

The courtyard was cooler. The clouds were low and soaked and the air had that heavy wet-cloth feeling that meant rain was close but hadn't committed. From somewhere behind the kitchen overhang came the sound of the custodians' assistant rinsing pots — the scrape of metal on stone, unhurried, as if nothing had happened, as if nothing had changed. Leni sat on the top step and pulled her knees up and wrapped her arms around them.

Tomas was below her, throwing stones at the wall. He'd arranged them in a line by size — smallest to largest, a ten-year-old's ritual of order in a place that offered very little. The thud of stone hitting stone was steady, almost soothing. He threw with his whole body, the way boys did, more force than accuracy.

"What happened in there?" he asked, without looking up.

"Nothing. Grown-up stuff."

"Dad was arguing."

"Yeah."

"Did he win?"

She watched him throw. Small stone, medium stone, larger stone. Retrieve, reset, repeat. His focus was complete and unconcerned and she envied it with a sharpness that surprised her.

"It wasn't that kind of argument," she said.

Tomas considered this. He threw the next stone and it hit the wall with a crack and bounced into the gutter that ran along the courtyard's edge. He watched it roll, then looked up at her.

"Like when he argued with the landlord?"

"Sort of."

"He didn't win that one either."

"No," Leni said. "He didn't."

Tomas nodded and went back to his stones. The simple economy of a ten-year-old's conclusions: either you won or you didn't, and then you threw the next rock. She wished it were that clean.

Below the steps, below the courtyard, below the shrine's stone walls, the valley was brown water all the way to the tree line. The road they'd driven in on was invisible — erased, the seven converging paths submerged under a flat expanse of flood that reflected the clouds and gave back nothing. Somewhere under that water was the world they'd come from. Bram's school, their apartment, the street where Leni used to walk to the bus stop in the mornings with her earbuds in, the particular and irretrievable normalcy of a life that hadn't required you to sit in a stone compound and watch your father lose to a man who didn't bother to win.

She thought about her journal. She'd write tonight, same as every night — on her mat, oil lamp turned low so her mother wouldn't tell her to sleep, her pen moving across the page in handwriting that got worse as the dark got deeper. She'd been recording the days with the scrupulous attention of someone who didn't know what the record was for. The details of the naming, who said what, who sat where, the arguments and their outcomes. But tonight would be different. Tonight the thing she needed to write wasn't a detail. It was a shape — the specific shape of watching someone you loved be entirely correct about something and have it change nothing.

Her father had seen the naming for what it was. He'd built the argument and delivered it and the argument was sound, and the custodians had looked at him and said *the naming is the shrine's practice* and that was the end. Not because the argument was wrong. Because arguments weren't the currency. The currency was rice and oil and dried fish and the twelve Kondos who needed feeding and the baby who'd need to eat in two hours and the flood that kept them all on this hill where the only people with food were the people with the bell.

The scariest part wasn't the food. The food was a problem that would solve itself one way or another — they'd comply, they'd eat, they'd stand in the hall and declare themselves and the bell would ring and the day would turn over and nothing would change except the size of what they'd agreed to. The scariest part was the thing underneath, the thing her father's whole argument had been reaching for without quite getting hold of: that you could see a thing for exactly what it was, name it precisely, construct every reason it was wrong, and it could still be done to you. Seeing clearly wasn't the same as being safe. Being right wasn't a door you could walk through. It was just a window.

Below them the flood made its constant sound — the steady rush of water over a road that no one could use. Tomas threw his last stone and it hit the wall dead center and fell straight down.

The bell would ring again tomorrow.


# Chapter 8

Suna had been watching Tomas Okoye for two days before she understood what she was watching.

It started the morning after the families agreed to keep sitting for the naming. Not agreed — submitted. Bram Okoye had argued his case with a teacher's precision and it hadn't mattered, because the custodians held the food stores and the Kondos had twelve mouths and the calculation was simple and humiliating: you eat, therefore you comply. The compound's mood since then had been the mood of people performing cooperation that fooled no one, and against that backdrop Suna noticed Tomas because noticing things was how she managed, and managing was how she stayed upright.

He was ten, slim, with his mother's round face but his father's long fingers — the kind of hands a boy didn't grow into until fifteen or sixteen, so that now they looked borrowed, oversized, as if he'd been assembled from parts that hadn't consulted each other. He played with the younger children more than most ten-year-olds would tolerate, carrying baby Ada on his hip when Nessa needed both hands, showing Kael how to stack pebbles without using the flat ones as a base. His patience with smaller children looked to Suna like the competence of a boy conscripted early into helping — not resentful patience, but resigned patience, the kind that came from being the older sibling of a baby who still woke three times a night.

The hesitation appeared at the morning meal. Or the morning before. She couldn't pin it, which bothered her, because Suna tracked moments the way other women tracked weather. She tracked what Asha ate and how much Kael drank and whether Rimi had slept on her left side again, which meant her shoulder would ache by noon and she'd be sullen until someone acknowledged it. She tracked the cistern's water level and the number of flatbreads remaining from the previous evening and which families had contributed labor to the kitchen that day and which had found reasons not to. She tracked other people's children too, not from sentiment but from the same instinct that made her count the rice portions before every meal — more people to account for meant more variables, and Suna handled variables by counting them.

So she couldn't say exactly when Tomas started catching on his consonants.

He was asking Kael to pass the water cup, and the word stuck. Not a stutter — Suna had grown up with a cousin who stammered on vowels, and she knew the sound of it, the way a stutterer's whole face participated in the effort. This was different. Tomas's jaw locked briefly before the hard consonant, the muscle along his cheek tightening for less than a second, and then the word released with a slight elongation of the first syllable. *C-cup.* A catch and release. A small mechanical failure in a mouth that had been working fine for ten years.

She knew the mechanism. She had been listening to it for ten days from the mouth of Jin Parra.

Jin's hesitation was subtle enough that most people seemed not to register it, or else they folded it into the general impression of a man who spoke as little as possible. Suna had noticed on the second day. She noticed speech the way she noticed how a person held a knife or whether they washed their hands before touching the communal bread — not from judgment, but from the restless inventory that ran beneath her waking life like water under paving stones. Jin caught on certain consonants, the hard ones, the plosives, the sounds that required the jaw to close and open with force. C, k, p, t. His jaw would lock for a fraction of a second, the muscle along his cheek visible beneath the skin, and then the word would emerge slightly stretched, as if he'd had to push it through a space that had narrowed without warning.

Tomas was doing the same thing. The same consonants. The same small muscular event along the jaw.

Suna set down the flatbread she'd been pulling apart for Asha and watched. Tomas was talking to Melie now — Jin's daughter, six, who had attached herself to the older boy with the uncritical loyalty of a child who recognized someone kind. He was explaining a rule of the pebble game, and the word *put* stuck in his mouth the same way. The catch. The elongation. The jaw unlocking like a hinge that had briefly rusted.

Across the morning circle, Nessa Okoye sat with Ada in her lap, bouncing the baby absently on one knee. She was watching her son. Nessa's face had no defense — never had, as far as Suna could tell. Fear, hope, confusion, tenderness — everything moved across her features without permission, which made her easy to read and hard to look at, because what Suna saw in her face right now was not general worry. It was recognition. Specific, consonant-by-consonant recognition.

Their eyes met. Suna looked away first, but not before she'd registered the question in Nessa's expression: *You see it too?*

She saw it. Four families now. The Meras and the Kondos, through Asha's gesture and Mava's dead sister's word. The Parras and the Daos, through Melie's impossible allergy. And now the Okoyes — the family of the man who had stood in the naming hall and argued, correctly and uselessly, that the ritual was coercion — connected to the Parras through a ten-year-old boy whose jaw had learned to lock on consonants he'd never had trouble with before.

Five families. Five out of seven.

Suna finished pulling apart the bread and handed the pieces to Asha, who received them with both hands and ate with her mouth open, watching her mother with the unreadable calm of a four-year-old who did not know that her own body had already become evidence.

---

That afternoon the rain came in hard and the children compressed into the main hall. Twenty-one of them, ranging from Ada's one year to Fen Tesh's sixteen, occupying the stone floor in a shifting mass of noise and negotiation while the adults arranged themselves along the walls and tried not to contribute to the volume. Deshi had positioned herself near the cold cooking fire — an act of territorial habit that no longer drew comment from anyone except Rimi, who rolled her eyes with the practiced contempt of a twelve-year-old who had decided that adults were a failing species.

Suna stationed herself where she could see the children's clump without sitting in it. The older ones — Leni, Fen, Raya Dao — maintained their adolescent separateness near the walls, performing the studied indifference of teenagers trapped with younger children they considered beneath their dignity. The younger ones pooled at the center in a shifting economy of games and grievances. Tomas was in the middle, as usual, mediating a dispute between Kael and Sem Dao over a set of smooth stones that had become, through the alchemy of confinement, more valuable than anything the adults could offer.

She watched Tomas talk. She counted the hesitations. Three in ten minutes, all on hard consonants. *T-take it back. I already p-put it there. C-come on, Sem.* The children around him didn't seem to notice, or processed it as unremarkable the way children processed most things — a man limped, a woman hummed, a boy started catching on his k's. The world was full of such things if you were eight or six.

Jin Parra was sitting against the far wall with his knees drawn up, Melie leaning against his side, her thumb in her mouth. He was watching the children with the specific vigilance of a man whose daughter's body had already done something impossible — whose daughter's throat had swelled shut from a food she'd eaten her whole life, claiming an allergy that belonged to another family. His jaw worked slightly as he watched, the unconscious motion of a habit he'd carried since adolescence.

The same motion Tomas was learning.

Suna looked at Jin's jaw, then at Tomas's jaw, then at Nessa. Nessa was nursing Ada in the corner with a cloth draped over her shoulder, her free hand resting on her thigh. Her fingers were opening and closing in a slow rhythm that had nothing to do with the baby and everything to do with the boy across the room whose mouth was becoming someone else's.

---

The next morning was the first without rain since the fourth day. The clouds didn't clear — they thinned, turning the light from gray to a diffuse white that made the courtyard stones steam as the moisture lifted. The children spilled outside with the urgency of animals released from a hold, and even Deshi stood at the edge of the covered walkway and lifted her face to the warm air with an expression that, on anyone else, would have been called relief.
