Suna was wringing laundry at the cistern. The work was necessary and constant — forty-three people produced a volume of soiled fabric that had become the compound's most tangible communal problem, a problem she addressed with the same methodical attention she brought to everything else because the alternative was to address nothing, to let the vertigo underneath the method have its way. She wrung a shirt of Kael's until her knuckles whitened against the wet fabric, draped it over the stone lip, picked up the next piece.

Kiri Tesh was sitting on the courtyard steps about ten meters away, her legs stretched in front of her, sorting seed pods she'd collected from the edge of the terraced garden. She was a wiry child, all elbows and sharp collarbones, with a habit of biting the inside of her cheek when she concentrated that made her look older than eight. She had her father Miro's dark hair, but she carried herself with a physical self-possession that belonged to neither parent — Miro was cautious in movement, Enn nervous, and Kiri was neither. She arranged her seed pods on the stone step with the focused determination of a scientist organizing specimens, dividing them by size into precise rows.

She was humming.

Suna didn't register the melody at first. It entered as background, the hum of a child busy with a task, part of the compound's ambient soundtrack where someone was always making sound — coughing, arguing, praying, scraping pots. Then the melody turned at a specific interval. A minor third dropping to a major second in a pattern that was not common, not universal, not the kind of thing a child would pick up from exposure to ordinary songs.

Suna stopped wringing. Her hands stayed in the wet fabric but her arms went still. Water dripped off her elbows and darkened the stone at her feet.

The melody was her mother's lullaby.

Not a similar melody. Not a tune with the same general shape. The specific song — the one with the descending line that reversed itself in the third phrase, the one that looped back to its own beginning without ever resolving, the one her mother had sung in the old dialect that nobody in this region spoke because it came from a mountain valley four hundred kilometers south where the roads were bad and the villages had been emptying for thirty years. The song was built on intervals that didn't follow the scale most people learned. Suna's mother used to say the tune was older than the words, that the melody had existed before anyone thought to put a child to sleep with it.

Her mother had died when Suna was nineteen. The lullaby had died with her — not deliberately, not as an act of refusal, but because the song belonged to a voice and a set of arms and a particular darkness that was her mother's bedroom in her mother's house, and it couldn't be reproduced in any other setting without becoming wrong. Suna had not sung it for her own children. She hadn't sung it here. She was certain of this because she hadn't thought of the song in months, possibly years, and the quality of hearing it now — from the mouth of an eight-year-old Tesh girl who had grown up in a different region, a different family, a different everything — was the quality of meeting a dead person on the street. Not shock. Not horror. The flat impossibility of a presence where an absence should be.

Kiri hummed through the third phrase, hit the reversal, and continued. Her pitch was good. Not perfect — she was eight — but the intervals were right. The rhythm was right. The held note before the loop, the place where her mother used to pause and let the breath carry the silence before the tune began again, was right.

Suna's hands released the fabric. She stood there with wet arms, water running down her wrists, watching an eight-year-old girl hum her dead mother's song while sorting seed pods on the steps.

She should go over. She should ask Kiri where she'd heard the melody. She should approach the way she'd approached Mava two weeks ago — carefully, looking for the ordinary explanation before admitting the extraordinary. Someone in the compound might know the song. She might have hummed it in her sleep. The melody might be more common than she thought, a variant that had traveled farther than she knew.

She didn't move.

She stood at the cistern and listened, and the melody looped twice more before Kiri lost interest and started talking to one of the younger Kondo children who'd wandered over, and the song stopped as easily as it had started, as if it had been nothing — a thing Kiri's mouth had done while her hands were busy, a fragment of sound with no history and no weight.

Six families. The Teshes, now. Through a lullaby that belonged to a dead woman from a place none of them had ever been.

Suna picked up the next piece of laundry. Her hands were trembling in a way that had nothing to do with cold water and everything to do with the specific frequency of the third phrase, which was still in her ears like a tone that continued after the source had stopped. She wrung the fabric until the tremor was absorbed into the work, and when Deshi called from across the courtyard to ask whether she planned to wash everything or just the things that mattered, she answered with a steadiness that cost her something she could feel but couldn't name.

"All of it," she said. "I'm doing all of it."

Deshi looked at her for a beat longer than necessary, and Suna understood that her mother-in-law's eye missed very little, and that the effort of seeming fine was itself a kind of signal to anyone watching closely enough.

---

Later that afternoon she went looking for Enn Tesh. She found her in the sleeping quarters, folding blankets with a sharpness that suggested she was working something out through the medium of linen. Enn's face was lean and mobile, the face of a woman perpetually bracing for the next problem, and she wore worry so openly that it had become a kind of social feature — people checked Enn's expression the way they checked the sky.

"Your daughter," Suna said. "Kiri. Does she hum a lot?"

Enn looked up, a blanket corner pinched between her fingers. "She hums constantly. Since she was three. It drives Miro mad."

"The tune she was humming this morning, on the courtyard steps. Do you know it?"

"I didn't hear her this morning." Enn folded the corner with unnecessary precision, matching edge to edge. "Which song?"

Suna almost hummed it. The notes were in her throat, ready, and she almost let them out, and then she stopped because something in her refused to produce the melody here, for this purpose. To hum it would be to confirm it — to make it real in a way that hearing it from Kiri's mouth had not quite accomplished, because hearing could be doubted but producing could not.

"A slow one," she said. "It doesn't end — it loops back."

Enn shrugged. "She picks things up. From the radio, from other children. She's got an ear."

"The radio doesn't play this song," Suna said, and the flatness of the statement — its certainty, its weight — made Enn stop folding and look at her with the sharpened attention that everyone in the compound was beginning to pay when a sentence carried more than its surface could hold.

"What song is it?" Enn asked.

"I don't know," Suna said. The lie was small and practical. She wasn't ready to say *my dead mother's lullaby* because saying it would make it part of the pattern, and the pattern was already larger than she could manage while also managing Asha's gestures and Kael's restless sleep and Rimi's sharpening silence and Deshi's observation and the fact that her husband was not ahead of them on the road but three months gone and probably never coming back.

She carried the lie out of the sleeping quarters and into the gray-white afternoon, and she added it to the ledger of things she couldn't yet afford to say.

---

That evening, after the children were settled and the compound had reached the uneasy quiet that passed for nighttime peace, Suna found Nessa at the edge of the prayer courtyard.

Nessa was standing with Ada asleep against her chest, the baby's weight pulling her slightly forward. She had wrapped Ada in a length of faded blue-gray cloth — her own shawl, repurposed, the fringe knotted where it had begun to unravel — and in the oil-lamp light she looked like something half-formed, not quite separate from the stone behind her. She had a way of holding Ada that wasn't cradling so much as armoring — the baby positioned between herself and whatever was coming, as if a sleeping one-year-old could absorb impact.

Suna had never spent time alone with Nessa. Their interactions had been practical — the choreography of women who cooked in the same kitchen and washed at the same cistern and negotiated sleeping arrangements for children who increasingly refused to sleep where they were told. They weren't unfriendly. They simply hadn't needed each other yet.

"Can I sit with you?" Suna asked.

Nessa shifted sideways on the low courtyard wall. "Please."

Suna sat. The stone was still warm from the afternoon's thin sun, and the warmth seeped through her skirt into her thighs. The courtyard smelled of the wet herbs from the terraced garden, a green mineral scent that the rain drew out of the soil and that clung to everything for hours after the water stopped.

They sat in silence for a while. Ada made small sounds in her sleep — the grunting, liquid noises of a baby whose breathing was a performance even unconscious. Moths circled the nearest oil lamp, their shadows huge and flickering against the courtyard stones.

"Tomas," Suna said.

Nessa's hand moved to Ada's back. "You've noticed."

"I've noticed."

"Since two days ago. Maybe three." Nessa stopped. She looked at the stones between her feet, and when she spoke again her voice was lower, compressed by something heavier than volume. "He's never had any kind of speech issue. Nothing. He talked early, talked clearly — his teachers used to say he was the most articulate boy in his year. Bram used to joke he'd talk for a living, same as his father." She paused. "Same stammer included, now, I guess."

"It's the same pattern," Suna said.

"It's exactly the same. The same sounds. The same thing with his jaw." Nessa's voice had dropped further, as if the volume itself were dangerous — as if speaking softly enough might keep the fact from solidifying. "I've been watching Jin for days. I don't know why — because I noticed the hesitation, because it seemed unusual, and I watch people the way you watch people. I see you watching."

"And now Tomas does it. Not a general stammer. Not his own version."

"Jin's version. The same consonants. The same—" She touched her own jaw, a small circling gesture. "The same lock."

"Have you told Bram?"

"He's heard it. He's got an explanation for it — Tomas is imitating Jin unconsciously, the way kids pick up accents. Children are sponges, that's what he says. But he's heard it, and when Tomas does it at the table, Bram stops eating and looks at his plate like the food's going to tell him something." She shook her head. "Bram's got four explanations. Imitation, suggestion, coincidence, and confirmation bias. He cycles through them. He's on confirmation bias this week."

"Does he believe any of them?"

"He believes all of them until ten at night, and then he lies next to me and holds my hand and doesn't believe any of them." She looked at Suna. "Don't tell him I said that."

"There's another one," Suna said. "Another manifestation. That's why I came to you."

Nessa's hand stilled on Ada's back. "Who?"

"Kiri Tesh. She was humming a song this morning on the courtyard steps. A lullaby."

"Lots of children hum."

"My mother's lullaby. A song from my mother's village, in a dialect nobody up here speaks. My mother's been dead nineteen years. I haven't sung it since."

The silence that followed had weight. Not the companionable silence of earlier — this one pressed. Nessa pulled Ada fractionally closer, and the baby grunted in protest, and Nessa loosened her hold but didn't change the position of her arms.

"How can you be sure it's the same song?" she asked. "Maybe it's something close. A common tune."

"It's not common. The intervals are wrong — I mean they're right, but they're wrong for the normal scale. The melody doesn't resolve. It loops back instead of ending. My mother used to say that was the point — a lullaby shouldn't stop, because sleep should feel like going around again, not arriving somewhere. Nobody writes songs like that. It was hers."

"Could you have hummed it without knowing? In your sleep, or while you were working?"

Suna sat with the question. Outside the courtyard, the flood's sound carried up from the valley, that constant rush that had become so woven into the compound's atmosphere that it registered only during strange silences, and then with the force of something that had been listening the whole time.

"I don't think so," she said. "But I can't be certain. Which is almost worse than knowing."

"It's six families now," Nessa said quietly. "If you're counting."

"I'm counting. Asha and the Kondos. Melie and the Daos. Tomas and Jin. Kiri and the Meras. That's the Teshes too. Everyone except the Leos."

"The Leos are quiet. Cade hardly talks to anyone who isn't Wynn or Vera."

"Have you noticed anything with Pik?"

Nessa shook her head. Then she said, "Give it time," and the bitterness in her voice wasn't dramatic bitterness, wasn't the performed bitterness of a complaint — it was the flat bitterness of a woman stating a probability she didn't want to be right about.

"Nessa."

"What?"

Suna chose her words the way she chose her steps on wet stone — testing the surface before committing weight. "When I watched you this morning — watching Tomas — you looked like you already knew. Not surprised. Like you'd been waiting."

Nessa's face did what it always did, which was everything. The oil lamp caught it all — the tightening around her eyes, the way her mouth pressed shut and then opened again as if the pressure of keeping it closed was worse than what would come out.

"I've been waiting since Melie's throat swelled shut," she said. "Since the allergy. Because when I watched Bett lean over her — knowing exactly what to do, knowing it was the Dao allergy before anyone said it — I thought: it's going to find us too. It's going to reach one of mine."

"And it has."

"And it has." She adjusted Ada's weight, resettling the baby higher against her chest. Ada's face was mashed against Nessa's collarbone, one fist curled against the faded cloth. "But this isn't — what's happening to Tomas isn't the first time something's been taken from me."

Suna waited. She could feel the shape of what was coming the way she felt the air change before rain — not the fact itself, but the displacement it produced, the pressure of something about to take up room.

"We had another child," Nessa said. "Before Tomas. A boy. His name was Efe."

The name entered the air between them and stayed.

"He died at three months. A fever. It came fast — by morning he was hot, by afternoon he wouldn't nurse, and by evening his breathing had changed. Bram drove for two hours to find a clinic that was open, and it didn't matter. By the time they got a line in, he was—" She broke off. Not dramatically. The sentence simply ran into something it couldn't pass. She breathed. Started again from a different place. "We buried him in the yard behind our house. Bram dug the hole himself because he said he wouldn't let someone else do it. The hole was very small. That's what I remember most. How small the hole was."

Suna didn't touch her. The impulse was there — hand on knee, hand on arm, the practical touch she used with her children when words weren't enough — but something in Nessa's stillness told her that touch right now would crack the structure that was holding the words in their sequence, and Nessa needed the sequence to finish.

"We don't talk about him," Nessa said. "We — Bram and I decided, after. That we wouldn't carry it forward. That Tomas and Leni would grow up without the weight of a dead brother they'd never met. We thought that was the right thing." Her voice was steady and her eyes were wet and the two facts weren't in contradiction — they were the specific product of a grief that had been managed for so long it had developed its own architecture, its own load-bearing walls. "I don't know anymore if it was the right thing."

"Is that why you've been waiting?" Suna asked. "Because if the children are manifesting other families' losses—"

"What's the loss?" Nessa finished. "What's the gap? Melie's allergy connects to the Daos, and the Daos lost a son. Asha connects to the Kondos, and Mava lost a sister. So when Tomas starts speaking like Jin — what's missing? Whose absence?" She looked at Suna with the directness of someone who had decided to be fully present rather than protected. "Ours. Maybe. Or maybe Jin's — his wife."

"His wife died," Suna said, and as she said it she heard the echo of her own constructed story — *my husband went ahead* — and recognized, not for the first time, how a lie took on the texture of fact when nobody pushed against it.

"His wife died," Nessa repeated, and her tone carried something that might have been doubt, or might have been the particular attention of a woman who had learned to listen past the surface of declarations.

The compound sounds filtered through the stillness around them — someone coughing in the sleeping quarters, the creak of the kitchen overhang's timber frame, the low murmur of the custodians' radio bleeding through their closed door, an indistinct voice reporting news from somewhere that wasn't here.

"I don't know what's happening," Suna said. "I don't know if it's the shrine, or the custodians, or something the children are doing to each other without knowing it, or something that would have happened anywhere if you put this many families in a small enough space for long enough. I don't have an explanation."

"Bram cycles through his four explanations like a man shuffling cards. He deals himself a new hand every morning." The faintest edge of humor entered Nessa's voice — not amusement but the reflex of a woman who had survived difficulty partly by describing it. "I don't have four explanations. I've got zero. I've just got my son's jaw doing something it shouldn't be able to do."

Suna reached across the space between them and put her hand on Nessa's knee. The gesture was practical, not sentimental — the economy of touch she used with her children when language was beside the point. Nessa's hand came down and covered hers, and they stayed like that for a while, two women on a warm stone wall with a sleeping baby between them and the flood's sound rising from the valley below like a fact that wouldn't be argued with.

"Efe would've been eleven this year," Nessa said. "Almost the same age as Tomas. They would've fought constantly — brothers do. Bram would've had to mediate, because I cave in about three seconds. Leni would've bossed them both." She was smiling now, a small smile that had nothing behind it but loss and the particular tenderness of imagining a life that hadn't happened. "Instead, Tomas is a patient older brother to a baby sister, and he's the kindest boy I know, and now he talks like a stranger he met ten days ago."

"Do you think we should tell the custodians?" Suna asked.

"About Tomas? They'll find out. They see everything."

"About Efe."

The name again. Suna felt its weight differently the second time — not surprise but confirmation, the way a word gained solidity through repetition.

Nessa was quiet for several breaths. "About the gap, you mean. About what might be pulling Tomas toward Jin's pattern."

"Yes."

"I don't know. I don't know what they'd do with it." She turned Suna's question back. "Would you tell them about the lullaby?"

Suna thought about Fasa's steady gaze during the namings, the way the custodian held silence like a vessel and waited for it to fill. She thought about Olet's flat calm, which was either faith or indifference, and which unsettled her precisely because she couldn't tell which.

"Not yet," she said. "Not until I understand what they want from us."

"They want the map," Nessa said. "Fasa keeps saying it — the true kinship map. As if we're hiding it instead of trying to figure out what the hell is happening to our kids."

Nessa stood, lifting Ada higher against her chest, the baby's weight resettling into the familiar position. "I should go in. Bram's probably lying in the dark composing his fifth explanation."

Suna let the joke pass. She stayed on the wall after Nessa went inside, listening to the flood, feeling the stone cool beneath her as the last of the day's heat bled away. She counted. Asha's borrowed gesture: the Mera-Kondo connection. Melie's impossible allergy: the Parra-Dao connection. Tomas's new hesitation: the Okoye-Parra connection. Kiri's lullaby: the Tesh-Mera connection.

Four connections. Six families. The Leos were the only ones untouched, and Nessa had been right — give it time.

The melody came back to her, uninvited, the way her mother's voice sometimes surfaced between waking and sleep — not as memory but as presence, as if the song existed independently of anyone who had ever sung it and had simply been waiting for a mouth to carry it forward. Kiri's mouth. An eight-year-old in a different family from a different region who sorted seed pods by size and bit the inside of her cheek when she concentrated and hummed songs she had no reason to know.

Suna pressed her lips together and didn't hum.

She sat with the silence and the flood and the cooling stone, and she held the new weight of Nessa's dead son alongside the older weight of her own absent husband and the growing weight of her daughter's borrowed gestures, and she catalogued each weight separately because that was what she did, and because the alternative was to feel them all at once, which was a vertigo she couldn't afford. Not yet. Not here. Not with three children sleeping in the next room and a mother-in-law whose sharp eye missed nothing and a naming ceremony she would have to sit through again tomorrow and a lullaby that had crossed nineteen years and four hundred kilometers to arrive in the throat of a stranger's child.

The oil lamps in the main hall threw soft light across the courtyard. Inside, someone coughed. Ada cried once — sharp, sudden, a single note — and then stopped. The flood went on.


# Chapter 9

Fasa reached the kitchen before the bell stopped ringing. She had learned, across twenty naming cycles, that the work rotations told her more than the naming itself — the naming showed her what people were willing to declare, but the kitchen showed her what their hands knew.

The cooking area was a stone ledge under an overhang on the compound's western side, open to the courtyard and the smell of wet hillside beyond. The fire pit held last night's coals, and the air beneath the overhang was layered — ash at the bottom, green mineral rain above, and the persistent wood-smoke that had worked its way into everyone's hair and clothing by the end of the first week. Fasa stirred the coals and fed them dried grass until flame caught. The wet season made everything slow. Even fire needed coaxing.

Suna Mera arrived first among the assigned families, carrying the morning's water in a clay jug balanced on her hip. She set it down without greeting, but the absence of greeting was itself familiar by now — they had reached the stage of proximity where not every arrival required acknowledgment. Deshi followed a few paces behind, her posture evaluative before she'd even looked at the cooking area, the lean angle of her shoulders conveying judgment about things she hadn't yet seen.

"The coals are still damp," Deshi said. She knelt beside the fire pit and held her palm over the heat, testing. "You need harder wood underneath. Softwood burns wet."

Fasa did not point out that she had been building fires at this shrine for two decades. She moved the clay jug to the flat stone where the cutting board lived and began slicing onions from the garden. Twenty years had taught her when to assert authority and when to let a woman who needed control find it. The kitchen was Deshi's territory, even someone else's kitchen.

Hala Dao came next, with Sem behind her carrying a basket of the dried pulse stores Olet had released from the custodians' pantry that morning. Hala was broader than Deshi, sturdier in the way she occupied space, and she entered the kitchen with the specific confidence of a woman who had fed more people on less. She wore a cotton scarf over her hair that she'd rewashed so many times at the cistern it had faded from blue to the color of overcast sky. She assessed the fire, the jug, the onions, and Deshi's kneeling posture in a single sweep.

"Morning," she said to Fasa. Then, to Deshi: "The wood bin behind the prayer courtyard has harder pieces. Sem, go."

Sem went. He was eleven, dark-eyed, quiet in a way that suggested he was accustomed to running errands without needing them explained. Hala watched him go with the automatic tracking of a mother who counted her children without deciding to.

Deshi looked up from the fire. "I was about to send for the same thing."

"Then we agree," Hala said, and knelt on the opposite side of the fire pit.

Fasa listened to the silence that followed — two women who had just, in three sentences, established that neither would yield the kitchen to the other. She continued slicing onions. The knife was dull; the shrine's tools needed sharpening every few days in the humidity, and no one had done it recently. She could feel the blade dragging rather than cutting, the fibers of the onion separating raggedly under the steel. Her eyes stung. She let them.

The Teshes arrived last. Miro first, a tall man of forty-five who moved with the careful deliberation of someone aware he took up more space than he wanted to and slightly apologetic about it. His hands were large and he used them gently, setting down the flour sack with the concentration of a man placing something breakable. Enn followed, her hair wet from the morning rain, wiping her face with the back of her wrist. She had the look of a woman who had been arguing with someone before she arrived — a tightness around her mouth that she was trying to smooth away.

Gitta came behind them, slower. She carried nothing — Miro had taken her share. She stood at the edge of the overhang where the rain dripped from the stone lip and watched the others settle into the kitchen's geography before choosing her own position. She lowered herself onto the flat stone by the secondary cutting area, folded her hands in her lap, and waited to be needed. Her stillness had a different quality from Suna's stillness — Suna went still to manage crisis, but Gitta went still to disappear, and the discipline of it was practiced enough that Fasa had to remind herself to keep watching.

"We've got lentils, some rice, dried peppers, onions, and turmeric," Suna said, inventorying the stores. "And the flour. That's it from the pantry. We can pull herbs from the garden if someone wants to go down."

"Flatbread," Deshi said. It was not a suggestion. "And a dal. We can stretch the lentils if we cook them long enough."

"Dal needs more spice than turmeric," Hala said.

"Then we use what we have. It'll be bland. People will eat it."

"My father brought cumin seed in his pack," Hala said. "And black pepper. He won't mind."

Deshi paused. The pause was evaluative, and Fasa read it clearly: Deshi was deciding whether to accept help from a woman she hadn't yet ranked in her private hierarchy of domestic competence. Whatever the internal calculus produced, the answer was practical.

"Good. Cumin first, then we'll see."

Enn offered to sort the lentils. She sat beside Gitta and began picking through the dried pulse on a tin plate, discarding stones and shriveled husks with a deftness that had its own kind of authority — her fingers moved quickly, two hands working in complementary arcs, and Fasa could see she had done this thousands of times, that the sorting was not a task she performed but a motion her hands had memorized. Gitta joined her without being asked, and for a few minutes the two women worked in silence, the small clicking of lentils against tin the only sound between them.

Miro took a knife and began cutting onions alongside Fasa. His cuts were slow, methodical, each slice the same width as the last. He did not try to make conversation. Fasa appreciated this. She had noticed that Miro Tesh was a man who understood the value of doing a task without narrating it — a quality she associated with people who had spent years alongside a dominant personality and learned that silence was its own contribution. His mother was that personality, though Gitta's dominance expressed itself through quiet rather than volume, through the particular gravity of a woman whose silence made rooms rearrange around it.

Suna measured rice into the largest pot. She rinsed it three times at the cistern — Fasa heard the water running and then Suna's footsteps on the wet stone, returning — and set the pot over the fire that was now, with Sem's harder wood, burning clean and steady. The rain continued, lighter than yesterday, tapping on the overhang in the irregular rhythm that had become the compound's background music.

The flour came out in a shared bowl. Deshi took half and began mixing it with water, her hands working the dough with an assurance that bordered on aggression — each fold sharp, the heel of her palm pressing with the specific force of decades. Hala took the other half. She mixed her dough more slowly, adding water in careful increments, and when she began to fold, she used two hands where Deshi used one, turning the dough over itself in a motion so similar to Deshi's that Fasa's own hands stopped moving on the cutting board.

They were making the same bread.

The technique was not identical — Deshi folded three times to Hala's two, Deshi used less water, Hala oiled her hands and Deshi floured hers — but the fundamental motion was the same. The press-fold-turn, the way they tested thickness by pushing two fingers into the center of the round and reading the give beneath, the rotational shaping that brought the dough from lump to disc with a specific wrist motion that no one taught because it was the kind of knowledge that passed from one pair of hands to another across a kitchen, not across a page. Neither woman had noticed. They were each absorbed in the rhythm their hands knew, the unconscious mechanics of a task performed so many times it had ceased to be learned and become simply known.

Suna saw it. She was kneeling by the fire, feeding it a piece of the harder wood, and her gaze moved from Deshi's hands to Hala's and back. She caught Fasa's eye across the cutting board. Neither of them spoke.

Fasa watched for another full minute before Hala glanced sideways and went still. She stared at Deshi's hands — at the three-fold motion, at the heel-press, at the two-finger test — and something moved across her face that was not surprise, exactly, but the particular disorientation of seeing your own muscle memory in a stranger's body.

"Your fold," she said.

Deshi, feeling the attention, looked up. Then she looked at her own hands, as though seeing them for the first time in years. Then she looked at Hala's dough, at the two-fold rounds forming under Hala's oiled palms.

A silence opened between them that was different from the competitive silence of the morning. This one had texture and weight, and Fasa could feel both women reaching for an explanation that would make the sameness ordinary.

"Where'd you learn?" Deshi asked.

"My mother. And hers before that."

"Same." Deshi turned her dough over, pressed it flat. "Three folds, though. Makes the layers softer."

"Two's enough if your flour is fine-ground."

"It's not fine-ground here."

"No," Hala said. She looked at the coarse communal flour on the stone. "It isn't."

Hala added a third fold. The concession was small and undramatic — one extra turn of the wrist, one adjustment to a lifelong habit — but Fasa heard it for what it was: one woman revising her technique because another woman's point was good. Deshi did not acknowledge the concession, which was its own form of respect — the respect of assuming competence rather than praising it.

They worked side by side. The flatbreads went onto the hot stone over the fire, and the compound filled with the smell of bread cooking — yeasty, charred at the edges where the dough pressed against the stone, the particular smell that is the same everywhere bread is made over open heat. Fasa breathed it and felt something shift in her chest that she would not have named if asked. She had been conducting namings for twenty years. She had watched families resist, fight, weep, confess, and leave. She had seen the naming produce truths that healed and truths that damaged and occasionally, rarely, something that felt like genuine recognition between strangers. She had never seen it produce flatbread.

But flatbread was not the naming's work, and she knew this. The naming had pushed these families together; the kitchen rotation had put Deshi and Hala at the same fire. The sameness of their technique was not a manifestation — it was culture, the deep grammar of food preparation passed between hands rather than words, alive in the muscles of women who had never met because the knowledge itself was older than either of them.

Still. She watched them, and she allowed herself the private pleasure of watching.

Gitta's voice interrupted the pleasure. "I can make something with the lentils."

Fasa turned. Gitta had finished sorting and sat with the clean pulse in a clay bowl balanced on her thin knees. Her hands were folded over the bowl's rim. Her tone was mild — the elderly-polite register Fasa had catalogued as Gitta's public mode from the first day.

"What did you have in mind?" Suna asked.

"There is a way to prepare them," Gitta said. "You crush the lentils first — not to powder, but rough, so the edges break. Then you toast the cumin in oil until it smokes. The turmeric goes in at the same time, not after. Then the crushed lentils and water, and you cook it thick. Not thin like a dal. Thick enough that it holds the shape of the spoon when you lift it."

She spoke with the precision of a woman reciting from memory, each instruction landing in its exact sequence like beads on a string. Fasa had heard her speak this way before — at the first naming, declaring Fen as her grandson without a flicker of hesitation. The same careful placement. The same certainty.

"That sounds like something my father makes," Hala said. "A tadka, but thicker."

"It is not the same," Gitta said.

Then she stopped. Her hands tightened on the bowl's rim.

"Gitta?" Enn said from beside her.

"I have never made this." Gitta's voice had not changed in volume, but its quality had shifted — the certainty cracked, and what showed through the crack was a bewilderment so raw that Fasa felt her own hands go still on the cumin she was grinding. "I know every step. I can taste it, the way it should taste. But I have never made it."

The kitchen went quiet. Not the charged silence of the naming hall, not the silence that preceded confrontation — smaller than that, more private, the silence of six women and one man recognizing that something strange had happened in a place where strange things had been happening for days. Deshi looked at Gitta. Hala looked at Gitta. Suna's hands stopped moving on the pot she was adjusting over the fire. Miro, who had finished the onions and stood with the knife loose in his hand, turned toward his mother with an expression Fasa could not fully read — concern, yes, but something beneath it, the first fracture of a son seeing that his mother's composure might be a thing she built rather than a thing she was.

Gitta recovered. It took three seconds, and Fasa counted them because counting was what she did when something mattered. Gitta's hands loosened on the bowl. Her face smoothed, each line settling back into the arrangement Fasa had come to recognize as Gitta's public architecture.

"Perhaps I watched someone make it, years ago," she said. "These things stay in the hands."

"They do," Fasa said. She said it because the silence needed filling, and because she wanted Gitta to know that her statement had been heard and would not be challenged in this room. Not now. Not yet.

Gitta made the lentil dish. She made it with the focused precision of a woman following internal instructions that arrived from a source she could not locate, and Fasa watched her hands — the specific way she crushed the lentils between her palms rather than with a stone, the exact moment she judged the oil hot enough by holding her open hand over the pan and reading the heat, the gesture with which she stirred the thickening mixture: slow, counterclockwise, three turns and a pause. Each movement had the quality of the deeply known, and the known had the quality of the uncanny, because knowledge this specific did not come from nowhere.

Twenty years of the naming, and Fasa had never seen it touch an adult. The children were porous — everyone said so, as though children's permeability to outside influence were a natural law that required no explanation. But Gitta was seventy. Her hands had belonged to the same body for seven decades. Whatever knowledge was moving through them now had not entered through the ordinary channels of learning, watching, doing.

Fasa ground pepper into the dal and kept her face arranged. She would bring this to Olet later — not as interpretation, not yet, but as observation. Gitta Tesh knows a recipe she has never made. The manifestations may not have a lower age limit. They may not have any limit at all.

For now, the food cooked. The flatbreads piled up on a wooden board, each one slightly different — Deshi's tighter and more layered, Hala's flatter with a golden char from the slightly hotter side of the stone, the ones Suna made thicker and less even but perfectly good. The dal thickened. Gitta's lentil dish sat in a smaller vessel near the edge of the fire, its surface dark with toasted spice, filling the overhang with a fragrance that did not belong to any of the three families who had contributed ingredients. The rain had eased to a fine mist, and the kitchen felt, for the first time since they'd all arrived at the shrine, like something other than a site of obligation.

---

The custodians' bell called the compound to eat. Fasa rang it herself — she preferred the bell to her voice for routine summons, saving the particular resonance of her speaking voice for the naming, where it served a different purpose.

They gathered in the main hall, forty-three people and the custodians arranging themselves on the stone floor in a configuration that had, over twelve days, become habitual without anyone deciding it should be. The families still sat in their clusters, but the clusters had loosened. Suna's children sat at the Mera family's edge rather than its center — Rimi by the wall where the light from the open side reached, Kael close to his grandmother. Arash Kondo sat a full body-length from his father, and neither acknowledged the distance. The Leo family — Cade and Wynn and Pik and Vera — occupied their corner with the settled air of people who had made a small territory and intended to keep it: a folded blanket on the stone, a tin cup Pik used for water placed at the blanket's edge like a claim marker.

But today the food was the room's center of gravity. The pots and the flatbread board arrived carried by the women who had made them, and when the food was set down on the flat stones in the middle of the hall, the room shifted register. The smell alone did it — the yeast and char of bread cooked on stone, the earthy thickness of dal, and Gitta's lentil dish with its dark toasted-spice fragrance cutting through the smoke-saturated air of the hall like something from a kitchen none of them could place. People leaned forward. Nessa Okoye, with baby Ada balanced on one hip, reached for a flatbread with her free hand and tore it in half with a motion practiced beyond thought, passing the larger piece to Tomas without looking at him.

Kal Dao had brought the cumin and pepper he'd contributed. He was Hala and Bett's father, sixty-eight, with a narrow face and the cautious bearing of a man who had once been taller and had not yet adjusted to the loss. He wore a wool vest with a broken clasp that he kept reaching for and adjusting, pinching the fabric closed over his chest in a gesture so habitual that Fasa suspected the clasp had been broken for months or longer, and the reaching had outlived the repair. He settled beside Peyam Mera as though the seat had been reserved, and Peyam shifted to make room without either man acknowledging the arrangement as deliberate.

The children ate in their clump. This had been the pattern since the first week — the play group that formed during the day carried its own gravity into mealtimes, pulling children out of family orbits and into a loose pile on the stone floor that organized itself by affinity rather than blood. Asha sat pressed against Melie Parra's side, their legs touching. Ori Kondo sat cross-legged with her bowl on one knee. Kiri Tesh was behind Rimi Mera, working on a braid in Rimi's hair with one hand while holding a piece of flatbread in her teeth with the other, tearing off bites between sections. Pik Leo watched the braiding with the serious attention of a seven-year-old encountering a skill he hadn't considered possible. Nori Dao sat beside Asha with her chin nearly in her bowl, eating with the focused hunger of a child who did not yet understand that food could be conversation.

Sem and Raya Dao sat at the group's perimeter — thirteen and eleven, old enough to feel self-conscious about the cross-family mixing but not old enough to walk away from it entirely. Fen Tesh sat further back, near the wall, eating slowly with his shoulders rounded. Sixteen was too old for the children's clump and too young for the adults' weary accommodation, and Fen inhabited the gap with the particular discomfort of a teenager who had not yet decided where his body belonged.

Leni Okoye sat with her parents and chewed her flatbread and watched the children's group with an expression Fasa recognized — the wary attention of someone who had recently understood that observing the world closely was both a skill and a kind of distance from it.

People ate. The dal was bland, as Deshi had warned, but the flatbread was good — warm and layered, with enough char at the edges to give it texture — and Gitta's lentil dish was something else entirely. Dense, fragrant, with a complexity of flavor that drew second tastes from people who had been eating without interest for days. Jin Parra lifted his spoon and looked at it, surprised, the way a person registers an unexpected kindness from a direction they weren't watching. Bram Okoye took a second helping and said, quietly, to the room at large, that it was the best thing he'd had since they arrived.

Enn tasted her mother-in-law's dish and turned to Gitta. "Where is this recipe from?"

"I am not sure," Gitta said. She said it with the deliberateness she brought to everything, each word placed rather than spoken, and the care made the uncertainty heavier than a shrug would have been. Enn looked at her a moment longer, waiting for more, and when more didn't come she turned back to her bowl.

Mava Kondo began telling a story. The children had asked for it — or rather, Asha had climbed into Mava's proximity with the imperious confidence of a four-year-old and said the word "tell," and the other children had arranged themselves in a ragged semicircle that constituted a request no one with a story in them could refuse. Mava settled her back against the wall, adjusted her shawl over her shoulders, and spoke.

The story was old. Fasa could hear its age in the rhythm — the repetitions that belonged to oral tradition rather than written composition, the formulaic openings that marked a tale passed through generations of telling. A family of birds lived on one side of a flooding river. Another family of birds lived on the other side. A storm tore the nests of both families loose from their trees and carried them downstream, tumbling and breaking apart, until the tangled remains of both nests caught in the branches of a single tree at the river's bend. The birds could not separate what was left — the twigs and grass and feathers had woven together too tightly — and so they rebuilt around each other, and the new nest was larger and stranger than either family's had been, and it held twice as many eggs.

Mava told it with the unhurried cadence of a woman who had told this story many times, to many children, in many rooms. Her voice dropped on the repetitions and rose on the moments of change. Her dialect surfaced in the telling — old-language phrasings that Fasa recognized but could not place exactly, the vowels rounder and the consonants softer than the common speech they all shared, as though the story itself demanded a different mouth. The children listened with the particular stillness that meant a story was working on them. Even Fen, from his position by the wall, had stopped eating.

The adults listened too, though most of them pretended they were doing something else. Bram's head was tilted, his glasses catching the light from the open side of the hall. Nessa had stopped bouncing Ada. Suna was looking at her own hands on her bowl as though the story were playing on a screen only she could see. Venn Kondo sat with his bowl balanced on his knees and his eyes on his aunt, and his face for once was not the patriarch's wall — it was a man hearing a story he had heard as a child, told in a voice that had always told it, and the sound of that voice had reached past the defenses he maintained and found the part of him that remembered being small enough to sit in the circle and believe that a nest could grow large enough to hold everyone.

Fasa felt the story land in the room. Not dramatically — no one wept, no one drew the obvious parallel to their own situation — but the quality of attention shifted, the way air shifts before weather changes. Forty-three people and the custodians in a stone hall on a hilltop above a flood, eating flatbread made by two women who used the same fold, listening to an old woman tell a story about nests tangling together in a storm. The rain had stopped while Mava was speaking. Fasa had not noticed when.

Through the hall's open side, she could see the courtyard, and on the courtyard steps she could see Peyam and Kal. They had taken their bowls outside and eaten there, and now Peyam's pipe was out — his personal possession, the clay pipe with a bowl darkened by years of use that he kept wrapped in cloth in his pack. Kal sat beside him, his broken-clasped vest open in the humidity, and they passed the pipe between them with the wordless choreography of two men who had learned each other's rhythm without deciding to. Smoke rose from the pipe in a thin line that the mist smeared before it reached the overhang.

They were not talking about the manifestations or the naming or the flood. Fasa could hear fragments of their conversation through the open side of the hall — roads, weather, the quality of this year's monsoon compared to past years. Practical talk, unhurried, the kind of exchange that had no destination and required no conclusion.

"Rains were heavier last year in our district," Kal said. His voice was lower than Peyam's, rougher, with a rasp that might have been tobacco or might have been the grain of how he'd always spoken. "And the year before. The whole lowland flooded out."

"Always heavier in the east."

"Depends where you draw the line for east."

Peyam took the pipe back and considered this. "East is east," he said. "A man knows it when he looks at it."

"That's what a western man would say."

Peyam's silence was amused. The pipe passed. The rain had stopped and the light had gone silver where the clouds thinned over the valley, and the two old men sat in the silver light with smoke between them and nothing urgent to say.

Fasa watched them and thought: this is what I have given twenty years for. Not the manifestations — those were the shrine's mystery, and she had learned to hold mystery without needing to resolve it, the way she held water in her cupped hands and accepted that some would escape between her fingers. Not the naming's forced declarations — those were the pressure, the mechanism that cracked people open, and she believed in them the way she believed in the necessity of questions even when the answers caused pain. What she had given her life for was this: the possibility that the pressure might sometimes produce something unforced. That the coercion — and it was coercion, she was not naive about the structure she served — might create conditions in which something genuine could take root. That the naming, for all its invasiveness, might leave behind a residue of the real.

She sat with her empty bowl on her knees and watched the hall. Suna was helping Deshi collect the flatbread board, and their hands met over the wood and neither pulled away — a small negotiation of proximity that would have been unthinkable on day one. Hala was talking to Bett in the Dao family's corner, and Bett's face was less guarded than Fasa had seen it, the careful economy of her expression softened by something as ordinary as a good meal and a few minutes of peace. Arash Kondo had moved closer to the children's group and was listening to the last threads of Mava's story with an expression that made him look younger than his twenty-seven years, the tension he carried between his father's expectations and his own unnamed wants temporarily released. Wynn Leo, quiet Wynn who rarely spoke in group settings, had begun humming while washing one of the communal pots at the cistern's edge — a low, tuneless sound, domestic and unselfconscious, the sound of a man performing a task without resentment. Vera Leo sat beside Cade with her thick fingers laced over the folded blanket they'd claimed as their territory, and her posture was the posture of a woman who had decided, for this afternoon at least, that being in this place with these people was not entirely a punishment.

The children were still in their clump. Asha had fallen asleep against Melie, her mouth slightly open, one hand curled in the fabric of Melie's shirt. Jin saw this from across the hall and did not get up to retrieve her. He sat with his hands around his empty bowl, watching his daughter sleep against another man's child in a pile of children from five different families, and he let it be. Fasa noticed that. She noticed that his face, which had carried the rigid blankness of a man holding himself together through refusal to feel, had eased into something more like exhaustion — which was, in its own way, a form of surrender, because exhaustion meant he had stopped clenching and let the weight settle where it would.

The afternoon stretched. The mist thickened and thinned outside the hall's open side, and the valley below remained drowned — brown water where the road should have been, the lower switchbacks submerged, the sound of the flood a constant rush beneath the silence of the hilltop. But the silence itself felt different today. It was not the fraught silence of families avoiding each other's gaze, or the tense silence that preceded a naming. It was the silence of people at rest after a meal, bodies warm and full, the communal quiet that settles when forty-three people have eaten the same bread and tasted the same food and listened to the same story about birds whose nests tangled in a flood.

Fasa sat with all of it. The warmth, the fullness, the ordinary noise of a child sleeping and an old man humming and two elders sharing smoke on the steps. The compound functioning, for a few hours, as something that resembled a community rather than a collection of people held in place by rising water and institutional authority.

She knew what tomorrow held. The naming would resume. The questions she had put to Jin — the questions that had cracked his story about his wife in front of forty-two witnesses three days ago, that had sent him walking out of the circle while Melie called after him — still hung in the compound's air. She would have to decide whether to press or to let the fracture settle, and either choice carried consequences she could feel but not yet calculate. Venn's resistance had not softened; if anything, the manifestations involving his family — Asha's gesture, Ori's terror at pooling water — had hardened him further, and Fasa knew that rigid things broke rather than bent when enough force was applied. And Gitta. Gitta's hands making food she'd never made, Gitta's face when she realized what she was doing, the three-second recovery that was itself a kind of confession — Fasa had filed this alongside every other small rupture, and the file was getting heavy.

But not now. Now the rain had paused and the courtyard was silver and Peyam was passing his pipe to Kal and Mava's story still hung in the hall's air and the children slept in a pile that did not respect the boundaries their parents maintained. Gitta's lentil dish — the dish she had never learned, the dish she knew from a source she could not name — sat in its pot with a wooden spoon resting against the rim, and someone would eat the rest at dinner, and the pot would be washed at the cistern and returned to its shelf and the day would turn toward evening. The warmth was real. Fasa had spent enough of her life reading people to know the difference between performed ease and genuine rest, and what she saw in this hall was rest — imperfect, temporary, achieved within a structure none of them had chosen and most of them resented — but rest.

She rinsed her bowl at the cistern and stood for a moment with wet hands in the returning mist, watching the clouds close over the thin place where the light had come through. The stone was cool under her feet. Water dripped from the overhang onto her wrist, and she let it run down to her elbow without wiping it away. The valley below was still drowned. The road was still gone. And behind her, in the hall she had served for twenty years, forty-three people who had not chosen each other sat together in the quiet that followed a meal, and some of them — not all, but enough — had let the quiet mean something.

It would not hold. The naming always asked more than people wanted to give, and the asking had not finished. But Fasa stood at the cistern with water on her hands and allowed herself, for the space of a few breaths, to believe that what she had seen today was not the naming's product or the shrine's design but something the families had made themselves — out of flour and oil and fire, out of an old woman's story and an old man's pipe, out of the simple human preference for warmth over cold and company over solitude. She allowed herself this, and then she dried her hands on her clothing and went back inside to begin preparing for tomorrow.


# Chapter 10

The bell found Jin at the cistern. He was filling the last of the kitchen jugs, the water cold against his wrists where it splashed over the stone lip, and for a count of three he let the sound mean nothing — just another instruction, another hour marked — before the weight of it settled. The naming bell. Different from the meal bell only in Fasa's way of striking it: three measured beats, a pause, three more.

He lifted the jug by both handles and walked it to the kitchen overhang, where Enn Tesh was banking the afternoon fire. She looked up at him and then past him, toward the main hall, and he recognized the expression on her face — the weariness of a woman who had been named enough times to resent the ritual without being able to refuse it. She had flour on her forearms from the evening dough, and she folded them against her chest as if she could keep the kitchen's warmth from leaking out while she was gone.

"Leave it there," she said, meaning the jug. "I'll pour when we're done."

Jin set it on the stone shelf and wiped his hands on his trousers. The water left dark marks on his thighs. He could hear the families moving now, the compound contracting around the bell's summons: Venn's voice directing the Kondos, a child's sandal scuffing flagstone, Nessa Okoye pulling Tomas by the hand while she settled baby Ada against her shoulder with the other arm. Thirteen days of this, and the movements had become practiced. People found their places without being told.

Melie was already inside. He could see her from the hall's entrance — cross-legged in the children's cluster near the left wall, her shoulder touching Kiri Tesh's, both of them still holding the chalked stones they'd been playing with in the courtyard. She hadn't noticed him yet. She was whispering something to Kiri, her mouth close to the older girl's ear, and Kiri was smiling.

She was so easy here. That was the thing Jin couldn't stop noticing and couldn't stop fearing. Melie, who had barely spoken for the first two weeks after her mother left, who had stopped asking for stories at bedtime, who had become careful with Jin in a way no six-year-old should be careful with her father — that Melie had dissolved somewhere during the first week at the shrine and been replaced by this one, this child who laughed at Pik's jokes and climbed into Suna Mera's lap without asking and had once, three days ago, called Bett Dao by her first name without any honorific, as though Bett were family.

Jin took his place at the back right of the hall, where the Parras always stood. Not a family section — there were only two of them, so they occupied the narrow gap between the Teshes and the stone wall, a margin that suited Jin's preference for being peripheral. The stone was cool through his shirt where he leaned against it. The oil lamps had been lit despite the daylight still showing through the hall's open side, and the combined glow gave everyone's skin a warm cast that felt dishonest, as if the light were trying to make this kinder than it was.

Fasa entered from the custodians' walkway. She wore the same cotton shawl she always wore for the naming — gray, undecorated, wrapped over her left shoulder in a way that gave her silhouette a formal asymmetry. She stood at the center of the hall's curved front wall, where the stone floor was slightly raised, and waited. She always waited. She didn't call for silence; she just stopped, and the room quieted around her the way water settles after a stone drops through it.

Olet was not present. He attended some namings and not others, and Jin had stopped trying to predict the pattern. Today there were four custodians in the hall — Fasa and three assistants, one of whom Jin knew only as the young man who refilled the oil lamps every evening and had a habit of humming under his breath while he worked, a thin tune that sounded like a prayer but might have been a pop song from before the crisis. He stood behind Fasa now with his hands clasped, his humming stilled, his face settled into the deliberate blankness all the custodians wore during the naming.

"The naming," Fasa said. Her voice carried without effort. "We are here to name."

The formula. Jin knew it now. First the formula, then each family in turn, then questions if Fasa had them, then whatever came after. The first naming had taken less than an hour. The subsequent ones had stretched longer as the manifestations accumulated and the questions grew pointed.

Fasa began with the Kondos, as she usually did — the largest family went first, either by custom or because Venn's impatience was easiest to manage when he was allowed to go early. Venn stood. He was wearing the same dark button-up he'd worn to every naming, the collar soft from washing in the cistern, and he declared the twelve Kondos without consulting anyone, each name a point of certainty driven into the room: his wife Tila, his son Arash, his sister Habel and Habel's husband Olu, their daughter Ori, aunt Mava, and the rest. A roll call that Venn performed as if repetition could make the family more solid, as if saying the names louder would stop whatever was loosening them from the inside.

Fasa asked Venn a single question about Ori's fear of water, whether it had continued. Venn said it had not. Habel, standing behind him, looked at the floor. Her fingers moved against her own wrist — rubbing, as if checking a pulse that wasn't there.

The Okoyes went next. Bram stood and spoke clearly, the way he always did, his teacher's diction turning the naming into something that sounded almost like a lesson. Five people. He named Nessa, Leni, Tomas, Ada. Fasa had no questions. Leni sat behind her parents with her arms crossed and a face that Jin would have read as boredom in any other context but that he had learned, over thirteen days, was her way of watching everything while pretending to watch nothing.

The Meras. Suna stood. She was holding Asha, who had her face buried in Suna's neck, and she shifted the child's weight to her left hip before speaking. She declared the six Meras — herself, Deshi, Peyam, Rimi, Kael, Asha. No mention of a husband. She never mentioned a husband. Jin noticed this the way you notice the shape of your own wound in someone else's body — the absence in her declaration was the same shape as the absence in his.

Deshi sat behind Suna with her hands folded in her lap, her back very straight, her face composed into the expression she reserved for namings: watchful, assessing, yielding nothing. Peyam had his eyes half-closed. He might have been praying. He might have been asleep. Jin had never been certain which.

The Teshes, the Daos, the Leos — each family standing and declaring and sitting down again, the ritual proceeding with the steady weight of a wheel turning. Gitta Tesh spoke slowly, as she always did, each word arriving with a pause before and after it, and Jin noticed that her hands were steady today. They had not been steady at the last naming. Hala Dao spoke for the Daos, her voice carrying the practiced efficiency of a woman who had assumed responsibility for more people than she'd planned on, and Bett sat beside her without adding anything, her eyes on some middle distance Jin recognized — the focal length of someone who had decided, in advance, exactly how much of herself she would bring to this room. Cade Leo declared the Leos simply — Cade, Wynn, Pik, Vera — and his mother Vera sat behind him with her hand on Pik's back, the child leaning into her with the unselfconscious trust of a seven-year-old who had not yet learned that trust was something people questioned.

Then Fasa looked at Jin.

The transition was nothing — just her gaze shifting — but his body knew before he did. His jaw locked. The muscles along the left side of his face tightened the way they always did before a hard consonant, that fraction of a second where his mouth refused to open, and he had to wait it out, push past it, the word coming through like a door forced against a swollen frame.

"Jin Parra," Fasa said. "You and Melie."

He stood away from the wall. His hands went to his sides. He was aware of the stone behind him, the lip of it pressing against his lower back where he'd been leaning, and he missed it — the wall's support — as soon as he stepped forward.

"Two," he said. "Jin and Melie Parra. Father and daughter."

He waited for the formula to release him, the way it had before — two people, the smallest family, nothing to interrogate because there was nothing to see. But Fasa didn't nod. She didn't look away. She stood very still at the center of the hall, her hands clasped at her waist in the pose she used when the naming was about to ask more than someone wanted to give.

"You declared, at the first naming, that your wife was deceased."

It wasn't a question. Jin felt the words land on his skin like the cistern water — cold, specific, from a source he couldn't stop. He heard a small sound from the children's cluster — Melie shifting, or Kiri, or one of the other children — and the sound drove a spike of focus through his skull.

"Yes," he said.

"The naming has noted a connection between Melie and the Dao family." Fasa's voice was steady, measured, the cadence of someone who had been asking hard questions for twenty years and had learned to remove urgency from her delivery so that the questions themselves could carry the weight. "A response in Melie's body that belongs to the Dao line, not to yours. This is connected to your declaration. Do you understand?"

"I understand what you're saying."

"Then I would like to ask about Melie's mother. When did she die?"

The room was very quiet. Jin could hear the flood below the shrine — that constant rush, deeper today because of last night's rain, a sound he could usually fold into the background but that was now pressing against the walls of the hall like something waiting to get in.

He'd prepared for this. Not carefully — he didn't have the kind of mind that prepared carefully, that built narrative architecture and checked it for consistency — but in the rough way of a man who knows he'll be asked and has a sentence ready. He had a sentence.

"Last year," he said. "In the winter."

"Last winter." Fasa's voice was patient. "Which month?"

He couldn't name a month. The fiction of it — that he had a wife who died in a named month of a named year — buckled under the need to say something true enough to pass. "Late winter," he said. "I don't — the cold months. Before the planting."

"Before the planting. What illness took her?"

He had said illness to someone — he couldn't remember who, maybe Bett, maybe one of the other mothers who had asked about Melie's mama in the way women asked about absent mothers, gently, assuming the worst. He had said illness and illness was what he had.

"A fever," he said. "She got sick and she didn't get better."

"A fever." Fasa repeated it without inflection. "She was young. Was she treated?"

"There wasn't — we don't have—" He stopped. The jaw-lock again, harder this time. He could feel the room's attention on him like heat from the oil lamps magnified, the weight of forty-odd people watching a man stand in lamplight and speak about his dead wife, and he hated them for watching and hated himself for standing here and hated the word *fever* for being so thin, so obviously the first word a liar grabs when he needs a cause of death.

"There wasn't a doctor," he said. "Not where we lived."

Fasa paused. The pause was its own instrument — a space she opened and then stood in, letting the room's silence do work that her voice did not. When she spoke again her tone had not changed, but the question carried something new beneath it, a pressure Jin recognized as the moment when a ritual stopped being form and became excavation.

"Where is she buried, Jin?"

"At home. In our village."

"Your home village." Fasa's hands had not moved from her waist. Her face had not moved from its ceremonial composure. But her voice leaned into the next words with the slight emphasis of someone placing a stone on a scale. "And you are traveling away from your home village. With Melie. During the crisis."

She didn't finish it as a question. She didn't need to. The direction was the crack and everyone in the room could see it: a man whose wife was buried at home, traveling away from home, away from the grave, with a six-year-old, during a political crisis that made the roads dangerous and the journey senseless unless there was something at the other end more powerful than what he'd left behind.

Jin's hands were shaking. He put them behind his back, pressing his knuckles against his spine, and the posture looked like a man trying to appear controlled but was really a man trying to hold on to the bones in his own body.

"I'm taking Melie to her mother's family," he said.

"Her mother's family. They're expecting you?"

"Yes."

"They know about their daughter's death?"

"Yes."

"When did you tell them? By letter? By messenger?"

He hadn't told anyone anything because there was no death to report. The fiction was now three layers deep — the death, the illness, the notification of family — and each layer was thinner than the last, and Fasa was standing there with her liturgist's patience and her ritual attention and she was going to keep asking until the whole structure fell through.

"I sent word," Jin said. His voice sounded wrong to him — flat, detached, as if it were coming from the wall behind him rather than from his own chest.

"When?"

"After she — after." He swallowed. "A few months."

"A few months after the death." Fasa's sentences had the cadence of repetition, of confirmation, but they were not confirmation — they were mirrors held up to his words so that the room could see what he had actually said. "Before the planting, or after?"

He couldn't remember what he'd said. Late winter, before the planting — that was the death. So sending word a few months later would be spring, early summer. But he'd told Bett — he was almost certain he'd told Bett, in the days after the allergic reaction, when she was asking what Melie had eaten at home — that they'd left home recently, weeks ago, not months. If the death was late winter and he'd left home weeks ago, then he'd spent most of a year at home after his wife died before leaving. Which was plausible. But the letter would have arrived at her family months before Jin himself set out, and they would have had time to send something back — a reply, a message, anything — and Jin had nothing, no letter, no response, because none of it had happened.

"I don't remember the exact—" He stopped. The jaw-lock seized him worse than it had in months, the consonant jammed behind his teeth like something physical, and the silence in the hall thickened around his failure to produce the word, and he heard — he was certain he heard — Melie say something from the children's cluster, not a word, just a breath, the small inhalation of a six-year-old whose attention had shifted from the game in front of her to her father standing rigid in the lamplight.

"Is there family on her side?" Fasa asked. "Parents, siblings, anyone who might help us understand the connection between—"

"I've answered your questions."

The words came out louder than he intended. Not a shout — Jin didn't shout — but louder, the volume of a man whose control was a physical object he had been gripping and the grip was failing.

"The naming asks—" Fasa began.

"I know what the naming asks." His hands came out from behind his back. They were still shaking. He looked at them and then at Fasa and then at the room — the ring of families, the oil lamps, the stone floor with its worn places where generations of travelers had stood and been named and been found acceptable or insufficient — and he understood that he was trapped in the exact space between his two impossibilities. He could not tell the truth. He could not sustain the lie. There was nothing else available to him except leaving.

"I said she died," he said. "She died. That's what happened."

The room was absolutely still. Jin could feel each person in it — their heat, their attention, the weight of their watching — and he could feel, behind all of them, the flood's roar from the valley below, and the oil lamps' tiny sounds, the wicks burning, the soft hiss of flame against liquid. Somewhere to his left, in the Okoye section, Leni was watching him with her arms no longer crossed. Somewhere behind Fasa, the young custodian who hummed had gone very still. Bett Dao, in the Dao section, had her eyes on him — steady, not pitying, not hostile, just present — and Jin couldn't bear that either, the gaze of the woman who had saved his daughter, watching him fail to speak about the woman who had walked away from her.

He could tell the truth. He could say it: *she left. She walked out of our house on a Tuesday morning while Melie was eating breakfast and she didn't come back. She's alive. She's somewhere. I told Melie heaven because what word do you give a six-year-old for a mother who chose to leave?*

But Melie was sitting fifteen feet away with her chalked stones in her lap, and if Jin said it here, said it in front of all these people and a six-year-old who believed her mother was with the angels, it would be worse than the lie. The lie at least gave Melie a mother she could love without complication. The truth would give her a mother who didn't want her.

"I won't answer any more questions," Jin said.

Fasa looked at him. Her face held its composure, but something moved behind it — a recognition, maybe, that she was standing at the edge of something she couldn't push past without breaking it. Her mouth opened and then closed. For a moment she looked less like a custodian conducting a rite and more like a woman who had just understood, too late, the cost of what she'd been pressing against.

"The naming notes your—"

"I'm done."

Jin stepped out of his place by the wall and walked toward the hall's open side. The space between the families' sections was narrow — he passed the Meras, passed Suna's face turned toward him with an expression he wouldn't let himself read, passed Deshi's sharp eyes and Peyam's closed ones — and then the air changed as he reached the open side of the hall, where the stone floor met the sky and the valley fell away below and the flood was visible, brown and endless, covering the roads.

Behind him, Melie's voice.

"Papa?"

Small. Not frightened — not yet — but confused, the confusion of a child whose father had just done something outside the architecture of her world. He was supposed to stand in his place and say the words and sit down. He was not supposed to walk away.

He didn't stop. He heard Suna say something low — to Melie, he thought, or to the room, it didn't matter — and then the sound of the hall receded as he stepped onto the courtyard flagstones and kept walking, past the kitchen overhang where Enn's fire was still smoking, past the cistern where he'd filled the water jugs an hour ago, past the terraced garden with its herbs going leggy in the monsoon heat, to the outer wall of the compound where the stone was rough-cut and body-warm from the afternoon sun and there was nothing beyond it but the hill's slope and the flood below and the gray-green sky.

***

He sat down with his back against the wall. The stone pressed against his spine through his shirt. He put his hands on his knees and looked at them — his hands, the dark water-marks still on his trousers from the cistern — and he tried to think about anything other than Melie's voice saying his name and the sound of it landing in the silence he'd made by leaving.

*Papa?*

He pressed his palms flat against his kneecaps. The fabric was damp. The air smelled like rain and wood smoke and the mineral tang of wet stone, and from inside the hall he could hear the naming continue — Fasa's voice, muted by distance, asking someone else's family their questions, the ritual proceeding as if his refusal were a stone thrown into a river, a disruption the current absorbed without changing course.

The shame was easier to feel out here, away from the walls, where there was nothing between him and the valley but the hill's slope and the darkening air. Inside, the shame had been public — witnessed, measured, turned over by forty-odd pairs of eyes. Out here it was just his. It sat in his chest like the water in the cistern — still, cold, with a weight he could feel pressing against his ribs when he breathed.

He thought about the first time he'd said it. Not to the naming — before that, months before. To the woman at the transit office where he'd applied for the travel documents. *Marital status.* He'd looked at the form and the word *married* was there and the word *widowed* was there and neither was true, but *widowed* was closer, because she was gone, because she might as well be dead, because *abandoned* was not a category the form offered and *separated* would have required an address for her and he had no address because she had given him nothing. Not a note, not a reason, not even the courtesy of a fight she could have won.

So he'd checked *widowed.* And the woman at the transit office hadn't looked up, hadn't asked, had stamped his papers and handed them back. That was the first lie. Every one since had been a child of it — quick, easy, each one costing less than the last until the cost had become invisible, until *she died* was as natural in his mouth as *good morning* or *please* or *thank you for letting us stay.*

The lie had worked because nobody asked follow-up questions. A dead wife was a closed door. People looked at you with a softness that was its own kind of shield, and they didn't push, didn't probe, didn't need the details of a grief they could imagine well enough on their own. The lie relied on decency — on the fact that most people would rather assume the worst than demand proof of it — and until Fasa, until this place with its naming and its ritual patience, that decency had held.

Now it hadn't.

He thought about the morning she left. He thought about it most days, but here, with the stone warm against his back and the compound's noise distant and the flood rushing below, the memory had the quality of something being pressed through a sieve — only the hardest parts getting through.

The shoes. She had put on her shoes. That was the part that kept him, because putting on shoes was nothing, was ordinary, was what you did before you walked to the market or the neighbor's or the well. She had been wearing the brown sandals with the strap that needed fixing, and she had bent to buckle them at the door, and Melie had been at the table eating rice with her fingers because Jin hadn't washed the spoons yet, and Jin had been standing at the stove watching the kettle and he hadn't said anything because there was nothing to say about a woman putting on her shoes.

She'd straightened up. She'd looked at Melie — not at Jin, at Melie — and the look was the thing Jin couldn't parse, even now, even after months of parsing. It wasn't guilt. It wasn't grief. It was something closer to a decision that had already been made, a door that was already closed, and the woman on the other side of it was just going through the physical motions of walking out. She looked at Melie the way you look at something you've already lost.

She said, "I'll be back."

She wasn't.

Melie had finished her rice and asked where mama was and Jin had said *out* and then *she went to the market* and then, later, when the market answer stopped working, *she had to go somewhere far away* and then, later still, when Melie's questions grew more specific and her eyes grew more careful, *heaven, baby, mama went to heaven, she's watching you from there.*

Heaven was a better country than the truth. In heaven, mama loved you. In heaven, mama hadn't chosen the brown sandals with the broken strap and the door and the road over the girl at the table with rice on her fingers.

The naming bell rang — three beats, the closing signal — and Jin let his head fall back against the wall and closed his eyes.

He didn't know how long he sat there. The light shifted.
